### [1] [Contextual Earnings-22: A Speech Recognition Benchmark with Custom Vocabulary in the Wild](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07354)
*Berkin Durmus,Chen Cen,Eduardo Pacheco,Arda Okan,Atila Orhon*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Contextual Earnings-22, a benchmark dataset that evaluates how well speech-to-text systems handle rare, context-specific vocabulary, and shows that scaling contextual methods like keyword prompting and boosting yields significant accuracy gains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Academic speech-to-text benchmarks suggest progress has plateaued, but real-world industrial use in high-stakes domains still shows room for improvement. The authors hypothesize this discrepancy comes from the lack of context-specific, custom vocabulary in academic tests, even though such terms critically affect usability. There is also no standardized benchmark to evaluate contextual ASR, limiting systematic research and comparison.

Method: The authors construct Contextual Earnings-22 by augmenting the existing Earnings-22 dataset with realistic custom vocabulary and associated contexts that reflect real-world usage. They design experimental protocols and then implement six strong baseline systems based on two main contextualization paradigms: keyword prompting (providing systems with relevant terms beforehand) and keyword boosting (increasing the likelihood of recognizing specified terms during decoding). They scale these methods from small proof-of-concept setups to large-scale systems to assess performance trends.

Result: Both keyword prompting and keyword boosting baselines show substantial accuracy improvements on contextual vocabulary compared with non-contextual baselines. When scaled up to larger systems, the two approaches achieve comparable performance and reveal that there is still meaningful headroom in contextual ASR accuracy that is not visible on traditional academic benchmarks.

Conclusion: Contextual Earnings-22 serves as a standardized, realistic benchmark for evaluating context-aware speech-to-text, focusing on rare and custom vocabulary. The results demonstrate that modern contextualization methods, when properly scaled, can significantly enhance recognition accuracy, explaining why industrial systems appear to outperform what academic benchmarks suggest and providing a foundation for further progress in contextual ASR.

Abstract: The accuracy frontier of speech-to-text systems has plateaued on academic benchmarks.1 In contrast, industrial benchmarks and adoption in high-stakes domains suggest otherwise. We hypothesize that the primary difference between the two is contextual conditioning: Academic benchmarks are dominated by frequently encountered general vocabulary that is relatively easy to recognize compared with rare and context-defined custom vocabulary that has disproportionate impact on the usability of speech transcripts. Despite progress on contextual speech-to-text, there is no standardized benchmark. We introduce Contextual Earnings-22, an open dataset built upon Earnings-22, with realistic custom vocabulary contexts to foster research and reveal latent progress. We set six strong baselines for two dominant approaches: keyword prompting and keyword boosting. Experiments show both reach comparable and significantly improved accuracy when scaled from proof-of-concept to large-scale systems.

</details>


### [2] [Hybrid CNN-Transformer Architecture for Arabic Speech Emotion Recognition](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07357)
*Youcef Soufiane Gheffari,Oussama Mustapha Benouddane,Samiya Silarbi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a hybrid CNN-Transformer model for Arabic Speech Emotion Recognition (SER), achieving very high accuracy on the EYASE Egyptian Arabic corpus and showing that attention-based methods work well for low-resource languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most speech emotion recognition research focuses on English, German, and a few other languages. Arabic is underexplored mainly due to a lack of annotated emotional speech datasets, creating a gap for human-centered applications in the Arabic-speaking world. The paper aims to address this gap with a strong model tailored to Arabic SER.

Method: The authors design a hybrid deep learning architecture that takes Mel-spectrograms of Arabic speech as input. Convolutional neural network (CNN) layers are used first to learn local, discriminative spectral features. These extracted features are then fed into Transformer encoder layers, which use self-attention to capture long-range temporal dependencies in the speech signal. The system is trained and evaluated on the EYASE (Egyptian Arabic speech emotion) corpus.

Result: On the EYASE Egyptian Arabic speech emotion corpus, the proposed system achieves 97.8% classification accuracy and a macro F1-score of 0.98, indicating very strong and balanced performance across emotion classes.

Conclusion: Combining CNN-based spectral feature extraction with Transformer-based temporal modeling is highly effective for Arabic speech emotion recognition, even in a low-resource setting. The results suggest that Transformer-based approaches are promising for SER in Arabic and potentially other low-resource languages.

Abstract: Recognizing emotions from speech using machine learning has become an active research area due to its importance in building human-centered applications. However, while many studies have been conducted in English, German, and other European and Asian languages, research in Arabic remains scarce because of the limited availability of annotated datasets. In this paper, we present an Arabic Speech Emotion Recognition (SER) system based on a hybrid CNN-Transformer architecture. The model leverages convolutional layers to extract discriminative spectral features from Mel-spectrogram inputs and Transformer encoders to capture long-range temporal dependencies in speech. Experiments were conducted on the EYASE (Egyptian Arabic speech emotion) corpus, and the proposed model achieved 97.8% accuracy and a macro F1-score of 0.98. These results demonstrate the effectiveness of combining convolutional feature extraction with attention-based modeling for Arabic SER and highlight the potential of Transformer-based approaches in low-resource languages.

</details>


### [3] [Cross-Tokenizer LLM Distillation through a Byte-Level Interface](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07466)
*Avyav Kumar Singh,Yen-Chen Wu,Alexandru Cioba,Alberto Bernacchia,Davide Buffelli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Byte-Level Distillation (BLD), a simple baseline for cross-tokenizer distillation that aligns teacher and student via byte-level outputs instead of complex vocabulary-matching heuristics, achieving competitive or superior performance to more sophisticated methods but showing CTD remains unsolved.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Cross-tokenizer distillation is hard because teacher and student language models often use different tokenizers, making their vocabularies misaligned. Existing methods need heuristic, complex vocabulary-matching strategies, which are cumbersome and brittle. The paper aims to find a simple, robust, and generally applicable way to transfer knowledge across models with different tokenizers.

Method: Introduce Byte-Level Distillation (BLD): (1) represent both teacher and student outputs at the shared byte level; (2) convert the teacher’s token-level output distribution into byte-level probabilities; (3) augment the student model with a lightweight byte-level decoder head; and (4) perform distillation by matching the student’s byte-level predictions to the teacher’s byte-level distribution, enabling cross-tokenizer knowledge transfer without explicit vocabulary alignment.

Result: Across multiple distillation tasks and model sizes from 1B to 8B parameters, BLD achieves performance comparable to or better than more elaborate cross-tokenizer distillation methods. On several benchmarks, it surpasses these sophisticated baselines, demonstrating that a simple byte-level interface can be surprisingly effective for CTD, though gains are not uniformly consistent across all tasks.

Conclusion: The byte level serves as a natural shared interface for language models with different tokenizers, enabling effective cross-tokenizer distillation using a simple architecture extension and training objective. However, the variability in improvements across benchmarks indicates that CTD is not fully solved; BLD is a strong baseline but further research is required to achieve stable, universal gains.

Abstract: Cross-tokenizer distillation (CTD), the transfer of knowledge from a teacher to a student language model when the two use different tokenizers, remains a largely unsolved problem. Existing approaches rely on heuristic strategies to align mismatched vocabularies, introducing considerable complexity. In this paper, we propose a simple but effective baseline called Byte-Level Distillation (BLD) which enables CTD by operating at a common interface across tokenizers: the byte level. In more detail, we convert the teacher's output distribution to byte-level probabilities, attach a lightweight byte-level decoder head to the student, and distill through this shared byte-level interface. Despite its simplicity, BLD performs competitively with--and on several benchmarks surpasses--significantly more sophisticated CTD methods, across a range of distillation tasks with models from 1B to 8B parameters. Our results suggest that the byte level is a natural common ground for cross-tokenizer knowledge transfer, while also highlighting that consistent improvements across all tasks and benchmarks remain elusive, underscoring that CTD is still an open problem.

</details>


### [4] [Lexical Tone is Hard to Quantize: Probing Discrete Speech Units in Mandarin and Yorùbá](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07467)
*Opeyemi Osakuade,Simon King*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates discrete speech units (DSUs) derived from self-supervised learning for encoding suprasegmental information (like lexical tone) and finds that standard quantisation methods underrepresent tone, proposing a residual clustering approach to better capture it.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: DSUs from SSL models are widely used in speech and multimodal tasks and are assumed to capture both segmental and suprasegmental information. However, for tone languages and prosody-sensitive applications (e.g., text-to-speech, dialogue), it is critical that lexical tone and other suprasegmental features be reliably encoded. The authors suspect current quantisation approaches may favour phonetic segments over suprasegmental cues and investigate this limitation.

Method: They analyze SSL latent representations and their quantised DSUs for tone languages (Mandarin and Yorùbá). First, they verify whether the continuous SSL representations themselves encode lexical tone. Then they examine how different quantisation approaches, including but not limited to K-means, convert these continuous features into DSUs and assess how well these DSUs preserve tone information versus phonetic (segmental) structure. Finally, they explore a two-stage quantisation: performing K-means once to capture phonetic information, then clustering the residual representation to target tonal information.

Result: The continuous SSL latent representations are found to encode lexical tone information. However, after quantisation into DSUs, the representations disproportionately emphasize phonetic (segmental) structure, making tone encoding less reliable across multiple quantisation schemes, not just K-means. The exploratory residual clustering approach improves the encoding of lexical tone compared to standard single-stage quantisation.

Conclusion: Current DSU quantisation strategies are not well suited for capturing suprasegmental features such as lexical tone, despite the fact that the underlying SSL latent space does contain such information. This indicates a need for new quantisation and representation learning methods that are explicitly tone- or prosody-aware. A promising direction is multi-stage or residual-based clustering, where one stage captures segmental information and subsequent stages focus on suprasegmental features like tone.

Abstract: Discrete speech units (DSUs) are derived by quantising representations from models trained using self-supervised learning (SSL). They are a popular representation for a wide variety of spoken language tasks, including those where prosody matters. DSUs are especially convenient for tasks where text and speech are jointly modelled, such as text-to-speech and multimodal dialogue systems. But we have found that DSUs encode suprasegmental information less reliably than segmental structure, which we demonstrate in this work using lexical tone, though this limitation likely extends to other suprasegmental features such as prosody.
  Our investigations using the tone languages Mandarin and Yorùbá show that the SSL latent representations themselves do encode tone, yet DSUs obtained using quantisation tend to prioritise phonetic structure, which makes lexical tone less reliably encoded. This remains true for a variety of quantisation methods, not only the most common, K-means.
  We conclude that current DSU quantisation strategies have limitations for suprasegmental features, which suggests a need for new, tone-aware (or prosody-aware) techniques in speech representation learning. We point towards a potential form of the solution by performing K-means clustering once to encode phonetic information, then again on the residual representation, which better encodes lexical tone.

</details>


### [5] [Enabling Intrinsic Reasoning over Dense Geospatial Embeddings with DFR-Gemma](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07490)
*Xuechen Zhang,Aviv Slobodkin,Joydeep Paul,Mandar Sharma,Samet Oymak,Shravya Shetty,Gautam Prasad*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces DFR-Gemma, a framework that lets LLMs directly reason over dense geospatial embeddings instead of converting them to text, improving efficiency and zero-shot performance on geospatial QA tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current geospatial foundation models produce rich embeddings of population and mobility dynamics, but LLMs cannot use them effectively. Existing integrations either use embeddings only for retrieval or convert them to text, which adds redundancy, wastes tokens, and can distort numeric and spatial information. The authors aim to unlock direct, efficient, and accurate reasoning over these embeddings inside LLMs.

Method: They propose Direct Feature Reasoning-Gemma (DFR-Gemma), which introduces a lightweight projector that maps high-dimensional geospatial embeddings (e.g., from PDFM) into the latent space of an LLM so they can be treated as semantic tokens. These projected embedding tokens are concatenated with natural language instructions, letting the LLM reason intrinsically over spatial features without intermediate textual descriptions. To assess this, they build a multi-task benchmark pairing embeddings with question-answer tasks covering feature querying, comparison, and semantic description.

Result: Experiments on the new benchmark show that DFR-Gemma enables LLMs to decode latent spatial patterns and perform accurate zero-shot reasoning across different geospatial tasks. Compared with baselines that first convert embeddings to text, DFR-Gemma achieves similar or better accuracy while being significantly more efficient in terms of token usage and computation.

Conclusion: Directly injecting projected geospatial embeddings into an LLM’s latent space is an effective paradigm for multimodal geospatial intelligence. By treating dense embeddings as primary inputs rather than converting them to text, DFR-Gemma offers a more direct, efficient, and scalable way for LLMs to perform geospatial reasoning over population and mobility data.

Abstract: Representation learning for geospatial and spatio-temporal data plays a critical role in enabling general-purpose geospatial intelligence. Recent geospatial foundation models, such as the Population Dynamics Foundation Model (PDFM), encode complex population and mobility dynamics into compact embeddings. However, their integration with Large Language Models (LLMs) remains limited. Existing approaches to LLM integration treat these embeddings as retrieval indices or convert them into textual descriptions for reasoning, introducing redundancy, token inefficiency, and numerical inaccuracies. We propose Direct Feature Reasoning-Gemma (DFR-Gemma), a novel framework that enables LLMs to reason directly over dense geospatial embeddings. DFR aligns high-dimensional embeddings with the latent space of an LLM via a lightweight projector, allowing embeddings to be injected as semantic tokens alongside natural language instructions. This design eliminates the need for intermediate textual representations and enables intrinsic reasoning over spatial features. To evaluate this paradigm, we introduce a multi-task geospatial benchmark that pairs embeddings with diverse question-answer tasks, including feature querying, comparison, and semantic description. Experimental results show that DFR allows LLMs to decode latent spatial patterns and perform accurate zero-shot reasoning across tasks, while significantly improving efficiency compared to text-based baselines. Our results demonstrate that treating embeddings as primary data inputs, provides a more direct, efficient, and scalable approach to multimodal geospatial intelligence.

</details>


### [6] [Decompose, Look, and Reason: Reinforced Latent Reasoning for VLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07518)
*Mengdan Zhu,Senhao Cheng,Liang Zhao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: DLR is a new framework that improves complex visual reasoning in vision-language models by using reinforced latent reasoning instead of purely textual chains-of-thought.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Vision-language models lose important visual details when converting images into text-based chain-of-thought, and existing fixes either require costly external tools or rely on local patch features that don’t capture semantics needed for multi-step reasoning.

Method: DLR decomposes a query into textual premises, conditions a continuous visual latent representation on each premise, and reasons over these latents to produce grounded rationales and answers. It uses a three-stage training pipeline and a Spherical Gaussian Latent Policy to explore and learn effective latent trajectories for reasoning.

Result: On multiple vision-centric benchmarks, DLR outperforms strong baselines, including text-only reasoning, interleaved multimodal chain-of-thought, and previous latent reasoning approaches, while also producing more interpretable step-by-step rationales.

Conclusion: Reinforced latent reasoning with premise-conditioned visual latents and a specialized exploration policy can significantly enhance complex visual reasoning and interpretability in vision-language models, without relying on costly external tools or limited patch-based embeddings.

Abstract: Vision-Language Models often struggle with complex visual reasoning due to the visual information loss in textual CoT. Existing methods either add the cost of tool calls or rely on localized patch-based embeddings that are insufficient to extract semantics in multi-step reasoning. We propose \emph{"Decompose, Look, and Reason" (DLR)}, a reinforced latent reasoning framework that dynamically decomposes queries into textual premises, extracts premise-conditioned continuous visual latents, and deduces answers through grounded rationales. We introduce a three-stage training pipeline and propose a novel Spherical Gaussian Latent Policy to enable effective exploration in the latent space. Extensive experiments on vision-centric benchmarks show that DLR consistently outperforms strong baselines, including text-only, interleaved multimodal CoT, and latent reasoning methods, while providing superior stepwise interpretability.

</details>


### [7] [EMSDialog: Synthetic Multi-person Emergency Medical Service Dialogue Generation from Electronic Patient Care Reports via Multi-LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07549)
*Xueren Ge,Sahil Murtaza,Anthony Cortez,Homa Alemzadeh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce EMSDialog, a synthetic but realistic multi-speaker EMS dialogue dataset for conversational diagnosis prediction and show it improves diagnostic performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Conversational diagnosis prediction in clinical settings needs models that can follow evolving evidence in real-time, multi-party conversations and decide when to commit to a diagnosis. Existing datasets are mostly dyadic (two speakers) and lack realistic multi-party workflows, turn-level topic structure, and rich diagnostic annotations, which limits the development and evaluation of such models.

Method: They build an ePCR-grounded, topic-flow-based multi-agent generation pipeline. The pipeline: (1) uses real-world electronic patient care report (ePCR) records as grounding; (2) iteratively plans, generates, and self-refines multi-speaker EMS dialogues; and (3) applies rule-based factual consistency checks and topic-flow constraints to maintain realism and coherence. This yields synthetic multi-party conversations annotated with diagnoses, speaker roles, and turn-level topics. They then train conversational diagnosis prediction models with and without this dataset and evaluate using human raters and LLM-based metrics at both utterance and conversation levels.

Result: The pipeline produces EMSDialog, a dataset of 4,414 synthetic, multi-speaker EMS conversations grounded in real ePCR data, annotated with 43 diagnoses, speaker roles, and per-turn topics. Human and LLM evaluations indicate that the generated dialogues are high quality and realistic. Models trained with EMSDialog achieve better performance on conversational diagnosis prediction, specifically in accuracy, timeliness (how early they get the right diagnosis), and stability (consistency of predictions over time).

Conclusion: Synthetic, carefully controlled, multi-party EMS dialogue data grounded in real ePCR records can effectively support conversational diagnosis prediction. The EMSDialog dataset and the proposed generation pipeline provide a practical way to overcome the lack of suitable multi-speaker clinical dialogue corpora and yield measurable gains in diagnostic accuracy, speed, and stability.

Abstract: Conversational diagnosis prediction requires models to track evolving evidence in streaming clinical conversations and decide when to commit to a diagnosis. Existing medical dialogue corpora are largely dyadic or lack the multi-party workflow and annotations needed for this setting. We introduce an ePCR-grounded, topic-flow-based multi-agent generation pipeline that iteratively plans, generates, and self-refines dialogues with rule-based factual and topic flow checks. The pipeline yields EMSDialog, a dataset of 4,414 synthetic multi-speaker EMS conversations based on a real-world ePCR dataset, annotated with 43 diagnoses, speaker roles, and turn-level topics. Human and LLM evaluations confirm high quality and realism of EMSDialog using both utterance- and conversation-level metrics. Results show that EMSDialog-augmented training improves accuracy, timeliness, and stability of EMS conversational diagnosis prediction.

</details>


### [8] [TR-EduVSum: A Turkish-Focused Dataset and Consensus Framework for Educational Video Summarization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07553)
*Figen Eğin,Aytuğ Onan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They build an automatic, reproducible way to generate a gold-standard summary from many human summaries of Turkish educational videos, via a new dataset and an automatic pyramid-style consensus method (AutoMUP).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing summarization evaluation often depends on a single gold summary, is expensive to build, and can be subjective. Pyramid-based methods that aggregate content units from multiple human references are powerful but usually require manual work. The authors want a scalable, fully automatic, and reproducible way to derive high-quality gold summaries from many human summaries, especially for Turkish educational videos where resources are scarce.

Method: They create TR-EduVSum, a dataset of 82 Turkish Data Structures and Algorithms course videos with 3281 human summaries. They propose AutoMUP (Automatic Meaning Unit Pyramid): (1) segment human summaries into meaning units; (2) obtain embeddings for each unit and cluster them to group semantically similar content; (3) model inter-participant agreement statistically to compute a consensus weight for each cluster based on how many annotators mention that content; and (4) generate graded summaries by selecting and ordering meaning units according to their consensus weights. The highest-consensus configuration (using the most frequently supported meaning units) is defined as the gold summary.

Result: AutoMUP-generated summaries show high semantic overlap with summaries produced by strong LLMs like Flash 2.5 and GPT-5.1, suggesting that the automatic golds are robust. Ablation experiments demonstrate that both the consensus weighting mechanism and the embedding-based clustering are critical for achieving good summary quality; removing or weakening these components degrades performance.

Conclusion: An automatic, pyramid-inspired method (AutoMUP) can reliably derive gold-standard summaries from many human references without manual pyramid construction. The TR-EduVSum dataset and AutoMUP provide a scalable way to build summarization benchmarks for Turkish educational videos, and the technique is likely transferable to other Turkic languages at low cost.

Abstract: This study presents a framework for generating the gold-standard summary fully automatically and reproducibly based on multiple human summaries of Turkish educational videos. Within the scope of the study, a new dataset called TR-EduVSum was created, encompassing 82 Turkish course videos in the field of "Data Structures and Algorithms" and containing a total of 3281 independent human summaries. Inspired by existing pyramid-based evaluation approaches, the AutoMUP (Automatic Meaning Unit Pyramid) method is proposed, which extracts consensus-based content from multiple human summaries. AutoMUP clusters the meaning units extracted from human summaries using embedding, statistically models inter-participant agreement, and generates graded summaries based on consensus weight. In this framework, the gold summary corresponds to the highest-consensus AutoMUP configuration, constructed from the most frequently supported meaning units across human summaries. Experimental results show that AutoMUP summaries exhibit high semantic overlap with robust LLM (Large Language Model) summaries such as Flash 2.5 and GPT-5.1. Furthermore, ablation studies clearly demonstrate the decisive role of consensus weight and clustering in determining summary quality. The proposed approach can be generalized to other Turkic languages at low cost.

</details>


### [9] [Reasoning-Based Refinement of Unsupervised Text Clusters with LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07562)
*Tunazzina Islam*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They use large language models to check and improve the quality of clusters produced by unsupervised text clustering, making topics more coherent, less redundant, and better labeled without any labeled data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Unsupervised clustering and topic modeling on large text corpora often produce noisy, incoherent, or redundant clusters, and it is hard to validate or interpret these clusters without labeled data or gold-standard annotations. Standard embedding-based methods struggle to ensure semantic coherence and interpretability, especially on messy real-world data like social media. There is a need for a general framework that can validate and refine the semantic structure discovered by unsupervised methods in a way that aligns with human judgments while remaining fully unsupervised.

Method: They introduce a reasoning-based refinement framework that treats large language models as semantic judges rather than just embedding generators. The framework operates after any unsupervised clustering algorithm and has three stages: (i) coherence verification, where the LLM checks whether each cluster’s summary is well supported by its member texts; (ii) redundancy adjudication, where the LLM determines whether clusters are semantically overlapping and should be merged or discarded; and (iii) label grounding, where the LLM generates interpretable, human-readable labels for each validated cluster without supervision. This decouples feature representation learning from structural validation and uses LLM reasoning to filter, restructure, and label clusters.

Result: On real-world social media data from two different platforms, the framework yields clusters that are more coherent and better aligned with human perception than those produced by classical topic models and recent representation-based baselines. Human annotators show strong agreement with the LLM-generated labels even though there are no gold-standard labels. Additional robustness experiments under matched time periods and dataset sizes suggest that the refined clusters are stable across platforms.

Conclusion: Using LLMs as reasoning-based semantic judges is an effective, general way to validate and refine the outputs of unsupervised clustering methods. This approach improves coherence, reduces redundancy, and produces interpretable labels for clusters without requiring any labeled data, thereby enabling more reliable and interpretable analysis of large text collections in an unsupervised setting.

Abstract: Unsupervised methods are widely used to induce latent semantic structure from large text collections, yet their outputs often contain incoherent, redundant, or poorly grounded clusters that are difficult to validate without labeled data. We propose a reasoning-based refinement framework that leverages large language models (LLMs) not as embedding generators, but as semantic judges that validate and restructure the outputs of arbitrary unsupervised clustering algorithms.Our framework introduces three reasoning stages: (i) coherence verification, where LLMs assess whether cluster summaries are supported by their member texts; (ii) redundancy adjudication, where candidate clusters are merged or rejected based on semantic overlap; and (iii) label grounding, where clusters are assigned interpretable labels in a fully unsupervised manner. This design decouples representation learning from structural validation and mitigates common failure modes of embedding-only approaches. We evaluate the framework on real-world social media corpora from two platforms with distinct interaction models, demonstrating consistent improvements in cluster coherence and human-aligned labeling quality over classical topic models and recent representation-based baselines. Human evaluation shows strong agreement with LLM-generated labels, despite the absence of gold-standard annotations. We further conduct robustness analyses under matched temporal and volume conditions to assess cross-platform stability. Beyond empirical gains, our results suggest that LLM-based reasoning can serve as a general mechanism for validating and refining unsupervised semantic structure, enabling more reliable and interpretable analyses of large text collections without supervision.

</details>


### [10] [CAMO: A Class-Aware Minority-Optimized Ensemble for Robust Language Model Evaluation on Imbalanced Data](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07583)
*Mohamed Ehab,Ali Hamdi,Khaled Shaban*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CAMO is an ensemble method tailored for imbalanced text classification that boosts minority-class predictions using class-aware voting and uncertainty, achieving state-of-the-art macro F1 on two domain-specific benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard ensemble methods tend to favor majority classes in imbalanced datasets, hurting minority-class performance and macro F1. The authors want an ensemble framework that explicitly addresses class imbalance while remaining broadly applicable across domains and model types.

Method: They introduce CAMO (Class-Aware Minority-Optimized), a hierarchical ensemble procedure that leverages vote distributions, confidence calibration, and inter-model uncertainty. CAMO dynamically upweights underrepresented classes and emphasizes minority predictions while maintaining stable performance on majority classes. It is evaluated on two highly imbalanced, domain-specific classification benchmarks (DIAR-AI/Emotion and BEA 2025 ternary) using eight language models (3 LLMs and 5 smaller LMs) in both zero-shot and fine-tuned settings, and compared against seven established ensemble baselines.

Result: On fine-tuned models, CAMO consistently achieves the highest strict macro F1-scores across the benchmarks, outperforming seven strong ensemble baselines. Its gains complement model adaptation, indicating that the optimal ensemble design depends on properties of the underlying models.

Conclusion: CAMO is an effective, domain-neutral ensemble framework for handling severe class imbalance in categorization tasks, particularly improving minority-class performance without sacrificing overall macro F1. Its class-aware, uncertainty-informed design makes it a strong choice for imbalanced real-world applications.

Abstract: Real-world categorization is severely hampered by class imbalance because traditional ensembles favor majority classes, which lowers minority performance and overall F1-score. We provide a unique ensemble technique for imbalanced problems called CAMO (Class-Aware Minority-Optimized).Through a hierarchical procedure that incorporates vote distributions, confidence calibration, and inter model uncertainty, CAMO dynamically boosts underrepresented classes while preserving and amplifying minority forecasts.We verify CAMO on two highly unbalanced, domain-specific benchmarks: the DIAR-AI/Emotion dataset and the ternary BEA 2025 dataset. We benchmark against seven proven ensemble algorithms using eight different language models (three LLMs and five SLMs) under zero-shot and fine-tuned settings .With refined models, CAMO consistently earns the greatest strict macro F1-score, setting a new benchmark. Its benefit works in concert with model adaptation, showing that the best ensemble choice depends on model properties .This proves that CAMO is a reliable, domain-neutral framework for unbalanced categorization.

</details>


### [11] [ADAG: Automatically Describing Attribution Graphs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07615)
*Aryaman Arora,Zhengxuan Wu,Jacob Steinhardt,Sarah Schwettmann*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose ADAG, a fully automated pipeline to interpret language model circuits by generating natural-language descriptions of features and their roles.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Circuit tracing in language models currently depends on manual, ad-hoc human analysis of internal features, which is slow, subjective, and hard to scale. There is a need for an automated, systematic way to describe which internal features contribute to behavior and how.

Method: They define attribution profiles based on input and output gradient effects to characterize each feature’s functional role. Then they cluster features using a new clustering algorithm and use an LLM explainer–simulator framework to automatically generate and evaluate natural-language explanations of these feature groups. This entire process forms the ADAG pipeline.

Result: On benchmark circuit-tracing tasks previously analyzed by humans, ADAG recovers interpretable circuits similar to human analyses. Additionally, it discovers steerable feature clusters that mediate a harmful advice jailbreak in Llama 3.1 8B Instruct, enabling targeted intervention.

Conclusion: ADAG demonstrates that circuit tracing and feature-role description can be automated end-to-end, reducing reliance on manual interpretation while still yielding interpretable and actionable insights about model internals, including for identifying and steering harmful behaviors.

Abstract: In language model interpretability research, \textbf{circuit tracing} aims to identify which internal features causally contributed to a particular output and how they affected each other, with the goal of explaining the computations underlying some behaviour. However, all prior circuit tracing work has relied on ad-hoc human interpretation of the role that each feature in the circuit plays, via manual inspection of data artifacts such as the dataset examples the component activates on. We introduce \textbf{ADAG}, an end-to-end pipeline for describing these attribution graphs which is fully automated. To achieve this, we introduce \textit{attribution profiles} which quantify the functional role of a feature via its input and output gradient effects. We then introduce a novel clustering algorithm for grouping features, and an LLM explainer--simulator setup which generates and scores natural-language explanations of the functional role of these feature groups. We run our system on known human-analysed circuit-tracing tasks and recover interpretable circuits, and further show ADAG can find steerable clusters which are responsible for a harmful advice jailbreak in Llama 3.1 8B Instruct.

</details>


### [12] [DIVERSED: Relaxed Speculative Decoding via Dynamic Ensemble Verification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07622)
*Ziyi Wang,Siva Rajesh Kasa,Ankith M S,Santhosh Kumar Kasa,Jiaru Zou,Sumit Negi,Ruqi Zhang,Nan Jiang,Qifan Song*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces DIVERSED, a relaxed verification framework for speculative decoding that blends draft and target model distributions to improve inference speed while maintaining output quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional speculative decoding requires that verified tokens exactly match the target model’s distribution, causing many reasonable drafted tokens to be rejected. This rigid constraint reduces token acceptance rates and limits the achievable speedup in large language model inference. The authors aim to alleviate this bottleneck to get faster inference without hurting generation quality.

Method: They propose Dynamic Verification Relaxed Speculative Decoding (DIVERSED), which replaces strict verification with a learned, ensemble-based verifier. This verifier dynamically blends the draft model and target model token distributions using task-dependent and context-dependent weights. The framework is theoretically analyzed to justify its correctness and impact on quality, and is implemented on top of standard speculative decoding for empirical evaluation.

Result: Experiments show that DIVERSED significantly increases token acceptance rates and overall inference efficiency compared to standard speculative decoding, achieving higher time speedups while maintaining comparable generation quality.

Conclusion: By relaxing the verification step through a dynamic ensemble of draft and target distributions, DIVERSED overcomes the main bottleneck of speculative decoding. The method yields substantially faster inference for large language models without sacrificing output quality, and is supported by both theoretical analysis and empirical results.

Abstract: Speculative decoding is an effective technique for accelerating large language model inference by drafting multiple tokens in parallel. In practice, its speedup is often bottlenecked by a rigid verification step that strictly enforces the accepted token distribution to exactly match the target model. This constraint leads to the rejection of many plausible tokens, lowering the acceptance rate and limiting overall time speedup. To overcome this limitation, we propose Dynamic Verification Relaxed Speculative Decoding (DIVERSED), a relaxed verification framework that improves time efficiency while preserving generation quality. DIVERSED learns an ensemble-based verifier that blends the draft and target model distributions with a task-dependent and context-dependent weight. We provide theoretical justification for our approach and demonstrate empirically that DIVERSED achieves substantially higher inference efficiency compared to standard speculative decoding methods. Code is available at: https://github.com/comeusr/diversed.

</details>


### [13] [Efficient and Effective Internal Memory Retrieval for LLM-Based Healthcare Prediction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07659)
*Mingchen Li,Jiatan Huang,Zonghai Yao,Hong yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Keys to Knowledge (K2K), an internal key-based knowledge access framework that encodes clinical knowledge directly into an LLM’s parameters to replace external RAG for faster, more reliable healthcare outcome prediction.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are promising for healthcare but prone to hallucinations and lack detailed medical context. RAG helps but requires expensive, slow retrieval over large external knowledge bases, which is unsuitable for time-sensitive clinical use. The authors seek a way to get RAG-like grounding and reliability without high latency and computational overhead.

Method: They introduce K2K, which encodes essential clinical information as keys directly within the model’s parameter space, enabling retrieval from internal key-value memory rather than external databases. The system constructs probes guided by model activations to query this internal memory and employs cross-attention-based reranking to improve which internal knowledge is selected, thereby eliminating inference-time external search while preserving contextually relevant retrieval.

Result: On four benchmark healthcare outcome prediction datasets, K2K achieves state-of-the-art performance, outperforming existing methods that rely on standard RAG or baseline LLM approaches, while avoiding the latency costs of large external retrieval.

Conclusion: Internal key-based knowledge access via K2K can substitute for conventional external RAG in clinical LLM applications, yielding faster, more accurate, and more reliable outcome predictions. This suggests that encoding curated domain knowledge into model parameters, combined with activation-guided probing and cross-attention reranking, is a practical path to deploy LLMs in time-critical healthcare settings.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) hold significant promise for healthcare, yet their reliability in high-stakes clinical settings is often compromised by hallucinations and a lack of granular medical context. While Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG) can mitigate these issues, standard supervised pipelines require computationally intensive searches over massive external knowledge bases, leading to high latency that is impractical for time-sensitive care. To address this, we introduce Keys to Knowledge (K2K), a novel framework that replaces external retrieval with internal, key-based knowledge access. By encoding essential clinical information directly into the model's parameter space, K2K enables rapid retrieval from internal key-value memory without inference-time overhead. We further enhance retrieval quality through activation-guided probe construction and cross-attention reranking. Experimental results demonstrate that K2K achieves state-of-the-art performance across four benchmark healthcare outcome prediction datasets.

GatorTron-large achieved the best overall performance (Micro F1 = 0.62). Performance varied by stigma subscale, with Negative Self-Image showing the highest predictability and Personalized Stigma remaining the most challenging. The authors aim to address this limitation without retraining models or modifying their architectures, to make LLMs more reliable and efficient on long-context numerical tasks.

Method: They propose Separate Sequence (SepSeq), a plug-and-play, training-free framework that strategically inserts separator tokens into input sequences. These separators function as attention sinks, segmenting the sequence so that attention focuses more on local regions while still maintaining access to global context. The method is applied at inference time to existing LLMs.

Additionally, the approach reduces total inference token consumption by 16.4% on average, indicating both performance and efficiency gains.

Conclusion: Attention dispersion in Softmax-based transformers is a key factor behind LLM failures on long numerical inputs. By inserting separator tokens as attention sinks, SepSeq can refocus attention, significantly improving accuracy and efficiency without any retraining or architectural changes, and works broadly across different LLMs.

Abstract: While transformer-based Large Language Models (LLMs) theoretically support massive context windows, they suffer from severe performance degradation when processing long numerical sequences. We attribute this failure to the attention dispersion in the Softmax mechanism, which prevents the model from concentrating attention. To overcome this, we propose Separate Sequence (SepSeq), a training-free, plug-and-play framework to mitigate dispersion by strategically inserting separator tokens. Mechanistically, we demonstrate that separator tokens act as an attention sink, recalibrating attention to focus on local segments while preserving global context. Extensive evaluations on 9 widely-adopted LLMs confirm the effectiveness of our approach: SepSeq yields an average relative accuracy improvement of 35.6% across diverse domains while reducing total inference token consumption by 16.4% on average.

### [16] [Beyond Social Pressure: Benchmarking Epistemic Attack in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07749)
*Steven Au,Sujit Noronha*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces PPT-Bench, a benchmark that tests how large language models respond to philosophical pressure that undermines knowledge, values, authority, or identity, revealing epistemic weaknesses beyond standard social-pressure tests, and evaluates mitigation strategies across models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work on sycophancy in LLMs mainly covers simple social pressures like disagreement, flattery, or preference following, but fails to capture deeper epistemic failures where models capitulate when the legitimacy of knowledge, values, or identity is challenged. The authors want a systematic way to probe and measure these richer forms of epistemic pressure and identify how vulnerable different models are and how to mitigate such failures.

Method: The authors design PPT-Bench, a diagnostic benchmark structured around a Philosophical Pressure Taxonomy with four categories: Epistemic Destabilization, Value Nullification, Authority Inversion, and Identity Dissolution. For each item, they create three levels: L0 (baseline prompt), L1 (single-turn pressure), and L2 (multi-turn Socratic escalation), and then evaluate five LLMs on this suite. They quantify epistemic inconsistency between L0 and L1, and measure conversational capitulation at L2. They also test several mitigation techniques, such as prompt-level anchoring, persona-stability prompts, and Leading Query Contrastive Decoding, comparing their effectiveness across API and open-source models.

Result: The benchmark reveals that different philosophical pressure types induce distinct, statistically separable inconsistency patterns across models, showing that epistemic attack elicits failure modes not captured by conventional social-pressure benchmarks. The experiments further show that mitigation effectiveness varies both by model and pressure type: prompt anchoring and persona-stability prompts are most effective in API-hosted models, while Leading Query Contrastive Decoding is the most consistently effective intervention for open models.

Conclusion: PPT-Bench provides a structured way to evaluate how LLMs handle sophisticated epistemic attacks, demonstrating that these attacks expose unique weaknesses beyond known sycophancy behaviors. The differentiated performance and mitigation outcomes across models and pressure types indicate that robust defense against epistemic pressure requires tailored techniques rather than one-size-fits-all fixes, and that future LLM safety and reliability work should explicitly account for these philosophical pressure dimensions.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) can shift their answers under pressure in ways that reflect accommodation rather than reasoning. Prior work on sycophancy has focused mainly on disagreement, flattery, and preference alignment, leaving a broader set of epistemic failures less explored. We introduce \textbf{PPT-Bench}, a diagnostic benchmark for evaluating \textit{epistemic attack}, where prompts challenge the legitimacy of knowledge, values, or identity rather than simply opposing a previous answer. PPT-Bench is organized around the Philosophical Pressure Taxonomy (PPT), which defines four types of philosophical pressure: Epistemic Destabilization, Value Nullification, Authority Inversion, and Identity Dissolution. Each item is tested at three layers: a baseline prompt (L0), a single-turn pressure condition (L1), and a multi-turn Socratic escalation (L2). This allows us to measure epistemic inconsistency between L0 and L1, and conversational capitulation in L2. Across five models, these pressure types produce statistically separable inconsistency patterns, suggesting that epistemic attack exposes weaknesses not captured by standard social-pressure benchmarks. Mitigation results are strongly type- and model-dependent: prompt-level anchoring and persona-stability prompts perform best in API settings, while Leading Query Contrastive Decoding is the most reliable intervention for open models.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often hallucinate when generating code, especially around external libraries (e.g., calling non-existent APIs), which is dangerous in real-world software development. Method: The authors evaluate various static analysis tools on NL-to-code benchmarks that require library usage. They run LLM-generated code through these tools, measuring how many total errors and specifically library hallucinations are detected. Result: LLMs hallucinate non-existent library features in 8.1–40% of responses on library-heavy benchmarks. Static analysis tools can detect 16–70% of all code errors, and 14–85% of library hallucinations, depending on the LLM and dataset. Manual inspection suggests that even an ideal static analysis approach could only ever detect between about 48.5% and 77% of such issues, defining a ceiling on this mitigation strategy.

Conclusion: Static analysis is a relatively cheap and useful method for detecting a sizable subset of LLM code hallucinations, particularly for library misuse, but it cannot fully solve the problem. There is an inherent upper bound—well below 100%—on what static methods can plausibly catch, so additional approaches and model improvements are necessary to more comprehensively address hallucinations.

Abstract: Despite extensive research, Large Language Models continue to hallucinate when generating code, particularly when using libraries. On NL-to-code benchmarks that require library use, we find that LLMs generate code that uses non-existent library features in 8.1-40% of responses.One intuitive approach for detection and mitigation of hallucinations is static analysis. In this paper, we analyse the potential of static analysis tools, both in terms of what they can solve and what they cannot. We find that static analysis tools can detect 16-70% of all errors, and 14-85% of library hallucinations, with performance varying by LLM and dataset. Through manual analysis, we identify cases a static method could not plausibly catch, which gives an upper bound on their potential from 48.5% to 77%. Overall, we show that static analysis methods are cheap method for addressing some forms of hallucination, and we quantify how far short of solving the problem they will always be.

### [18] [GRASS: Gradient-based Adaptive Layer-wise Importance Sampling for Memory-efficient Large Language Model Fine-tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07808)
*Kaiyuan Tian,Yu Tang,Gongqingjian Jiang,Baihui Liu,Yifu Gao,Xialin Su,Linbo Qiao,Dongsheng Li*

TL;DR: GRASS is a gradient-based, adaptive layer-wise fine-tuning framework for large language models that cuts memory while improving performance over existing parameter-efficient methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Full-parameter fine-tuning of large language models needs large GPU memory, which is often infeasible. Existing parameter-efficient approaches like low-rank adaptation and static layer-wise sampling reduce memory use but either hurt expressiveness or ignore that layer importance changes across tasks and training stages, leading to suboptimal downstream performance.

Method: GRASS performs layer-wise fine-tuning where only a subset of layers is updated at each step. It measures layer importance using mean gradient norms, which are both task-aware and training-stage-aware. Based on these importance scores, GRASS adaptively updates the sampling probabilities of layers during training. Additionally, it introduces a layer-wise optimizer state offloading mechanism that overlaps computation and communication, reducing on-device memory usage without significantly harming training throughput.

Result: Across multiple models and benchmarks, GRASS outperforms state-of-the-art parameter-efficient and layer-wise fine-tuning methods, achieving up to 4.38 percentage points higher average accuracy and up to 19.97% lower memory usage.

Conclusion: Adaptive, gradient-based layer importance estimation enables more effective layer-wise fine-tuning than static strategies. Combined with optimizer state offloading, GRASS provides a practical solution for memory-efficient fine-tuning of large language models, narrowing the performance gap to full-parameter fine-tuning while improving hardware efficiency.

Abstract: Full-parameter fine-tuning of large language models is constrained by substantial GPU memory requirements. Low-rank adaptation methods mitigate this challenge by updating only a subset of parameters. However, these approaches often limit model expressiveness and yield lower performance than full-parameter fine-tuning. Layer-wise fine-tuning methods have emerged as an alternative, enabling memory-efficient training through static layer importance sampling strategies. However, these methods overlook variations in layer importance across tasks and training stages, resulting in suboptimal performance on downstream tasks. To address these limitations, we propose GRASS, a gradient-based adaptive layer-wise importance sampling framework. GRASS utilizes mean gradient norms as a task-aware and training-stage-aware metric for estimating layer importance. Furthermore, GRASS adaptively adjusts layer sampling probabilities through an adaptive training strategy. We also introduce a layer-wise optimizer state offloading mechanism that overlaps computation and communication to further reduce memory usage while maintaining comparable training throughput. Extensive experiments across multiple models and benchmarks demonstrate that GRASS consistently outperforms state-of-the-art methods, achieving an average accuracy improvement of up to 4.38 points and reducing memory usage by up to 19.97\%.

### [19] [AsyncTLS: Efficient Generative LLM Inference with Asynchronous Two-level Sparse Attention](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07815)
*Yuxuan Hu,Jianchao Tan,Jiaqi Zhang,Wen Zan,Pingwei Sun,Yifan Lu,Yerui Sun,Yuchen Xie,Xunliang Cai,Jing Zhang*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-context inference in large language models is computationally expensive due to quadratic attention and large KV cache memory requirements. Existing sparse attention methods either incur high indexing overhead (token-level) or lose precision (block-level). There is a need for a method that balances accuracy and efficiency, while also reducing KV cache memory and transfer bottlenecks.

Method: AsyncTLS combines coarse-grained block-based filtering to quickly identify relevant regions of the context with fine-grained token-level selection within those regions to retain accuracy. It introduces an asynchronous offloading engine that leverages temporal locality to overlap KV cache transfers between memory tiers with attention computation, reducing the perceived cost of KV cache movement.

Result: On Qwen3 and GLM-4.7-Flash models using GQA and MLA architectures and context lengths of 48k–96k tokens, AsyncTLS matches the accuracy of full attention while achieving 1.2x–10.0x speedup at the operator level and 1.3x–4.7x end-to-end throughput improvement.

Conclusion: AsyncTLS effectively addresses the trade-off between accuracy and efficiency in long-context LLM inference by using hierarchical sparse attention and asynchronous KV cache offloading, achieving near-full-attention accuracy with substantial speed and throughput gains over large context lengths.

Abstract: Long-context inference in LLMs faces the dual challenges of quadratic attention complexity and prohibitive KV cache memory. While token-level sparse attention offers superior accuracy, its indexing overhead is costly; block-level methods improve efficiency but sacrifice precision. We propose AsyncTLS, a hierarchical sparse attention system that combines coarse-grained block filtering with fine-grained token selection to balance accuracy and efficiency, coupled with an asynchronous offloading engine that overlaps KV cache transfers with computation via temporal locality exploitation. Evaluated on Qwen3 and GLM-4.7-Flash across GQA, and MLA architectures, AsyncTLS achieves accuracy comparable to full attention while delivering 1.2x - 10.0x operator speedups and 1.3x - 4.7x end-to-end throughput improvements on 48k - 96k contexts.

### [21] [Loop, Think, & Generalize: Implicit Reasoning in Recurrent-Depth Transformers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07822)
*Harsh Kohli,Srinivasan Parthasarathy,Huan Sun,Yuekun Yao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how to get transformers to perform implicit multi-hop reasoning in a single forward pass using recurrent-depth transformers, showing they can generalize compositionally better than standard transformers but can suffer from overthinking when recurrence is excessive.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard transformer-based language models store lots of facts and rules in their parameters but often fail when those pieces of knowledge must be combined in new ways (implicit multi-hop reasoning). This indicates poor compositional generalization over parametric knowledge. The authors aim to understand and improve this by modifying the transformer architecture and training, so models can reliably do multi-step reasoning they were not explicitly trained to compose.

Method: They compare vanilla transformers with recurrent-depth transformers, where the same transformer layers are reused iteratively in a single forward pass. Using controlled synthetic setups and models trained from scratch, they design two main evaluation settings: (1) systematic generalization, where specific combinations of facts/rules are never seen during training, and (2) depth extrapolation, where training uses only shallow reasoning chains (e.g., up to 5 hops) but testing requires deeper chains (e.g., 10 hops). They analyze training dynamics (grokking-like behavior), perform mechanistic analyses of the learned solutions, and vary inference-time recurrence depth and training strategies to see how these affect generalization and failure modes.

Result: Vanilla transformers largely fail at both systematic generalization and depth extrapolation in the implicit reasoning tasks. Recurrent-depth transformers succeed: they can correctly compose unseen combinations of knowledge (systematic generalization) and can solve deeper reasoning chains than seen in training (depth extrapolation) by increasing the number of recurrent passes at inference. The authors observe a three-stage grokking process for systematic generalization—first memorization, then in-distribution generalization, and finally true systematic generalization—backed by mechanistic interpretability evidence. For depth extrapolation, carefully scaling the number of recurrent iterations improves performance up to a point, but excessive recurrence leads to an overthinking phenomenon where accuracy degrades at very large depths.

Conclusion: Reusing transformer layers recurrently within a forward pass is an effective way to endow models with stronger implicit multi-hop reasoning and compositional generalization abilities, outperforming standard transformers on systematic generalization and depth extrapolation tasks. However, this capability emerges via a nontrivial training trajectory resembling grokking, and is sensitive to both training strategies and the amount of recurrence used at inference. There is an inherent tradeoff: additional recurrence enables deeper reasoning but too much introduces overthinking, which harms performance on very deep compositions. The work provides both architectural and training recommendations for building and using recurrent-depth transformers for compositional implicit reasoning.

Abstract: We study implicit reasoning, i.e. the ability to combine knowledge or rules within a single forward pass. While transformer-based large language models store substantial factual knowledge and rules, they often fail to compose this knowledge for implicit multi-hop reasoning, suggesting a lack of compositional generalization over their parametric knowledge. To address this limitation, we study recurrent-depth transformers, which enables iterative computation over the same transformer layers. We investigate two compositional generalization challenges under the implicit reasoning scenario: systematic generalization, i.e. combining knowledge that is never used for compositions during training, and depth extrapolation, i.e. generalizing from limited reasoning depth (e.g. training on up to 5-hop) to deeper compositions (e.g. 10-hop). Through controlled studies with models trained from scratch, we show that while vanilla transformers struggle with both generalization challenges, recurrent-depth transformers can effectively make such generalization. For systematic generalization, we find that this ability emerges through a three-stage grokking process, transitioning from memorization to in-distribution generalization and finally to systematic generalization, supported by mechanistic analysis. For depth extrapolation, we show that generalization beyond training depth can be unlocked by scaling inference-time recurrence, with more iterations enabling deeper reasoning. We further study how training strategies affect extrapolation, providing guidance on training recurrent-depth transformers, and identify a key limitation, overthinking, where excessive recurrence degrades predictions and limits generalization to very deep compositions.

</details>

Demographic extraction shows Reddit can provide a diverse caregiver loneliness dataset.

Conclusion: The study validates an LLM-based pipeline as a practical way to build high-quality social media datasets for loneliness research. It demonstrates that LLMs, combined with expert frameworks and human validation, can reliably evaluate loneliness levels and categorize its causes at scale. The findings reveal that caregivers experience loneliness in qualitatively different ways than non-caregivers, and they support the use of Reddit as a valuable data source for understanding caregiver loneliness and population-level differences in loneliness manifestations.

Abstract: This paper presents an LLM-driven approach for constructing diverse social media datasets to measure and compare loneliness in the caregiver and non-caregiver populations. We introduce an expert-developed loneliness evaluation framework and an expert-informed typology for categorizing causes of loneliness for analyzing social media text. Using a human-validated data processing pipeline, we apply GPT-4o, GPT-5-nano, and GPT-5 to build a high-quality Reddit corpus and analyze loneliness across both populations. The loneliness evaluation framework achieved average accuracies of 76.09% and 79.78% for caregivers and non-caregivers, respectively. The cause categorization framework achieved micro-aggregate F1 scores of 0.825 and 0.80 for caregivers and non-caregivers, respectively. Across populations, we observe substantial differences in the distribution of types of causes of loneliness. Caregivers' loneliness were predominantly linked to caregiving roles, identity recognition, and feelings of abandonment, indicating distinct loneliness experiences between the two groups. Demographic extraction further demonstrates the viability of Reddit for building a diverse caregiver loneliness dataset. Overall, this work establishes an LLM-based pipeline for creating high quality social media datasets for studying loneliness and demonstrates its effectiveness in analyzing population-level differences in the manifestation of loneliness.

### [23] [MemReader: From Passive to Active Extraction for Long-Term Agent Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07877)
*Jingyi Kang,Chunyu Li,Ding Chen,Bo Tang,Feiyu Xiong,Zhiyu Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes MemReader, a family of models for active, selective long-term memory extraction in agent systems, improving memory quality and downstream reasoning compared to passive, one-shot extractors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current agent memory systems passively transcribe dialogue into structured memories in a single shot, which fails under noisy, incomplete, or cross-turn-dependent conversations. This leads to polluted, low-value, and inconsistent long-term memory, limiting personalization, reliability, and autonomy of agents. The authors aim to make memory extraction more accurate, selective, and reasoning-aware so that agents maintain cleaner, more useful long-term memories.

Method: The authors design two complementary models. MemReader-0.6B is a compact, passively used extractor distilled to output accurate, schema-consistent structured memories. MemReader-4B explicitly reasons over information value, ambiguity, and completeness, and can choose among actions: writing new memories, deferring incomplete information, retrieving prior context to resolve references, or discarding irrelevant dialogue. They evaluate these models on several long-term memory benchmarks involving noisy dialogue, temporal updates, and hallucination-prone settings.

Result: Across benchmarks like LOCOMO, LongMemEval, and HaluMem, MemReader models outperform prior extraction-based baselines. MemReader-4B, in particular, sets a new state of the art on tasks that require knowledge updating, temporal reasoning, and reducing hallucinations, demonstrating better long-term memory management and utilization than passive approaches.

Conclusion: The study concludes that effective long-term memory for agents requires not just extracting more facts but performing reasoning-driven, selective extraction and writing. Active decision-making about what, when, and whether to store leads to lower-noise, dynamically updated memories and better downstream performance. The MemReader models and APIs are released and already integrated into MemOS and real-world applications, encouraging further research and adoption of active memory extraction paradigms.

Abstract: Long-term memory is fundamental for personalized and autonomous agents, yet populating it remains a bottleneck. Existing systems treat memory extraction as a one-shot, passive transcription from context to structured entries, which struggles with noisy dialogue, missing references, and cross-turn dependencies, leading to memory pollution, low-value writes, and inconsistency. In this paper, we introduce the MemReader family for active long-term memory extraction in agent systems: MemReader-0.6B, a compact and cost-efficient passive extractor distilled for accurate and schema-consistent structured outputs, and MemReader-4B, an active extractor optimized with Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) to make memory writing decisions. Under a ReAct-style paradigm, MemReader-4B explicitly evaluates information value, reference ambiguity, and completeness before acting, and can selectively write memories, defer incomplete inputs, retrieve historical context, or discard irrelevant chatter. Experiments on LOCOMO, LongMemEval, and HaluMem show that MemReader consistently outperforms existing extraction-based baselines. In particular, MemReader-4B achieves state-of-the-art performance on tasks involving knowledge updating, temporal reasoning, and hallucination reduction. These results suggest that effective agent memory requires not merely extracting more information, but performing reasoning-driven and selective memory extraction to build low-noise and dynamically evolving long-term memory. Furthermore, MemReader has been integrated into MemOS and is being deployed in real-world applications. To support future research and adoption, we release the models and provide public API access.

### [24] [Contextualising (Im)plausible Events Triggers Figurative Language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07885)
*Annerose Eichel,Tonmoy Rakshit,Sabine Schulte im Walde*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how humans and large language models (LLMs) differ in judging whether simple subject-verb-object events are literal, non-literal, plausible, or implausible.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Understanding the relationship between literalness and plausibility is key for interpreting language, especially figurative or metaphorical expressions. Existing work often assumes LLMs approximate human judgments on plausibility and literalness, but this assumption has not been systematically tested with controlled examples that vary both in concreteness/abstractness and plausibility. The authors want to clarify how well LLMs capture humanlike nuance in these judgments and where they fail.

Method: The authors construct a controlled set of English subject-verb-object event triples that cross two dimensions: (1) plausible vs. implausible events and (2) abstract vs. concrete categories for subjects and objects. They then collect human judgments and contextualizations of these events, asking people to assess plausibility and interpret whether events are literal or non-literal, and to provide example contexts. They run the same tasks with large language models, comparing LLM-generated judgments and example contexts to human data, and then analyze differences in how plausibility and (non-)literalness are handled.

Result: Humans distinguish carefully between implausible events that might be understood as figurative or metaphorical and those that are simply impossible or odd, and they generate rich, nuanced contexts that clarify when an event is literal vs. non-literal. In contrast, LLMs show shallow contextualization patterns: instead of recognizing genuine implausibility, they often reinterpret implausible events as non-literal but still plausible in some figurative sense, effectively "explaining away" implausibility. This leads to systematic divergences from human plausibility judgments.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that LLMs do not yet match human nuance in separating literalness from plausibility in simple event descriptions. They exhibit a strong bias to reinterpret implausible events as non-literal but still plausibly interpretable, rather than recognizing them as genuinely implausible. This reveals limitations in LLMs’ semantic and pragmatic understanding and suggests that relying on LLMs for humanlike plausibility and literalness judgments, especially in figurative language contexts, is premature without further model and evaluation improvements.

Abstract: This work explores the connection between (non-)literalness and plausibility at the example of subject-verb-object events in English. We design a systematic setup of plausible and implausible event triples in combination with abstract and concrete constituent categories. Our analysis of human and LLM-generated judgments and example contexts reveals substantial differences between assessments of plausibility. While humans excel at nuanced detection and contextualization of (non-)literal vs. implausible events, LLM results reveal only shallow contextualization patterns with a bias to trade implausibility for non-literal, plausible interpretations.

### [25] [Linear Representations of Hierarchical Concepts in Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07886)
*Masaki Sakata,Benjamin Heinzerling,Takumi Ito,Sho Yokoi,Kentaro Inui*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how well language models linearly encode hierarchical relations (like country–region–continent) in their internal representations, using domain- and depth-specific linear transformations across layers and multi-token entities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although language models capture various semantic relations, it is unclear how hierarchical concept structures (e.g., geographic or taxonomic hierarchies) are internally represented, especially for multi-token entities and across layers. Prior work has mostly focused on simpler or single-token settings. Understanding this can shed light on the interpretability and structure of LM representations.

Method: The authors extend the Linear Relational Concepts framework. For each semantic domain (e.g., geography) and each depth in a hierarchy (e.g., city, country, region), they train linear transformations that map model representations to hierarchical levels. They then compare these transformations to characterize how hierarchical relations differ in representation space. They analyze both in-domain generalization (to unseen entities within the same domain) and cross-domain transfer, and study representations across layers and for multi-token entities. They further inspect the dimensionality and domain-specificity of the subspace that encodes hierarchical information.

Result: Within each domain, hierarchical relations can be linearly decoded from the internal representations, indicating that hierarchy information is recoverable via simple linear maps. The hierarchy-encoding subspace is low-dimensional and tends to be specific to each domain. However, when comparing across domains, these domain-specific subspaces turn out to be highly similar, suggesting a shared structural pattern for encoding hierarchies across different semantic areas.

Conclusion: Language models consistently encode concept hierarchies in a way that is linearly recoverable and highly interpretable. Hierarchical information resides in low-dimensional, largely domain-specific subspaces, but these subspaces are structurally similar across domains, pointing to a common representational scheme for hierarchies in modern LMs.

Abstract: We investigate how and to what extent hierarchical relations (e.g., Japan $\subset$ Eastern Asia $\subset$ Asia) are encoded in the internal representations of language models. Building on Linear Relational Concepts, we train linear transformations specific to each hierarchical depth and semantic domain, and characterize representational differences associated with hierarchical relations by comparing these transformations. Going beyond prior work on the representational geometry of hierarchies in LMs, our analysis covers multi-token entities and cross-layer representations. Across multiple domains we learn such transformations and evaluate in-domain generalization to unseen data and cross-domain transfer. Experiments show that, within a domain, hierarchical relations can be linearly recovered from model representations. We then analyze how hierarchical information is encoded in representation space. We find that it is encoded in a relatively low-dimensional subspace and that this subspace tends to be domain-specific. Our main result is that hierarchy representation is highly similar across these domain-specific subspaces. Overall, we find that all models considered in our experiments encode concept hierarchies in the form of highly interpretable linear representations.
