### [26] [Data Selection for Multi-turn Dialogue Instruction Tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07892)
*Bo Li,Shikun Zhang,Wei Ye*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes MDS, a framework for selecting high-quality multi-turn dialogue data for instruction-tuning LLMs, by scoring entire conversations instead of individual turns.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-turn dialogue corpora used for instruction-tuning are noisy and inconsistent, suffering from topic drift, repetitive chit-chat, and mismatched answer formats. Most existing data selection methods work at the single-turn level and fail to capture conversation-level structure and quality. There is a need for a principled, dialogue-level selection method that can pick representative, structurally sound conversations under a fixed training budget.

Method: MDS (Multi-turn Dialogue Selection) is a dialogue-level framework that assigns scores to full conversations using two stages: (1) Global coverage: represents user-query trajectories and performs bin-wise selection to keep dialogues that are representative of the space but not redundant. (2) Local structural scoring: assesses reliability within each dialogue via entity-grounded topic grounding and information progress, and also checks query–answer format consistency to enforce functional alignment. Dialogues are then selected according to these combined scores.

Result: On three multi-turn benchmarks and an in-domain Banking test set, MDS outperforms strong single-turn data selectors, dialogue-level LLM-based scorers, and heuristic baselines. It achieves the best overall rank across both reference-free and reference-based evaluation metrics and shows better robustness on long conversations under the same training budget.

Conclusion: Selecting at the dialogue level with both global coverage and local structural criteria yields higher-quality multi-turn training data than single-turn or purely heuristic methods. MDS improves instruction-tuned model performance, especially on long conversations, and provides a more principled way to curate dialogue datasets. Code and resources are released for reproducibility and further use.

Abstract: Instruction-tuned language models increasingly rely on large multi-turn dialogue corpora, but these datasets are often noisy and structurally inconsistent, with topic drift, repetitive chitchat, and mismatched answer formats across turns. We address this from a data selection perspective and propose \textbf{MDS} (Multi-turn Dialogue Selection), a dialogue-level framework that scores whole conversations rather than isolated turns. MDS combines a global coverage stage that performs bin-wise selection in the user-query trajectory space to retain representative yet non-redundant dialogues, with a local structural stage that evaluates within-dialogue reliability through entity-grounded topic grounding and information progress, together with query-answer form consistency for functional alignment. MDS outperforms strong single-turn selectors, dialogue-level LLM scorers, and heuristic baselines on three multi-turn benchmarks and an in-domain Banking test set, achieving the best overall rank across reference-free and reference-based metrics, and is more robust on long conversations under the same training budget. Code and resources are included in the supplementary materials.

Conclusion: TSUBASA improves personalized LLMs’ ability to handle long-horizon user interactions by jointly optimizing how memories are written and read. Abstract: Personalized large language models (PLLMs) have garnered significant attention for their ability to align outputs with individual's needs and preferences. However, they still struggle with long-horizon tasks, such as tracking a user's extensive history of conversations or activities. Existing memory mechanisms often fail to capture evolving behaviors, and RAG paradigms are trapped by a quality-efficiency tradeoff. Meanwhile, parametric adaptation is bottlenecked by train-inference gap due to the scarcity of labeled data. Our analyses further confirms that TSUBASA breaks the quality-efficiency barrier to achieve Pareto improvements, delivering robust, high-fidelity personalization with a reduced token budget.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Cross-document relation extraction requires understanding and linking entities across multiple documents, but existing "small language model + classifier" approaches are limited by the weaker semantic understanding of small models. Initial attempts to directly apply LLMs do not reliably outperform these baselines, especially when there is a large set of predefined relations that makes flat classification difficult. The authors are motivated to understand why LLMs underperform and to design a method that better exploits LLM capabilities while coping with the large relation space.

Method: The authors first empirically investigate LLM performance on cross-document relation extraction and analyze the causes of underperformance. They then propose HCRE, an LLM-based hierarchical classification framework. HCRE builds a hierarchical relation tree from the predefined relation set and uses an LLM to conduct level-by-level relation prediction along this tree. At each level, the LLM chooses among a small set of child nodes, reducing the cognitive load versus selecting directly from all relations. To address error propagation from incorrect higher-level decisions, they introduce a prediction-then-verification inference strategy, in which predictions at each level are rechecked using multi-view verification to improve reliability.

Result: Experiments across cross-document RE benchmarks show that HCRE achieves better performance than existing baselines based on small language models and standard classifiers, as well as direct LLM-based approaches. The hierarchical classification combined with prediction-then-verification effectively improves prediction accuracy in the presence of many predefined relations.

Conclusion: Directly applying LLMs to cross-document relation extraction does not automatically yield superior performance, primarily due to the difficulty of flat classification over many relation types. Structuring the relation space into a hierarchical relation tree and using LLMs for stepwise, verified decisions significantly enhances performance. The proposed HCRE framework demonstrates that carefully designed hierarchical classification and verification strategies are key to fully leveraging LLMs for cross-document RE.

Abstract: Cross-document relation extraction (RE) aims to identify relations between the head and tail entities located in different documents. Existing approaches typically adopt the paradigm of ``\textit{Small Language Model (SLM) + Classifier}''. However, the limited language understanding ability of SLMs hinders further improvement of their performance. In this paper, we conduct a preliminary study to explore the performance of Large Language Models (LLMs) in cross-document RE. Despite their extensive parameters, our findings indicate that LLMs do not consistently surpass existing SLMs. Further analysis suggests that the underperformance is largely attributed to the challenges posed by the numerous predefined relations. To overcome this issue, we propose an LLM-based \underline{H}ierarchical \underline{C}lassification model for cross-document \underline{RE} (HCRE), which consists of two core components: 1) an LLM for relation prediction and 2) a \textit{hierarchical relation tree} derived from the predefined relation set. This tree enables the LLM to perform hierarchical classification, where the target relation is inferred level by level. Since the number of child nodes is much smaller than the size of the entire predefined relation set, the hierarchical relation tree significantly reduces the number of relation options that LLM needs to consider during inference. However, hierarchical classification introduces the risk of error propagation across levels. To mitigate this, we propose a \textit{prediction-then-verification} inference strategy that improves prediction reliability through multi-view verification at each level. Extensive experiments show that HCRE outperforms existing baselines, validating its effectiveness.

### [29] [Large Language Model Post-Training: A Unified View of Off-Policy and On-Policy Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07941)
*Shiwan Zhao,Zhihu Wang,Xuyang Zhao,Jiaming Zhou,Caiyue Xu,Chenfei Liu,Liting Zhang,Yuhang Jia,Yanzhe Zhang,Hualong Yu,Zichen Xu,Qicheng Li,Yong Qin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper is a survey proposing a unified framework to understand post-training methods for large language models as structured interventions on behavior, organized by trajectory provenance and their roles in expanding, reshaping, and consolidating model behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Post-training methods like SFT, preference optimization, RL, and distillation are critical for aligning and deploying LLMs, but they are usually discussed in a fragmented way based on method labels or objective families, making it hard to see how they relate or which behavioral problems they solve.

Method: The authors present a conceptual framework that organizes post-training along two axes: (1) trajectory provenance, distinguishing off-policy learning on externally provided trajectories from on-policy learning on model-generated rollouts, and (2) functional roles of methods, namely effective support expansion, policy reshaping, and behavioral consolidation. They then reinterpret existing paradigms (SFT, preference methods, RL, distillation, multi-stage pipelines) within this framework.

Result: Using this framework, the survey shows that: SFT can either expand behavioral support or reshape policies; preference-based methods are typically off-policy reshaping; on-policy RL mainly improves behavior on visited states but can also expand reach under strong guidance; distillation is better seen as behavioral consolidation rather than mere compression; and complex pipelines can be described as coordinated multi-stage compositions of these roles.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that understanding post-training as structured behavioral intervention clarifies bottlenecks, explains how different methods complement each other, and highlights that future progress will depend less on any single objective and more on careful, system-level design of multi-stage post-training pipelines.

Abstract: Post-training has become central to turning pretrained large language models (LLMs) into aligned and deployable systems. Recent progress spans supervised fine-tuning (SFT), preference optimization, reinforcement learning (RL), process supervision, verifier-guided methods, distillation, and multi-stage pipelines. Yet these methods are often discussed in fragmented ways, organized by labels or objective families rather than by the behavioral bottlenecks they address.
  This survey argues that LLM post-training is best understood as structured intervention on model behavior. We organize the field first by trajectory provenance, which defines two primary learning regimes: off-policy learning on externally supplied trajectories, and on-policy learning on learner-generated rollouts. We then interpret methods through two recurring roles -- effective support expansion, which makes useful behaviors more reachable, and policy reshaping, which improves behavior within already reachable regions -- together with a complementary systems-level role, behavioral consolidation, which preserves, transfers, and amortizes behavior across stages and model transitions.
  This perspective yields a unified reading of major paradigms. SFT may serve either support expansion or policy reshaping, whereas preference-based methods are usually off-policy reshaping. On-policy RL often improves behavior on learner-generated states, though under stronger guidance it can also make hard-to-reach reasoning paths reachable. Distillation is often best understood as consolidation rather than only compression, and hybrid pipelines emerge as coordinated multi-stage compositions.
  Overall, the framework helps diagnose post-training bottlenecks and reason about stage composition, suggesting that progress in LLM post-training increasingly depends on coordinated system design rather than any single dominant objective.

</details>

Conclusion: A principled treatment of domains via gradient-domain theory clarifies how domain weighting affects LLM training, and the proposed DoGraph framework shows that viewing data scheduling as a graph-constrained optimization problem is an effective and robust way to improve LLM generalization in practice.

Extensive experiments on GPT-2 models of varying scales demonstrate that DoGraph consistently achieves competitive performance.

They apply AtomEval to adversarial attacks generated by different LLMs on the FEVER fact-checking dataset.

Result: Across various adversarial attack strategies and multiple LLM generators evaluated on FEVER, AtomEval yields more faithful and reliable evaluation signals compared to standard, largely similarity-based metrics. It can systematically identify rewrites that change the factual content even when they appear superficially similar to the original claim.

Conclusion: Validity-aware, atom-level evaluation like AtomEval is necessary for correctly assessing adversarial claim quality and, by extension, the robustness of fact-checking models. The analysis also reveals that stronger LLMs do not always produce more effective adversarial claims when evaluated for factual validity, exposing limitations of current adversarial evaluation practices that ignore truth-conditional consistency.

Abstract: Adversarial claim rewriting is widely used to test fact-checking systems, but standard metrics fail to capture truth-conditional consistency and often label semantically corrupted rewrites as successful. We introduce AtomEval, a validity-aware evaluation framework that decomposes claims into subject-relation-object-modifier (SROM) atoms and scores adversarial rewrites with Atomic Validity Scoring (AVS), enabling detection of factual corruption beyond surface similarity. Experiments on the FEVER dataset across representative attack strategies and LLM generators show that AtomEval provides more reliable evaluation signals in our experiments. Using AtomEval, we further analyze LLM-based adversarial generators and observe that stronger models do not necessarily produce more effective adversarial claims under validity-aware evaluation, highlighting previously overlooked limitations in current adversarial evaluation practices.

### [32] [Kathleen: Oscillator-Based Byte-Level Text Classification Without Tokenization or Attention](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07969)
*George Fountzoulas*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Kathleen is a very small, tokenizer-free byte-level text classifier that uses frequency-domain components to achieve strong accuracy and linear-time processing.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reduce the complexity and parameter count of text classifiers by removing tokenizers and attention, while retaining or improving accuracy and scalability to long byte sequences.

Method: Design a 733K-parameter architecture that operates directly on UTF-8 bytes using three key ideas: (1) RecurrentOscillatorBanks—damped sinusoidal convolutions providing O(L) sequence processing; (2) an FFT-Rotate Wavetable Encoder that represents all 256 byte values with a single learnable vector instead of a full embedding table; (3) PhaseHarmonics—a compact sinusoidal non-linearity with only 6 learnable phase parameters. They compare to and ablate a larger 1.8M-parameter predecessor, systematically removing architectural parts to measure their impact.

Result: Kathleen-Clean, the final pared-down model, attains 88.6% accuracy on IMDB, 92.3% on AG News, and 83.3% on SST-2. It outperforms a tokenized model with 16x more parameters on IMDB (+1.6%) and AG News (+2.1%). Ablations show that complex cognitive/bio-inspired modules can be removed with minor loss (-0.2%), whereas removing the 6-parameter PhaseHarmonics hurts accuracy by -2.6%.

Conclusion: Frequency-domain and sinusoidal components can replace heavier tokenization, attention, and cognitive modules, enabling compact, linear-time, byte-level text classification models that match or surpass much larger token-based architectures, especially at long sequence lengths where Transformers are memory-limited.

Abstract: We present Kathleen, a text classification architecture that operates directly on raw UTF-8 bytes using frequency-domain processing -- requiring no tokenizer, no attention mechanism, and only 733K parameters. Kathleen introduces three novel components: (1) RecurrentOscillatorBanks -- damped sinusoid convolutions with temporal memory for O(L) sequence processing; (2) an FFT-Rotate Wavetable Encoder that maps all 256 byte values using a single learnable vector (256 floats), replacing conventional embedding tables (65K parameters) while improving accuracy; (3) PhaseHarmonics -- a sinusoidal non-linearity with just 6 learnable phase parameters that our ablation identifies as the single most impactful component (+2.6% accuracy, <0.001% of model parameters). Through comprehensive ablation of a 1.8M-parameter predecessor, we show that frequency-domain components systematically outperform complex cognitive architectures: removing a 560K-parameter bio-inspired framework costs only -0.2%, while removing the 6-parameter PhaseHarmonics costs -2.6%. The resulting Kathleen-Clean achieves 88.6% on IMDB, 92.3% on AG News, and 83.3% on SST-2 -- outperforming a tokenized counterpart with 16x more parameters on IMDB (+1.6%) and AG News (+2.1%). Kathleen processes sequences in O(L) time and memory, enabling byte-level operation at sequence lengths where O(L^2) Transformers exhaust GPU memory.

### [33] [A Decomposition Perspective to Long-context Reasoning for LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07981)
*Yanling Xiao,Huaibing Xie,Guoliang Zhao,Shihan Dou,Shaolei Wang,Yiting Liu,Nantao Zheng,Cheng Zhang,Pluto Zhou,Zhisong Zhang,Lemao Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes decomposing long-context reasoning into atomic skills, generating synthetic datasets for each skill, and using reinforcement learning on these datasets to improve LLM long-context reasoning, achieving a 7.7% average performance gain on multiple benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-context reasoning is crucial but challenging for LLMs, and prior work treats it as a monolithic ability, overlooking its internal structure. The authors aim to understand and improve long-context reasoning by breaking it into fundamental skills and directly training models on them.

Method: 1) Conceptually decompose long-context reasoning into several atomic reasoning skills. 2) Automatically synthesize pseudo datasets where each dataset isolates and targets one atomic skill. 3) Measure the correlation between a model’s performance on these atomic-skill tasks and its general long-context reasoning performance. 4) Apply reinforcement learning on the pseudo datasets to strengthen these atomic skills. 5) Evaluate the resulting models on a collection of long-context reasoning benchmarks.

Result: The study finds a strong correlation between competency in the defined atomic skills and overall long-text reasoning performance. Models trained via reinforcement learning on the synthetic atomic-skill datasets achieve an average improvement of 7.7% (from 46.3% to 54.0%) over a strong baseline on several long-context benchmarks, including Loogle, Loong, LongBench-v2, BrowscompLong, Ruler-qa2, and MRCR.

Conclusion: Decomposing long-context reasoning into targeted atomic skills and training on synthetic datasets via reinforcement learning is an effective strategy to improve LLM performance on long-context tasks. The results suggest that focusing on structured subskills can yield substantial gains in general long-text reasoning, validating the atomic-skill perspective as a useful lens for future research.

Abstract: Long-context reasoning is essential for complex real-world applications, yet remains a significant challenge for Large Language Models (LLMs). Despite the rapid evolution in long-context reasoning, current research often overlooks the internal complexity of the long-context reasoning task itself. In this paper, we move beyond this holistic view and decompose long-context reasoning into a set of fundamental atomic skills, and we then automatically synthesize a suite of pseudo datasets, each explicitly targeting a specific atomic skill. Our empirical analysis confirms that proficiency in these atomic skills is strongly correlated with general long-text reasoning performance. Building on this insight, we employ reinforcement learning on these pseudo datasets to sharpen the model's atomic skills, in the hope of boosting its general long-context reasoning ability. Extensive experiments across multiple benchmarks demonstrate the effectiveness of our approach: it outperforms a strong baseline by an average margin of 7.7\% (improving from 46.3\% to 54.0\%) across Loogle, Loong, LongBench-v2, BrowscompLong, Ruler-qa2, and MRCR.

### [34] [Rag Performance Prediction for Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07985)
*Or Dado,David Carmel. Oren Kurland*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates how to predict in advance whether using retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) will actually help question answering compared to not using retrieval.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RAG systems are not always beneficial: retrieving documents and conditioning generation on them adds cost and complexity, and sometimes even hurts answer quality. Being able to predict when RAG will be helpful would allow systems to selectively use retrieval, saving resources and potentially improving answer quality and robustness.

Method: The authors repurpose existing pre-retrieval and post-retrieval prediction methods from ad hoc retrieval to this new task and evaluate them. They also design and test several post-generation predictors that estimate the gain from RAG after seeing the generated answer. Their key contribution is a new supervised predictor that explicitly models semantic relationships among three elements: the user question, the retrieved passages, and the generated answer, and uses these features to predict the expected benefit of RAG.

Result: Among all tested approaches, the newly proposed supervised post-generation predictor that jointly encodes question, passages, and answer achieves the best prediction performance for estimating the gain of RAG over non-RAG question answering.

Conclusion: Predicting the benefit of RAG is feasible, and the most effective approach is to use a supervised model that explicitly captures semantic interactions between the question, retrieved documents, and generated answers. This opens the way to adaptive QA systems that invoke retrieval only when it is predicted to be beneficial.

Abstract: We address the task of predicting the gain of using RAG (retrieval augmented generation) for question answering with respect to not using it. We study the performance of a few pre-retrieval and post-retrieval predictors originally devised for ad hoc retrieval. We also study a few post-generation predictors, one of which is novel to this study and posts the best prediction quality. Our results show that the most effective prediction approach is a novel supervised predictor that explicitly models the semantic relationships among the question, retrieved passages, and the generated answer.

### [35] [Guaranteeing Knowledge Integration with Joint Decoding for Retrieval-Augmented Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08046)
*Zhengyi Zhao,Shubo Zhang,Zezhong Wang,Yuxi Zhang,Huimin Wang,Yutian Zhao,Yefeng Zheng,Binyang Li,Kam-Fai Wong,Xian Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes GuarantRAG, a RAG framework that separates parametric reasoning from evidence integration and then jointly decodes them to better use external documents and reduce hallucinations in LLM-based QA.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG methods mainly optimize retrieval but ignore the integration bottleneck: LLMs often fail to properly use retrieved documents when they conflict with internal parametric knowledge, leading to hallucinations and suboptimal use of evidence.

Method: 1) Generate an Inner-Answer using only the model’s parametric knowledge to capture its reasoning trajectory. 2) Generate a Refer-Answer trained with a new Contrastive DPO objective, where the Inner-Answer acts as a negative signal and retrieved documents act as positive signals to force reliance on external evidence. 3) Use a joint decoding mechanism that fuses, at token level, the reasoning coherence of the Inner-Answer with the factual grounding of the Refer-Answer instead of simple concatenation or using the DPO model alone.

Result: On five QA benchmarks, GuarantRAG yields up to 12.1% accuracy improvement and 16.3% hallucination reduction compared with standard and dynamic RAG baselines.

Conclusion: Decoupling reasoning from evidence integration and then recombining them via contrastive training plus joint decoding more effectively exploits retrieved documents, mitigates conflicts between parametric and external knowledge, and improves both accuracy and faithfulness in RAG-based QA systems.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) significantly enhances Large Language Models (LLMs) by providing access to external knowledge. However, current research primarily focuses on retrieval quality, often overlooking the critical ''integration bottleneck'': even when relevant documents are retrieved, LLMs frequently fail to utilize them effectively due to conflicts with their internal parametric knowledge. In this paper, we argue that implicitly resolving this conflict in a single generation pass is suboptimal. We introduce GuarantRAG, a framework that explicitly decouples reasoning from evidence integration. First, we generate an ''Inner-Answer'' based solely on parametric knowledge to capture the model's reasoning flow. Second, to guarantee faithful evidence extraction, we generate a ''Refer-Answer'' using a novel Contrastive DPO objective. This objective treats the parametric Inner-Answer as a negative constraint and the retrieved documents as positive ground truth, forcing the model to suppress internal hallucinations in favor of external evidence during this phase. Finally, rather than naive concatenation or using the DPO trained model directly, we propose a joint decoding mechanism that dynamically fuses the logical coherence of the Inner-Answer with the factual precision of the Refer-Answer at the token level. Experiments on five QA benchmarks demonstrate that GuarantRAG improves accuracy by up to 12.1% and reduces hallucinations by 16.3% compared to standard and dynamic RAG baselines.

</details>


### [36] [Efficient Provably Secure Linguistic Steganography via Range Coding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08052)
*Ruiyi Yan,Yugo Murawaki*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a provably secure linguistic steganography method using range coding and a rotation mechanism, achieving near-100% entropy utilization and high embedding speed in language models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing provably secure linguistic steganography methods achieve perfect imperceptibility (zero KL divergence) but suffer from low embedding capacity, limiting practical usability. There is a need for a steganographic scheme that maintains provable security while significantly improving capacity and efficiency when used with modern language models.

Method: The authors directly apply a classical entropy coding technique—range coding—to the problem of linguistic steganography. They integrate range coding with language-model-based generation, and introduce a rotation mechanism to distribute embedding operations over different parts of the model or token sequence. This design aims to fully exploit the entropy offered by the language model while preserving the statistical properties required for provable security. The scheme is analyzed in terms of KL divergence and embedding efficiency and implemented over various language models (e.g., GPT-2).

Result: Across multiple language models, the proposed method achieves approximately 100% entropy utilization (i.e., embedding efficiency close to the theoretical maximum) and substantially higher embedding capacity than existing baselines. It also achieves high embedding speeds, up to 1554.66 bits per second on GPT-2, demonstrating its practical viability.

Conclusion: By combining range coding with a rotation mechanism, the paper presents an efficient linguistic steganography method that is both provably secure and capacity-efficient. It overcomes the traditional trade-off between security and embedding capacity, making language-model-based steganography more practical for real-world covert communication scenarios.

Abstract: Linguistic steganography involves embedding secret messages within seemingly innocuous texts to enable covert communication. Provable security, which is a long-standing goal and key motivation, has been extended to language-model-based steganography. Previous provably secure approaches have achieved perfect imperceptibility, measured by zero Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence, but at the expense of embedding capacity. In this paper, we attempt to directly use a classic entropy coding method (range coding) to achieve secure steganography, and then propose an efficient and provably secure linguistic steganographic method with a rotation mechanism. Experiments across various language models show that our method achieves around 100% entropy utilization (embedding efficiency) for embedding capacity, outperforming the existing baseline methods. Moreover, it achieves high embedding speeds (up to 1554.66 bits/s on GPT-2). The code is available at github.com/ryehr/RRC_steganography.

### [37] [Dual-Pool Token-Budget Routing for Cost-Efficient and Reliable LLM Serving](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08075)
*Xunzhuo Liu,Bowei He,Xue Liu,Andy Luo,Haichen Zhang,Huamin Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a simple routing mechanism that splits a vLLM fleet into two pools (short-context and long-context) and routes requests by estimated token budget, greatly reducing KV-cache waste and GPU cost while improving reliability and latency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current production vLLM fleets are provisioned for worst-case long context, causing large KV-cache over-allocation. Since most requests are actually short, this leads to 4–8× throughput loss, OOM crashes, preemptions, and request drops. The authors want a lightweight, practical way to better match system configuration to actual traffic without complex changes or model-specific tools.

Method: They introduce dual-pool token-budget routing, which partitions a homogeneous GPU fleet into two logical pools: a high-throughput pool for short-context requests and a high-capacity pool for long-context ones. Each incoming request is routed based on an estimated total token budget, computed via a per-category bytes-to-token ratio. This ratio is learned online using exponential moving averages from usage.prompt_tokens feedback, avoiding running a tokenizer. They additionally build a simple analytical model that predicts cost savings from fleet-level throughput measurements and workload statistics, allowing pre-deployment benefit estimation. The method is designed to have O(1) dispatch overhead and to compose with existing optimizations like PagedAttention, continuous batching, and prefill-decode disaggregation.

Result: On real-world traces (Azure LLM Inference Dataset, LMSYS-Chat-1M) with Llama-3-70B on A100 GPUs, the approach reduces GPU-hours by 31–42%, implying about $2.86M yearly savings at fleet scale, cuts preemption rates by 5.4×, and improves P99 time-to-first-token (TTFT) by 6%. A case study with Qwen3-235B-A22B on AMD MI300X at 10,000 requests/s projects $15.4M annual savings. The system maintains low dispatch overhead and handles heterogeneous workloads effectively.

Conclusion: Configuration-traffic mismatch is a central cause of inefficiency in production vLLM fleets. A simple dual-pool architecture with token-budget-based routing can substantially improve utilization, reduce costs, and increase reliability without requiring tokenization or invasive system changes. The mechanism is lightweight, adaptive, and compatible with state-of-the-art inference optimizations, making it practical for deployment at large scale.

Abstract: Production vLLM fleets typically provision each instance for the worst-case context length, leading to substantial KV-cache over-allocation and under-utilized concurrency. In practice, 80-95% of requests are short, yet are served under configurations optimized for long contexts, wasting 4-8$\times$ throughput capacity and triggering reliability issues such as OOM crashes, preemption, and request rejections. We identify a common root cause for these inefficiencies: configuration-traffic mismatch. We propose dual-pool token-budget routing, a lightweight dispatch mechanism that partitions a homogeneous fleet into two specialized pools: a high-throughput short-context pool and a high-capacity long-context pool. Each request is routed based on its estimated total token budget, computed using a per-category bytes-to-token ratio that is learned online via exponential moving average from usage.prompt_tokens feedback, eliminating the need for a tokenizer. We also develop a simple analytical model that predicts fleet-level cost savings from workload characteristics and measured throughput differences, enabling practitioners to estimate benefits prior to deployment. Evaluations on real-world traces from the Azure LLM Inference Dataset and LMSYS-Chat-1M, serving Llama-3-70B on A100 GPUs, show that our approach reduces GPU-hours by 31-42%, corresponding to \$2.86M annual savings at fleet scale, while lowering preemption rates by 5.4$\times$ and improving P99 TTFT by 6%. A case study with Qwen3-235B-A22B on AMD MI300X at 10,000 req/s projects \$15.4M in annual savings. The method incurs only O(1) dispatch overhead, adapts automatically to heterogeneous workloads, and composes seamlessly with existing optimizations such as PagedAttention, continuous batching, and prefill-decode disaggregation.

</details>


### [38] [Quantum Vision Theory Applied to Audio Classification for Deepfake Speech Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08104)
*Khalid Zaman,Melike Sah,Anuwat Chaiwongyenc,Cem Direkoglu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Quantum Vision (QV) theory for audio deepfake detection, showing that transforming standard speech features into ‘information waves’ before feeding them into CNNs and ViTs significantly improves deepfake speech classification performance on ASVspoof.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Deep learning models for audio and image classification typically operate on ‘collapsed’ data representations (e.g., spectrograms or images), potentially ignoring richer structural information. Previous work showed that Quantum Vision theory, which mimics particle–wave duality by converting inputs into ‘information waves’, can enhance image classification. The motivation here is to investigate whether applying QV theory to speech representations (STFT, Mel-spectrograms, MFCC) can improve performance and robustness in audio deepfake (spoofed speech) detection, a growing security concern for speaker verification systems.

Method: The authors extend Quantum Vision (QV) theory to audio. Conventional audio features—STFT spectrograms, Mel-spectrograms, and MFCCs—are processed by a QV block that maps these ‘collapsed’ features into ‘information waves’. These transformed representations are then fed into QV-based CNNs (QV-CNN) and QV-based Vision Transformers (QV-ViT) for classification. They perform extensive experiments on the ASVspoof dataset, comparing QV-CNN and QV-ViT against standard CNN and ViT baselines, and evaluate performance using accuracy and Equal Error Rate (EER), focusing on robustness in discriminating genuine vs. spoofed speech.

Result: QV-based models (QV-CNN and QV-ViT) consistently outperform their non-QV counterparts in deepfake speech detection on the ASVspoof dataset. They achieve higher classification accuracy and improved robustness when distinguishing genuine from spoofed speech. Specifically, QV-CNN with MFCC features reaches 94.20% accuracy and 9.04% EER, while QV-CNN with Mel-spectrograms achieves the highest accuracy of 94.57%. These metrics surpass standard CNN and ViT baselines under the same experimental settings.

Conclusion: Quantum Vision theory, previously validated in image tasks, is shown to be effective for audio deepfake detection by transforming conventional speech features into information-wave representations before classification. The superior performance and robustness of QV-CNN and QV-ViT over standard models on ASVspoof suggest that QV is a promising, quantum-inspired framework for audio perception tasks. The work opens up new research directions in designing QV blocks and architectures tailored to different audio features and security-critical applications such as spoofing detection in speaker verification systems.

Abstract: We propose Quantum Vision (QV) theory as a new perspective for deep learning-based audio classification, applied to deepfake speech detection. Inspired by particle-wave duality in quantum physics, QV theory is based on the idea that data can be represented not only in its observable, collapsed form, but also as information waves. In conventional deep learning, models are trained directly on these collapsed representations, such as images. In QV theory, inputs are first transformed into information waves using a QV block, and then fed into deep learning models for classification. QV-based models improve performance in image classification compared to their non-QV counterparts. What if QV theory is applied speech spectrograms for audio classification tasks? This is the motivation and novelty of the proposed approach. In this work, Short-Time Fourier Transform (STFT), Mel-spectrograms, and Mel-Frequency Cepstral Coefficients (MFCC) of speech signals are converted into information waves using the proposed QV block and used to train QV-based Convolutional Neural Networks (QV-CNN) and QV-based Vision Transformers (QV-ViT). Extensive experiments are conducted on the ASVSpoof dataset for deepfake speech classification. The results show that QV-CNN and QV-ViT consistently outperform standard CNN and ViT models, achieving higher classification accuracy and improved robustness in distinguishing genuine and spoofed speech. Moreover, the QV-CNN model using MFCC features achieves the best overall performance on the ASVspoof dataset, with an accuracy of 94.20% and an EER of 9.04%, while the QV-CNN with Mel-spectrograms attains the highest accuracy of 94.57%. These findings demonstrate that QV theory is an effective and promising approach for audio deepfake detection and opens new directions for quantum-inspired learning in audio perception tasks.

### [39] [Initialisation Determines the Basin: Efficient Codebook Optimisation for Extreme LLM Quantization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08118)
*Ian W. Kennedy,Nafise Sadat Moosavi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies and fixes a critical initialization bottleneck in additive quantization for ultra-low-bit (especially 2-bit) LLM compression, enabling much better quality-compute trade-offs for edge deployment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Additive quantization is appealing for aggressively compressing large language models because it supports constant-time lookup-table dequantization, which is ideal for edge devices. However, at very low precision (notably 2 bits), existing approaches often fail badly despite heavy search and finetuning, limiting practical adoption. The authors want to understand why this happens and how to reliably make 2-bit AQ work.

Method: They study the optimization behavior of additive quantization through a new representational ratio metric ρ = N/(K·M), relating the number of weight groups N to codebook capacity K·M. They analyze how common greedy sequential codebook initialization leads to bad regions in the loss landscape, especially when ρ is large. To address this, they propose OA-EM, an output-aware expectation–maximization initialization procedure that uses Hessian-weighted Mahalanobis distance to better align codebooks with the model’s output geometry. They then combine OA-EM with standard search and post-quantization finetuning (PV-tuning) and evaluate on several LLM architectures under different compression and compute budgets.

Result: OA-EM initialization consistently yields better compressed models after PV-tuning across multiple LLMs (Llama 3.2 3B, Llama 3.1 8B, Qwen 2.5 3B), bitrates, and search budgets. It dominates the quality–compute frontier compared to greedy sequential initialization and other baselines. They find that the initialization bottleneck grows worse as ρ increases: it is noticeable at 3 bits per parameter and becomes severe at 2 bits, where bad initialization can worsen perplexity by orders of magnitude. OA-EM mitigates these failures and makes 2-bit AQ substantially more reliable.

Conclusion: The main limitation of current additive quantization for extreme LLM compression is not just search or finetuning but codebook initialization and the induced optimization geometry. By providing an output-aware, Hessian-informed EM initialization, the authors significantly improve low-bit AQ, especially at 2 bits, and show that careful attention to optimization geometry in the compressed parameter space is crucial. This shifts focus from just better search or tuning to better initialization strategies when compressing large models to ultra-low precision.

Abstract: Additive quantization enables extreme LLM compression with O(1) lookup-table dequantization, making it attractive for edge deployment. Yet at 2-bit precision, it often fails catastrophically, even with extensive search and finetuning. We show that the dominant bottleneck is codebook initialisation. Greedy sequential initialisation frequently places the model in poor optimisation regions that subsequent beam search and PV-tuning struggle to overcome. We analyse this behaviour through the representational ratio \r{ho} = N/KM, which characterises the relationship between weight groups and codebook capacity, and propose OA-EM, an output-aware EM initialisation method using Hessian-weighted Mahalanobis distance. Across compression rates, search budgets, and three architectures (Llama 3.2 3B, Llama 3.1 8B, Qwen 2.5 3B), OA-EM consistently produces better solutions after PV-tuning and dominates the quality-compute frontier. The severity of the bottleneck scales with \r{ho}: moderate at 3 bpp but extreme at 2 bpp, where poor initialisation can degrade perplexity by orders of magnitude. More broadly, our results highlight the importance of optimisation geometry in compressed model spaces, where initialisation can dominate subsequent search and fine-tuning.

</details>

Yet, real French OSCE annotated transcripts remain extremely scarce, limiting reproducible research and reliable benchmarking. To address these challenges, we investigate the use of LLMs for both generating and evaluating French OSCE dialogues in a low-resource context. We introduce a controlled pipeline that produces synthetic doctor-patient interview transcripts guided by scenario-specific evaluation criteria, combining ideal and perturbed performances to simulate varying student skill levels. The resulting dialogues are automatically silver-labeled through an LLM-assisted framework supporting adjustable evaluation strictness. Benchmarking multiple open-source and proprietary LLMs shows that mid-size models ($\le$32B parameters) achieve accuracies comparable to GPT-4o ($\sim$90\%) on synthetic data, highlighting the feasibility of locally deployable, privacy-preserving evaluation systems for medical education.

### [41] [Graph Neural Networks for Misinformation Detection: Performance-Efficiency Trade-offs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08131)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing misinformation detectors are becoming increasingly complex and computationally heavy (e.g., large language models, hybrid systems), raising concerns about deployment in real-world, resource-constrained settings. The authors want to know whether simpler, classic GNNs—when fairly compared—already offer strong performance-efficiency trade-offs, potentially making such complexity unnecessary.

Method: The authors design a controlled benchmark where multiple lightweight GNN architectures (GCN, GraphSAGE, GAT, ChebNet) are compared with non-graph baselines (Logistic Regression, SVM, MLP). All models receive the same TF-IDF text features so that the only difference is the use of graph structure. The benchmark spans seven public misinformation datasets in three languages (English, Indonesian, Polish). Performance is assessed primarily via F1 score, and inference time is measured to capture computational efficiency.

Result: Across all datasets, GNNs systematically outperform Logistic Regression, SVM, and MLP in terms of F1 score, despite using the same TF-IDF features. GraphSAGE in particular achieves very high F1 scores (e.g., 96.8% on Kaggle, 91.9% on WELFake, 90.5% on COVID-19), substantially exceeding MLP baselines (e.g., 73.2%, 66.8%, 74.9% on the same datasets). ChebNet also shows strong gains (e.g., 79.1% vs. 66.4% on FakeNewsNet). These performance improvements come with comparable or even lower inference times than non-graph baselines.

Conclusion: Classic GNN architectures, when given the same textual features as standard ML models, leverage relational structure to yield higher accuracy in misinformation detection without sacrificing efficiency. This suggests that increasingly complex, computationally expensive models are not strictly necessary; well-designed, lightweight GNNs can provide a strong and practical solution for real-world misinformation detection tasks across multiple languages and datasets.

Abstract: The rapid spread of online misinformation has led to increasingly complex detection models, including large language models and hybrid architectures. However, their computational cost and deployment limitations raise concerns about practical applicability. In this work, we benchmark graph neural networks (GNNs) against non-graph-based machine learning methods under controlled and comparable conditions. We evaluate lightweight GNN architectures (GCN, GraphSAGE, GAT, ChebNet) against Logistic Regression, Support Vector Machines, and Multilayer Perceptrons across seven public datasets in English, Indonesian, and Polish. All models use identical TF-IDF features to isolate the impact of relational structure. Performance is measured using F1 score, with inference time reported to assess efficiency. GNNs consistently outperform non-graph baselines across all datasets. For example, GraphSAGE achieves 96.8% F1 on Kaggle and 91.9% on WELFake, compared to 73.2% and 66.8% for MLP, respectively. On COVID-19, GraphSAGE reaches 90.5% F1 vs. 74.9%, while ChebNet attains 79.1% vs. 66.4% on FakeNewsNet. These gains are achieved with comparable or lower inference times. Overall, the results show that classic GNNs remain effective and efficient, challenging the need for increasingly complex architectures in misinformation detection.

### [43] [Training Data Size Sensitivity in Unsupervised Rhyme Recognition](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08156)
*Petr Plecháč,Artjoms Šeļa,Silvie Cinková,Mirella De Sisto,Lara Nugues,Neža Kočnik,Antonina Martynenko,Ben Nagy,Luca Giovannini,Robert Kolár*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They study how well and with how much data an unsupervised, language-independent tool (RhymeTagger) can automatically detect rhymes in poetry across seven languages, and compare it to humans and LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Rhyme is not as clear-cut as it seems: definitions are historically contingent, scholars use different classifications, and even experts disagree on whether pairs of words rhyme. This makes automatic rhyme detection challenging, particularly in a multilingual setting. The authors want to know whether an unsupervised, language-independent method can reliably recognize rhymes, how much training data it needs, and how it compares to human annotators and modern LLMs.

Method: They use RhymeTagger, an unsupervised tool that infers rhyme patterns purely from recurring endings in poetry corpora, without language-specific phonetic knowledge. They train and test it on corpora in seven languages (Czech, German, English, French, Italian, Russian, Slovene), systematically varying training size to see how data quantity affects accuracy across languages. They create a manually annotated gold subset of poems and measure inter-annotator agreement to define an upper bound of human reliability. They analyze disagreements in terms of phonetic similarity and positional distance between potential rhyme pairs. Finally, they test three large language models using a one-shot prompt-based setup and compare their rhyme judgments to RhymeTagger and human benchmarks.

Result: With enough training data, RhymeTagger reaches and then surpasses the level of consistency observed between human annotators for rhyme classification across all tested languages. Its performance is robust to cross-linguistic variation once data is sufficient. In contrast, the three tested LLMs, which lack explicit phonetic representations, perform markedly worse on rhyme detection in this setup. The analysis of human annotations shows that disagreements frequently occur when word pairs are only moderately phonetically similar or are farther apart in the poem.

Conclusion: Unsupervised, language-independent rhyme detection based on distributional repetition patterns can be highly effective across typologically different languages, provided that enough poetic data is available. Human rhyme judgments are themselves variable, so automated systems can exceed average human agreement. Current text-only LLMs are ill-suited to fine-grained rhyme recognition, likely because they do not model phonology explicitly. Reliable computational rhyme analysis in multilingual contexts appears more feasible with specialized phonology-aware tools like RhymeTagger than with general-purpose LLMs, at least in the tested conditions.

Abstract: Rhyme is deceptively intuitive: what is or is not a rhyme is constructed historically, scholars struggle with rhyme classification, and people disagree on whether two words are rhymed or not. This complicates automated rhymed recognition and evaluation, especially in multilingual context. This article investigates how much training data is needed for reliable unsupervised rhyme recognition using RhymeTagger, a language-independent tool that identifies rhymes based on repeating patterns in poetry corpora. We evaluate its performance across seven languages (Czech, German, English, French, Italian, Russian, and Slovene), examining how training size and language differences affect accuracy. To set a realistic performance benchmark, we assess inter-annotator agreement on a manually annotated subset of poems and analyze factors contributing to disagreement in expert annotations: phonetic similarity between rhyming words and their distance from each other in a poem. We also compare RhymeTagger to three large language models using a one-shot learning strategy. Our findings show that, once provided with sufficient training data, RhymeTagger consistently outperforms human agreement, while LLMs lacking phonetic representation significantly struggle with the task.

### [44] [Self-Debias: Self-correcting for Debiasing Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08243)
*Xuan Feng,Shuai Zhao,Luwei Xiao,Tianlong Gu,Bo An*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Self-Debias, a framework that lets LLMs progressively self-correct social bias during chain-of-thought reasoning by reallocating probability mass away from biased reasoning paths toward unbiased ones, using fine-grained trajectory-level optimization and online self-improvement.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs show strong reasoning abilities but their chain-of-thought rationales can embed and amplify social biases as the reasoning unfolds. Existing debiasing techniques tend to impose static constraints or rely on external interventions (e.g., filters, prompts, or post-hoc corrections) that do not actively detect and interrupt bias once it has appeared in the reasoning trajectory. This leaves a gap: models need an intrinsic, dynamic mechanism to recognize and correct bias as it propagates through multi-step reasoning, without sacrificing general reasoning performance or requiring constant external monitoring.

Method: The authors propose Self-Debias, a progressive debiasing framework. They conceptualize the model’s output probability distribution as a limited resource that can be strategically reallocated: shifting probability mass from biased heuristics to more neutral or fair reasoning paths. Technically, instead of using coarse preference optimization that penalizes entire outputs, Self-Debias defines a trajectory-level objective over chain-of-thought steps, with dynamic constraints that target biased suffixes while preserving earlier, valid prefixes. The framework can revise only the problematic continuation of a reasoning trajectory. Additionally, Self-Debias incorporates an online self-improvement module that uses consistency filtering: the model generates candidate rationales, filters them based on internal consistency and debiasing criteria, and then uses the filtered outputs as supervision signals to further refine its own debiasing behavior. This reduces reliance on large manually-annotated datasets, working effectively with about 20k labeled examples.

Result: With around 20k annotated samples, Self-Debias enables LLMs to achieve better debiasing performance compared to baselines while maintaining general reasoning ability. The framework demonstrates efficient self-correction: it can detect and revise biased suffixes in chain-of-thought reasoning without degrading valid context or requiring continuous external debiasing modules. Empirical results indicate improved fairness metrics or reduced social bias in generated rationales, along with preserved or minimally affected performance on standard reasoning tasks.

Conclusion: Self-Debias provides an intrinsic and progressive debiasing mechanism for LLMs, reframing debiasing as probabilistic resource redistribution along reasoning trajectories. By applying fine-grained trajectory-level optimization under dynamic constraints and leveraging an online self-improvement loop with consistency filtering, the method interrupts bias propagation within chain-of-thought reasoning. The approach achieves stronger debiasing with limited annotation, while maintaining general reasoning quality, suggesting a scalable path to more self-correcting and fair LLMs.

Abstract: Although Large Language Models (LLMs) demonstrate remarkable reasoning capabilities, inherent social biases often cascade throughout the Chain-of-Thought (CoT) process, leading to continuous "Bias Propagation". Existing debiasing methods primarily focus on static constraints or external interventions, failing to identify and interrupt this propagation once triggered. To address this limitation, we introduce Self-Debias, a progressive framework designed to instill intrinsic self-correction capabilities. Specifically, we reformulate the debiasing process as a strategic resource redistribution problem, treating the model's output probability mass as a limited resource to be reallocated from biased heuristics to unbiased reasoning paths. Unlike standard preference optimization which applies broad penalties, Self-Debias employs a fine-grained trajectory-level objective subject to dynamic debiasing constraints. This enables the model to selectively revise biased reasoning suffixes while preserving valid contextual prefixes. Furthermore, we integrate an online self-improvement mechanism utilizing consistency filtering to autonomously synthesize supervision signals. With merely 20k annotated samples, Self-Debias activates efficient self-correction, achieving superior debiasing performance while preserving general reasoning capabilities without continuous external oversight.

### [45] [HyperMem: Hypergraph Memory for Long-Term Conversations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08256)
*Juwei Yue,Chuanrui Hu,Jiawei Sheng,Zuyi Zhou,Wenyuan Zhang,Tingwen Liu,Li Guo,Yafeng Deng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: HyperMem is a hypergraph-based hierarchical memory system for conversational agents that captures high-order associations to improve long-term conversational coherence and retrieval accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing long-term memory methods for conversational agents, such as RAG and graph-based memory, mainly model pairwise relations and thus fail to capture high-order associations among multiple dialogue elements. This leads to fragmented retrieval and poor coherence in long conversations, especially when multiple related topics, episodes, or facts should be jointly considered. There is a need for a memory architecture that can explicitly represent and retrieve such complex associations efficiently.

Method: The authors propose HyperMem, a hypergraph-based hierarchical memory architecture. Memory is organized into three levels: topics, episodes, and facts. Hyperedges are used to group related episodes and their facts into coherent units, explicitly modeling high-order associations. On top of this structure, they build a hybrid lexical-semantic index and design a coarse-to-fine retrieval strategy: first performing coarse retrieval over the structured memory and then refining results, enabling accurate and efficient access to related content across the hierarchy.

Result: On the LoCoMo benchmark for long-term conversation evaluation, HyperMem achieves state-of-the-art performance, reaching 92.73% accuracy as measured by an LLM-as-a-judge metric. This indicates that HyperMem significantly improves the quality and correctness of retrieved information and responses in extended dialogues compared to prior approaches.

Conclusion: HyperMem’s hypergraph-based hierarchical memory effectively captures high-order associations among conversational elements, overcoming the limitations of pairwise-relation models like standard RAG or graph memories. Its structure and retrieval strategy lead to better coherence and accuracy in long-term dialogues, making it a strong solution for long-term memory in conversational agents.

Abstract: Long-term memory is essential for conversational agents to maintain coherence, track persistent tasks, and provide personalized interactions across extended dialogues. However, existing approaches as Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) and graph-based memory mostly rely on pairwise relations, which can hardly capture high-order associations, i.e., joint dependencies among multiple elements, causing fragmented retrieval. To this end, we propose HyperMem, a hypergraph-based hierarchical memory architecture that explicitly models such associations using hyperedges. Particularly, HyperMem structures memory into three levels: topics, episodes, and facts, and groups related episodes and their facts via hyperedges, unifying scattered content into coherent units. Leveraging this structure, we design a hybrid lexical-semantic index and a coarse-to-fine retrieval strategy, supporting accurate and efficient retrieval of high-order associations. Experiments on the LoCoMo benchmark show that HyperMem achieves state-of-the-art performance with 92.73% LLM-as-a-judge accuracy, demonstrating the effectiveness of HyperMem for long-term conversations.

### [46] [Behavior-Aware Item Modeling via Dynamic Procedural Solution Representations for Knowledge Tracing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08260)
*Jun Seo,Sangwon Ryu,Heejin Do,Hyounghun Kim,Gary Geunbae Lee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They improve knowledge tracing by adding behavior-aware item representations that capture procedural problem-solving stages using a reasoning LLM, and adaptively route these stages per learner, leading to better performance, especially with repeated interactions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing knowledge tracing methods focus on static item/skill representations and ignore the procedural, step-by-step dynamics of how students solve problems. However, learning and performance are strongly affected by the problem-solving process, not just final correctness. The authors want to incorporate rich procedural solution information into item modeling to better reflect how different learners engage with different steps of a solution.

Method: They propose BAIM (Behavior-Aware Item Modeling). First, a reasoning language model decomposes each item's solution into four Polya-inspired stages: understand, plan, carry out, and look back. For each stage, they construct per-stage embedding trajectories, then derive stage-level item representations that capture latent procedural signals beyond simple surface features. These stage-wise item embeddings are then fed into a KT backbone via a context-conditioned routing mechanism that adaptively emphasizes different stages for different learners and contexts, reflecting learner heterogeneity in which parts of the solution process matter most.

Result: On two large-scale datasets (XES3G5M and NIPS34), BAIM consistently outperforms strong pretraining-based KT baselines. The performance gains are especially notable when learners have repeated interactions with items, suggesting that the behavior-aware, procedural item modeling particularly benefits settings where student-item interactions are rich and iterative.

Conclusion: Modeling procedural solution stages and integrating them into item representations improves knowledge tracing. By leveraging a reasoning LLM to decompose solutions and a context-conditioned routing mechanism to adaptively weight stages per learner, BAIM captures behavior-aware information that leads to more accurate predictions of future performance, particularly in scenarios with repeated learner interactions.

Abstract: Knowledge Tracing (KT) aims to predict learners' future performance from past interactions. While recent KT approaches have improved via learning item representations aligned with Knowledge Components, they overlook the procedural dynamics of problem solving. We propose Behavior-Aware Item Modeling (BAIM), a framework that enriches item representations by integrating dynamic procedural solution information. BAIM leverages a reasoning language model to decompose each item's solution into four problem-solving stages (i.e., understand, plan, carry out, and look back), pedagogically grounded in Polya's framework. Specifically, it derives stage-level representations from per-stage embedding trajectories, capturing latent signals beyond surface features. To reflect learner heterogeneity, BAIM adaptively routes these stage-wise representations, introducing a context-conditioned mechanism within a KT backbone, allowing different procedural stages to be emphasized for different learners. Experiments on XES3G5M and NIPS34 show that BAIM consistently outperforms strong pretraining-based baselines, achieving particularly large gains under repeated learner interactions.

*Prisca Piccirilli,Alexander Fraser,Sabine Schulte im Walde*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Large-scale corpus study compares metaphorical vs. literal verb–object uses using rich linguistic and cognitive features, finding heterogeneous, construction-specific differences rather than a single global pattern.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Metaphors are central to everyday language, but most prior work on metaphors is small-scale, experimental, or theoretical. There is little systematic, corpus-based comparison between metaphorical and literal uses of near-synonymous expressions (e.g., float an idea vs. suggest an idea). The authors aim to fill this gap by quantifying how metaphorical and literal usages differ across many contexts using modern NLP tools.

Method: The authors collect ~2 million English corpus sentences containing 297 near-synonymous verb–object pairs that vary in metaphoricity. They use five NLP tools to automatically derive 2,293 features per instance, covering affective properties (e.g., valence, arousal), lexical statistics (frequency, diversity), syntactic structure, cohesion, and discourse-level characteristics. They then conduct two analyses: (i) cross-pair, comparing the aggregate feature profiles of metaphorical vs. literal usages across all pairs; and (ii) within-pair, examining how each verb–object pair’s metaphorical and literal uses differ from each other.

Result: Cross-pair analyses show that literal contexts tend to involve more frequent vocabulary, greater lexical cohesion, and more regular syntactic structures. Metaphorical contexts, by contrast, are more affectively charged, more imageable, more lexically diverse, and more tied to specific constructions. Within-pair analyses, however, reveal strong heterogeneity: most verb–object pairs do not follow a uniform pattern, and the direction and magnitude of metaphor–literal contrasts vary substantially across pairs.

Conclusion: There is no single, universal distributional signature that cleanly separates metaphorical from literal language use in verb–object constructions. Instead, metaphor–literal differences are strongly construction-specific and heterogeneous. Rich, large-scale corpus analyses using diverse cognitive and linguistic features are thus necessary to capture the nuanced ways in which metaphorical and literal meanings are realized in actual language use.

Abstract: Metaphor pervades everyday language, allowing speakers to express abstract concepts via concrete domains. While prior work has studied metaphors cognitively and psycholinguistically, large-scale comparisons with literal language remain limited, especially for near-synonymous expressions. We analyze 297 English verb-object pairs (e.g., float idea vs. suggest idea) in ~2M corpus sentences, examining their contextual usage. Using five NLP tools, we extract 2,293 cognitive and linguistic features capturing affective, lexical, syntactic, and discourse-level properties. We address: (i) whether features differ between metaphorical and literal contexts (cross-pair analysis), and (ii) whether individual VO pairs diverge internally (within-pair analysis). Cross-pair results show literal contexts have higher lexical frequency, cohesion, and structural regularity, while metaphorical contexts show greater affective load, imageability, lexical diversity, and constructional specificity. Within-pair analyses reveal substantial heterogeneity, with most pairs showing non-uniform effects. These results suggest no single, consistent distributional pattern that distinguishes metaphorical from literal usage. Instead, differences are largely construction-specific. Overall, large-scale data combined with diverse features provides a fine-grained understanding of metaphor-literal contrasts in VO usage.

### [48] [When to Trust Tools? Adaptive Tool Trust Calibration For Tool-Integrated Math Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08281)
*Ruotao Xu,Yixin Ji,Yu Luo,Jinpeng Li,Dong Li,Peifeng Li,Juntao Li,Min Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a method to make tool-integrated reasoning models better decide when to trust external tools versus their own reasoning, reducing cases where correct tool outputs are ignored and improving performance by 4–8%.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large reasoning models improved by scaling test-time computation still struggle with tasks requiring precise calculations and rich knowledge, even when equipped with external tools. Existing tool-integrated reasoning (TIR) models show a problematic behavior: when there is a conflict between their internal reasoning and tool outputs, they often over-trust themselves, ignoring correct tool results—a failure mode termed “Tool Ignored.” This reveals a gap: current models lack a calibrated mechanism to decide when to rely on tools versus internal reasoning. The paper aims to systematically identify this issue and provide a general solution that can be applied across different TIR models and datasets.

Method: They first analyze existing open-source TIR models to characterize the “Tool Ignored” phenomenon—cases where models disregard correct tool outputs and instead stick with their (wrong) internal reasoning. To address this, they propose Adaptive Tool Trust Calibration (ATTC), a framework that computes a confidence score for generated code blocks or tool calls. Based on this confidence, ATTC adaptively adjusts how much the model should trust or ignore the tool outputs when integrating them into the final reasoning chain. This calibration process is applied at inference time, without fundamentally changing the underlying TIR models, and is evaluated across multiple model sizes and benchmarks.

Result: Across several open-source TIR models of various sizes and on multiple datasets, applying ATTC substantially reduces the frequency of “Tool Ignored” cases. Empirically, this leads to consistent performance gains, with improvements reported in the range of 4.1% to 7.5% over baselines without ATTC. These results suggest that better trust calibration between internal reasoning and tools directly translates into higher task accuracy.

Conclusion: The work shows that a key bottleneck for tool-integrated reasoning systems is not just having access to tools, but deciding when to rely on them. The identified “Tool Ignored” failure mode highlights that models often over-trust their own reasoning even against correct tool outputs. The proposed ATTC framework, by adaptively calibrating trust based on confidence scores of tool-related code, mitigates this issue and yields substantial performance gains. This suggests that future progress in TIR should focus on principled trust and confidence modeling between the base model and external tools, and that ATTC offers a practical, model-agnostic step in that direction.

Abstract: Large reasoning models (LRMs) have achieved strong performance enhancement through scaling test time computation, but due to the inherent limitations of the underlying language models, they still have shortcomings in tasks that require precise computation and extensive knowledge reserves. Tool-Integrated Reasoning (TIR) has emerged as a promising paradigm that incorporates tool call and execution within the reasoning trajectory. Although recent works have released some powerful open-source TIR models, our analysis reveals that these models still suffer from critical deficiencies. We find that when the reasoning of the model conflicts with the tool results, the model tends to believe in its own reasoning. And there are cases where the tool results are correct but are ignored by the model, resulting in incorrect answers, which we define as "Tool Ignored''. This indicates that the model does not know when to trust or ignore the tool. To overcome these limitations, We introduce Adaptive Tool Trust Calibration (ATTC), a novel framework that guides the model to adaptively choose to trust or ignore the tool results based on the confidence score of generated code blocks. The experimental results from various open-source TIR models of different sizes and across multiple datasets demonstrate that ATTC effectively reduces the "Tool Ignored" issue, resulting in a performance increase of 4.1% to 7.5%.

### [49] [Distributed Multi-Layer Editing for Rule-Level Knowledge in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08284)
*Yating Wang,Wenting Zhao,Yaqi Zhao,Yongshun Gong,Yilong Yin,Haoliang Sun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models store and can be edited at the level of general rules (not just atomic facts), shows that rule knowledge is distributed across layers in different forms (formulas, descriptions, instances), and proposes a multi-layer editing method (DMLE) that significantly improves rule-level editing performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing model editing techniques mostly target isolated factual statements and assume that an edit can be done via a localized change in a single or contiguous set of layers. However, large language models also encode abstract rules that must be consistently reflected across symbolic expressions, natural language descriptions, and concrete examples. Rule-level edits require maintaining cross-form consistency, which current methods fail to achieve. The authors are motivated to understand where and how rule knowledge is stored mechanistically and to design editing methods that respect this structure.

Method: 1) Extend the RuleEdit benchmark from 80 to 200 manually validated rules in mathematics and physics, each with multiple forms (formulas, descriptions, instances). 2) Perform fine-grained causal tracing across transformer layers of several LLMs (GPT-J-6B, Qwen2.5-7B, Qwen2-7B, LLaMA-3-8B) to identify where different forms of a rule are represented. 3) Analyze the layer-wise organization and localization patterns of rule knowledge. 4) Based on these findings, propose Distributed Multi-Layer Editing (DMLE), which applies one shared edit in early layers for formulas and descriptions and another edit in middle layers for instances, instead of a single-layer or contiguous-block intervention. 5) Evaluate DMLE against strong baselines on standard editing metrics and new rule-level metrics such as instance portability and rule understanding.

Result: The causal tracing experiments reveal that rule knowledge is organized in a form-specific way: formulas and natural language descriptions are predominantly encoded in early transformer layers, while concrete instances are more strongly represented in middle layers. This indicates that rule knowledge is not localized to a single layer or contiguous block. Using this insight, DMLE, which applies coordinated edits across early and middle layers, achieves much better rule-level editing, substantially improving instance portability and rule understanding (by 13.91 and 50.19 percentage points on average, respectively) over the strongest baseline across multiple LLM architectures, while remaining competitive on standard model editing benchmarks.

Conclusion: Rule-level knowledge in large language models is distributed and form-specific across layers, so the common assumption that edits can be done via a single localized intervention fails for such knowledge. Effective editing of rules requires coordinated, distributed updates that respect how formulas, descriptions, and instances are represented. The proposed DMLE method operationalizes this idea and significantly improves rule-level editing performance across several models, demonstrating that understanding the internal organization of knowledge can directly inform better editing algorithms.

Abstract: Large language models store not only isolated facts but also rules that support reasoning across symbolic expressions, natural language explanations, and concrete instances. Yet most model editing methods are built for fact-level knowledge, assuming that a target edit can be achieved through a localized intervention. This assumption does not hold for rule-level knowledge, where a single rule must remain consistent across multiple interdependent forms. We investigate this problem through a mechanistic study of rule-level knowledge editing. To support this study, we extend the RuleEdit benchmark from 80 to 200 manually verified rules spanning mathematics and physics. Fine-grained causal tracing reveals a form-specific organization of rule knowledge in transformer layers: formulas and descriptions are concentrated in earlier layers, while instances are more associated with middle layers. These results suggest that rule knowledge is not uniformly localized, and therefore cannot be reliably edited by a single-layer or contiguous-block intervention. Based on this insight, we propose Distributed Multi-Layer Editing (DMLE), which applies a shared early-layer update to formulas and descriptions and a separate middle-layer update to instances. While remaining competitive on standard editing metrics, DMLE achieves substantially stronger rule-level editing performance. On average, it improves instance portability and rule understanding by 13.91 and 50.19 percentage points, respectively, over the strongest baseline across GPT-J-6B, Qwen2.5-7B, Qwen2-7B, and LLaMA-3-8B. The code is available at https://github.com/Pepper66/DMLE.
