### [50] [SeLaR: Selective Latent Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08299)
*Renyu Fu,Guibo Luo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SeLaR, a training-free method that improves Chain-of-Thought reasoning by selectively applying latent (soft) reasoning only at uncertain steps and regularizing it to avoid collapse to a single token.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Chain-of-Thought reasoning in LLMs is limited by discrete token sampling, which lacks expressiveness. Latent reasoning with soft embeddings or hidden states has been proposed, but existing methods perturb confident steps and rapidly collapse to the top token, reducing stability and exploration. The paper aims to enhance reasoning performance while keeping methods lightweight and training-free.

Method: The authors propose SeLaR (Selective Latent Reasoning), which uses an entropy-gated mechanism to activate soft embeddings only when the model is uncertain (low-confidence, high-entropy token distributions) while keeping standard discrete decoding when confidence is high. They further introduce an entropy-aware contrastive regularization term that repels the soft embedding from the direction of the highest-probability token, thus maintaining diversity in the latent reasoning space. The overall framework is applied at inference time without additional training.

Result: Across five reasoning benchmarks, SeLaR achieves better performance than standard Chain-of-Thought decoding and surpasses other state-of-the-art training-free latent reasoning methods, indicating both improved accuracy and robustness in reasoning tasks.

Conclusion: Selective and regularized use of latent reasoning can overcome key drawbacks of prior soft-embedding approaches. By only activating latent reasoning at uncertain steps and preventing collapse toward a single dominant token, SeLaR offers a practical, training-free path to stronger, more stable reasoning in large language models.

Abstract: Chain-of-Thought (CoT) has become a cornerstone of reasoning in large language models, yet its effectiveness is constrained by the limited expressiveness of discrete token sampling. Recent latent reasoning approaches attempt to alleviate this limitation by replacing discrete tokens with soft embeddings (probability-weighted mixtures of token embeddings) or hidden states, but they commonly suffer from two issues: (1) global activation injects perturbations into high-confidence steps, impairing reasoning stability; and (2) soft embeddings quickly collapse toward the highest-probability token, limiting exploration of alternative trajectories. To address these challenges, we propose SeLaR (Selective Latent Reasoning), a lightweight and training-free framework. SeLaR introduces an entropy-gated mechanism that activates soft embeddings only at low-confidence steps, while preserving discrete decoding at high-confidence steps. Additionally, we propose an entropy-aware contrastive regularization that pushes soft embeddings away from the dominant (highest-probability) token's direction, encouraging sustained exploration of multiple latent reasoning paths. Experiments on five reasoning benchmarks demonstrate that SeLaR consistently outperforms standard CoT and state-of-the-art training-free methods.

### [51] [Towards Real-world Human Behavior Simulation: Benchmarking Large Language Models on Long-horizon, Cross-scenario, Heterogeneous Behavior Traces](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08362)
*Jiawei Chen,Ruoxi Xu,Boxi Cao,Ruotong Pan,Yunfei Zhang,Yifei Hu,Yong Du,Tingting Gao,Yaojie Lu,Yingfei Sun,Xianpei Han,Le Sun,Xiangyu Wu,Hongyu Lin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: OmniBehavior is a benchmark built from real-world data to evaluate how well LLMs can simulate realistic, long-horizon, cross-scenario human behavior, revealing that current LLMs show strong structural biases and fail to capture individual differences and rare behaviors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing user-simulation benchmarks are limited: they focus on single, isolated scenarios, small and simple action spaces, or synthetic data. This prevents them from capturing realistic, holistic human behavior that unfolds over long time horizons and across different contexts. The authors aim to provide a more faithful, comprehensive benchmark to study and improve LLM-based user simulators.

Method: The authors construct OmniBehavior, a benchmark derived entirely from real-world user data. It is designed to combine long-horizon interactions, multiple scenarios, and heterogeneous behavior patterns in one unified framework. They use this benchmark to empirically compare how decision-making in realistic settings differs from that in isolated-scenario datasets, and then systematically evaluate state-of-the-art LLMs on their ability to simulate such behaviors. They also compare LLM-generated behaviors to authentic ones to uncover systematic discrepancies.

Result: Empirical analysis shows that datasets with isolated scenarios encourage a ‘tunnel vision’ view of behavior, while real-world decision-making depends on long-term, cross-scenario causal chains. Evaluations reveal that current LLMs perform poorly on simulating these complex, realistic behaviors, and their performance saturates even when given larger context windows. The systematic comparison between simulated and real behaviors highlights consistent patterns of deviation in LLM outputs.

Conclusion: LLMs exhibit a fundamental structural bias when simulating users: they default toward a ‘positive average person’ characterized by hyper-activity, homogenized personas, and overly optimistic or Utopian behaviors. This leads to loss of individuality and rare, long-tail behaviors. The work establishes OmniBehavior as a challenging, realistic benchmark and argues that overcoming these structural biases and better modeling diversity and long-horizon causal chains are key research directions for building high-fidelity user simulators.

Abstract: The emergence of Large Language Models (LLMs) has illuminated the potential for a general-purpose user simulator. However, existing benchmarks remain constrained to isolated scenarios, narrow action spaces, or synthetic data, failing to capture the holistic nature of authentic human behavior. To bridge this gap, we introduce OmniBehavior, the first user simulation benchmark constructed entirely from real-world data, integrating long-horizon, cross-scenario, and heterogeneous behavioral patterns into a unified framework. Based on this benchmark, we first provide empirical evidence that previous datasets with isolated scenarios suffer from tunnel vision, whereas real-world decision-making relies on long-term, cross-scenario causal chains. Extensive evaluations of state-of-the-art LLMs reveal that current models struggle to accurately simulate these complex behaviors, with performance plateauing even as context windows expand. Crucially, a systematic comparison between simulated and authentic behaviors uncovers a fundamental structural bias: LLMs tend to converge toward a positive average person, exhibiting hyper-activity, persona homogenization, and a Utopian bias. This results in the loss of individual differences and long-tail behaviors, highlighting critical directions for future high-fidelity simulation research.

### [52] [A GAN and LLM-Driven Data Augmentation Framework for Dynamic Linguistic Pattern Modeling in Chinese Sarcasm Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08381)
*Wenxian Wang,Xiaohu Luo,Junfeng Hao,Xiaoming Gu,Xingshu Chen,Zhu Wang,Haizhou Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper builds a GAN- and LLM-based data augmentation framework plus a user-aware BERT model to improve Chinese sarcasm detection, achieving SOTA F1 on a new Sina Weibo sarcasm dataset.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Chinese sarcasm detection is hindered by small, costly datasets and models that use only text, ignoring how different users have distinct, stable linguistic habits when expressing opinions and emotions. The work aims to overcome data scarcity and to leverage user-specific historical behavior to better detect subtle, implicit sarcastic cues.

Method: (1) Crawl multi-topic Sina Weibo comments with user and context information. (2) Train a GAN and use GPT-3.5 to perform data augmentation, generating additional sarcastic comments and constructing a larger dataset, SinaSarc, which includes target comments, their context, and user historical behavior. (3) Extend BERT to ingest multi-dimensional features—text, context, and user history—so the model can dynamically learn user-level linguistic patterns. (4) Evaluate this enhanced BERT model against existing SOTA sarcasm detection baselines.

Result: The proposed user-aware, augmented BERT model achieves the best performance among all compared methods on the SinaSarc dataset. It reaches F1-scores of 0.9138 for non-sarcastic and 0.9151 for sarcastic categories, surpassing prior SOTA sarcasm detectors under the same evaluation setup.

Conclusion: Incorporating user historical behavior and dynamically modeled linguistic patterns, together with GAN- and LLM-based data augmentation, significantly improves Chinese sarcasm detection. The new SinaSarc dataset and the user-aware BERT framework advance both data resources and methodological approaches in this domain, showing that user-centric, context-rich modeling is key to better sarcasm recognition.

Abstract: Sarcasm is a rhetorical device that expresses criticism or emphasizes characteristics of certain individuals or situations through exaggeration, irony, or comparison. Existing methods for Chinese sarcasm detection are constrained by limited datasets and high construction costs, and they mainly focus on textual features, overlooking user-specific linguistic patterns that shape how opinions and emotions are expressed. This paper proposes a Generative Adversarial Network (GAN) and Large Language Model (LLM)-driven data augmentation framework to dynamically model users' linguistic patterns for enhanced Chinese sarcasm detection. First, we collect raw data from various topics on Sina Weibo. Then, we train a GAN on these data and apply a GPT-3.5 based data augmentation technique to synthesize an extended sarcastic comment dataset, named SinaSarc. This dataset contains target comments, contextual information, and user historical behavior. Finally, we extend the BERT architecture to incorporate multi-dimensional information, particularly user historical behavior, enabling the model to capture dynamic linguistic patterns and uncover implicit sarcastic cues in comments. Experimental results demonstrate the effectiveness of our proposed method. Specifically, our model achieves the highest F1-scores on both the non-sarcastic and sarcastic categories, with values of 0.9138 and 0.9151 respectively, which outperforms all existing state-of-the-art (SOTA) approaches. This study presents a novel framework for dynamically modeling users' long-term linguistic patterns in Chinese sarcasm detection, contributing to both dataset construction and methodological advancement in this field.

</details>


### [53] [Synthetic Data for any Differentiable Target](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08423)
*Tristan Thrush,Sung Min Park,Herman Brunborg,Luke Bailey,Marcel Roed,Neil Band,Christopher Potts,Tatsunori Hashimoto*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Dataset Policy Gradient (DPG), a reinforcement learning method to optimize synthetic data generators so that supervised fine-tuning on their outputs steers a target language model toward desired differentiable properties.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand and extend how far synthetic training data alone can be used to control and steer language models, beyond prompt engineering and standard fine-tuning, by directly optimizing the data generator to induce specific internal and behavioral properties in a target model.

Method: They formulate synthetic data generation as a policy in an RL setting and develop Dataset Policy Gradient (DPG), which uses exact data attribution via higher-order gradients to compute how each generated example influences a differentiable objective on the target model. These influence scores are treated as rewards in a policy gradient update of the generator, approximating the intractable true gradient of the generator with respect to the final objective.

Result: Using DPG-optimized synthetic datasets for supervised fine-tuning, they successfully steer a target model’s LM head to embed a QR code, embed a numeric pattern ("67"), reduce its L2 norm, and make the generator produce outputs that rephrase inputs in a new language or include a specific UUID, despite these targets not being specified in the prompts.

Conclusion: DPG can precisely and flexibly shape both internal representations and output behavior of language models through carefully optimized synthetic training examples alone, indicating substantial power—and potential risks—in controlling models via data rather than direct architectural or objective modifications.

Abstract: What are the limits of controlling language models via synthetic training data? We develop a reinforcement learning (RL) primitive, the Dataset Policy Gradient (DPG), which can precisely optimize synthetic data generators to produce a dataset of targeted examples. When used for supervised fine-tuning (SFT) of a target model, these examples cause the target model to do well on a differentiable metric of our choice. Our approach achieves this by taking exact data attribution via higher-order gradients and using those scores as policy gradient rewards. We prove that this procedure closely approximates the true, intractable gradient for the synthetic data generator. To illustrate the potential of DPG, we show that, using only SFT on generated examples, we can cause the target model's LM head weights to (1) embed a QR code, (2) embed the pattern $\texttt{67}$, and (3) have lower $\ell^2$ norm. We additionally show that we can cause the generator to (4) rephrase inputs in a new language and (5) produce a specific UUID, even though neither of these objectives is conveyed in the generator's input prompts. These findings suggest that DPG is a powerful and flexible technique for shaping model properties using only synthetic training examples.

</details>

*Lilian Wanzare,Cynthia Amol,zekiel Maina,Nelson Odhiambo,Hope Kerubo,Leila Misula,Vivian Oloo,Rennish Mboya,Edwin Onkoba,Edward Ombui,Joseph Muguro,Ciira wa Maina,Andrew Kipkebut,Alfred Omondi Otom,Ian Ndung'u Kang'ethe,Angela Wambui Kanyi,Brian Gichana Omwenga*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a significant lack of high-quality speech resources for African languages, which limits the development of automatic speech recognition (ASR), text-to-speech (TTS), and other speech technologies for these communities. The authors aim to address this gap by creating a large, linguistically rich dataset for several Kenyan languages, supporting both technological inclusion and linguistic preservation.

Method: The authors collected about 3,000 hours of speech in five Kenyan languages (Dholuo, Kikuyu, Kalenjin, Maasai, Somali) from 4,777 native speakers. They used a dual data collection strategy: (1) Scripted speech (750 hours) based on compiled text corpora, translations, and generated sentences from 11 Kenya-relevant domains; (2) Spontaneous speech (2,250 hours) elicited via text and image prompts to capture natural variation and dialectal differences. A custom mobile app allowed contributors to record via smartphones. Quality control combined pre-recording automated SNR checks with post-recording human review for content and accuracy. Operational challenges typical of low-resource environments (e.g., infrastructure, device issues, trust) were mitigated using local mobilizers, partnerships, and adaptable training.

Result: The project produced AfriVoices-KE, a large-scale, multi-domain, and demographically diverse speech corpus comprising 3,000 hours across five Kenyan languages, with a substantial portion of spontaneous speech and multi-layer quality assurance. The dataset successfully captures regional and dialectal variation and demonstrates the feasibility of large-scale, phone-based collection in low-resource contexts.

Conclusion: AfriVoices-KE constitutes a foundational resource for building inclusive ASR and TTS systems in Kenyan languages and contributes to the digital preservation of Kenya’s linguistic diversity. The collection methodology and mitigation strategies for low-resource challenges provide a template for future efforts in other underrepresented languages and regions.

Abstract: AfriVoices-KE is a large-scale multilingual speech dataset comprising approximately 3,000 hours of audio across five Kenyan languages: Dholuo, Kikuyu, Kalenjin, Maasai, and Somali. The dataset includes 750 hours of scripted speech and 2,250 hours of spontaneous speech, collected from 4,777 native speakers across diverse regions and demographics. This work addresses the critical underrepresentation of African languages in speech technology by providing a high-quality, linguistically diverse resource. Data collection followed a dual methodology: scripted recordings drew from compiled text corpora, translations, and domain-specific generated sentences spanning eleven domains relevant to the Kenyan context, while unscripted speech was elicited through textual and image prompts to capture natural linguistic variation and dialectal nuances. A customized mobile application enabled contributors to record using smartphones. Quality assurance operated at multiple layers, encompassing automated signal-to-noise ratio validation prior to recording and human review for content accuracy. Though the project encountered challenges common to low-resource settings, including unreliable infrastructure, device compatibility issues, and community trust barriers, these were mitigated through local mobilizers, stakeholder partnerships, and adaptive training protocols. AfriVoices-KE provides a foundational resource for developing inclusive automatic speech recognition and text-to-speech systems, while advancing the digital preservation of Kenya's linguistic heritage.

### [55] [AI generates well-liked but templatic empathic responses](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08479)
*Emma Gueorguieva,Hongli Zhan,Jina Suh,Javier Hernandez,Tatiana Lau,Junyi Jessy Li,Desmond C. Ong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes why large language models (LLMs) are often perceived as highly empathic, finding that they rely on a highly formulaic empathy template based on recurring linguistic tactics, in contrast to more diverse human responses.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: More people are using LLMs for emotional support and often judge their responses as more empathic than human ones, but it is unclear what linguistic features drive this perception and how LLM empathy differs structurally from human empathy.

Method: The authors create a taxonomy of 10 empathic language tactics (e.g., validating feelings, paraphrasing) and use it to code and compare n = 3,265 LLM-generated responses from six models and n = 1,290 human-written responses across two studies, analyzing discourse-level patterns and the presence and ordering of tactics to discover common templates.

Result: LLM responses show strong regularities: 83–90% of LLM responses (and 60–83% in held-out data) follow a discovered empathy template defined as a structured sequence of tactics, and when a response matches the template, that template accounts for 81–92% of its content, while human-written responses use the same tactics in a more varied, less templated manner.

Conclusion: LLMs achieve their high perceived empathy largely by consistently deploying a popular, formulaic empathy template composed of specific empathic tactics, whereas human empathy expression is more diverse; this has implications for how AI-mediated emotional support is designed, evaluated, and integrated into future human–AI interactions.

Abstract: Recent research shows that greater numbers of people are turning to Large Language Models (LLMs) for emotional support, and that people rate LLM responses as more empathic than human-written responses. We suggest a reason for this success: LLMs have learned and consistently deploy a well-liked template for expressing empathy. We develop a taxonomy of 10 empathic language "tactics" that include validating someone's feelings and paraphrasing, and apply this taxonomy to characterize the language that people and LLMs produce when writing empathic responses. Across a set of 2 studies comparing a total of n = 3,265 AI-generated (by six models) and n = 1,290 human-written responses, we find that LLM responses are highly formulaic at a discourse functional level. We discovered a template -- a structured sequence of tactics -- that matches between 83--90% of LLM responses (and 60--83\% in a held out sample), and when those are matched, covers 81--92% of the response. By contrast, human-written responses are more diverse. We end with a discussion of implications for the future of AI-generated empathy.

### [56] [What do Language Models Learn and When? The Implicit Curriculum Hypothesis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08510)
*Emmy Liu,Kaiser Sun,Millicent Li,Isabelle Lee,Lindia Tjuatja,Jen-tse Huang,Graham Neubig*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how specific capabilities emerge during LLM pretraining and finds that skills appear in a consistent, compositional order across models, which can be predicted from internal representations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While scaling laws describe how overall loss improves with more compute, they do not explain *which* skills LLMs acquire and *when* during pretraining. The authors want a fine-grained understanding of capability emergence, the order in which different skills appear, and whether this order is consistent and predictable across model families and data mixtures. This matters for interpretability, capability forecasting, and targeted training.

Method: The authors propose the Implicit Curriculum Hypothesis, which posits that pretraining implicitly follows a structured, compositional curriculum. To test it, they build a benchmark of simple, composable tasks (retrieval, morphology, coreference, logical reasoning, and math). They then measure, across four LLM families ranging from 410M to 13B parameters, the “emergence point” at which each model crosses fixed accuracy thresholds on these tasks. They compute consistency of emergence ordering across model pairs, analyze how composite tasks relate to their component tasks, and derive task “function vectors” from model representations. Using these representation-based task embeddings, they predict the training trajectories of held-out compositional tasks without directly training on or evaluating them at all checkpoints.

Result: They find highly consistent orders of skill emergence across model families, with Spearman correlation ρ = 0.81 in emergence orderings for 45 model pairs. Composite tasks generally emerge after the tasks that compose them. They also show that task structure is reflected in internal representations: tasks with similar function vectors have similar training trajectories. Using the representation space learned from their task set, they can predict the full pretraining trajectories of new simple compositional tasks with high accuracy (R^2 between 0.68 and 0.84 across models).

Conclusion: Pretraining of LLMs is not an unstructured process that is only summarized by loss curves; instead, it follows an implicit, compositional curriculum where skills emerge in a consistent, predictable order. This order is shared across architectures and data mixtures and is encoded in internal representations, allowing forecasting of when new tasks will be learned. This provides evidence that LLM capabilities develop in a structured way that can be probed, modeled, and potentially guided.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) can perform remarkably complex tasks, yet the fine-grained details of how these capabilities emerge during pretraining remain poorly understood. Scaling laws on validation loss tell us how much a model improves with additional compute, but not what skills it acquires in which order. To remedy this, we propose the Implicit Curriculum Hypothesis: pretraining follows a compositional and predictable curriculum across models and data mixtures. We test this by designing a suite of simple, composable tasks spanning retrieval, morphological transformations, coreference, logical reasoning, and mathematics. Using these tasks, we track emergence points across four model families spanning sizes from 410M-13B parameters. We find that emergence orderings of when models reach fixed accuracy thresholds are strikingly consistent ($ρ= .81$ across 45 model pairs), and that composite tasks most often emerge after their component tasks. Furthermore, we find that this structure is encoded in model representations: tasks with similar function vector representations also tend to follow similar trajectories in training. By using the space of representations derived from our task set, we can effectively predict the training trajectories of simple held-out compositional tasks throughout the course of pretraining ($R^2 = .68$-$.84$ across models) without previously evaluating them. Together, these results suggest that pretraining is more structured than loss curves reveal: skills emerge in a compositional order that is consistent across models and readable from their internals.

</details>


### [57] [Cram Less to Fit More: Training Data Pruning Improves Memorization of Facts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08519)
*Jiayuan Ye,Vitaly Feldman,Kunal Talwar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models memorize factual knowledge, showing that training data can overwhelm model capacity and hurt fact accuracy, and proposes loss-based data selection to improve factual memorization efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often hallucinate or fail on knowledge-intensive tasks because they cannot reliably store all needed factual knowledge in their parameters. Understanding when and how facts are memorized, and how training data design affects this, is crucial to improve factual accuracy without simply scaling models up massively.

Method: The authors give an information-theoretic formalization of fact memorization and analyze how the information content and frequency distribution of training facts interact with model capacity. They then design data selection schemes that use only the training loss to (1) limit the total number of distinct facts the model must memorize and (2) flatten the skew in fact frequency. They test these schemes on semi-synthetic high-entropy fact datasets and on pretraining GPT2-Small from scratch on an annotated Wikipedia corpus, comparing to standard training and to a much larger model.

Result: They prove that fact accuracy is necessarily below the capacity limit when the information in training facts exceeds model capacity, and this problem becomes worse under skewed (e.g., power-law) fact frequency distributions. Empirically, on semi-synthetic data their selection strategy pushes fact accuracy up to the theoretical capacity limit. On a real Wikipedia corpus, the selection method allows a 110M-parameter GPT2-Small to memorize 1.3× more entity facts than standard training, achieving similar factual performance to a 1.3B-parameter model trained on all data.

Conclusion: Training data design, not just model size, is critical for factual accuracy in LLMs. By carefully selecting and rebalancing facts based on training loss, one can align the information load with model capacity and substantially improve factual memorization, enabling smaller models to match or approach the factual performance of much larger ones.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) can struggle to memorize factual knowledge in their parameters, often leading to hallucinations and poor performance on knowledge-intensive tasks. In this paper, we formalize fact memorization from an information-theoretic perspective and study how training data distributions affect fact accuracy. We show that fact accuracy is suboptimal (below the capacity limit) whenever the amount of information contained in the training data facts exceeds model capacity. This is further exacerbated when the fact frequency distribution is skewed (e.g. a power law).
  We propose data selection schemes based on the training loss alone that aim to limit the number of facts in the training data and flatten their frequency distribution. On semi-synthetic datasets containing high-entropy facts, our selection method effectively boosts fact accuracy to the capacity limit. When pretraining language models from scratch on an annotated Wikipedia corpus, our selection method enables a GPT2-Small model (110m parameters) to memorize 1.3X more entity facts compared to standard training, matching the performance of a 10X larger model (1.3B parameters) pretrained on the full dataset.

</details>


### [58] [ClawBench: Can AI Agents Complete Everyday Online Tasks?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08523)
*Yuxuan Zhang,Yubo Wang,Yipeng Zhu,Penghui Du,Junwen Miao,Xuan Lu,Wendong Xu,Yunzhuo Hao,Songcheng Cai,Xiaochen Wang,Huaisong Zhang,Xian Wu,Yi Lu,Minyi Lei,Kai Zou,Huifeng Yin,Ping Nie,Liang Chen,Dongfu Jiang,Wenhu Chen,Kelsey R. Allen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ClawBench, a benchmark of 153 real-world online tasks across 144 live platforms to evaluate AI agents’ ability to handle everyday web-based activities, showing that current frontier models succeed on only a small fraction of tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI agent benchmarks mostly use offline, static, sandboxed web environments that fail to capture the complexity, dynamism, and multi-step nature of real-world online tasks. There is a gap between what current benchmarks test and what users actually need: AI agents that can reliably perform routine online activities such as purchasing items, booking services, and filling detailed forms across diverse live websites. The authors aim to create a realistic and challenging evaluation framework that better measures progress toward practical, general-purpose assistant agents.

Method: The authors design ClawBench as a collection of 153 standardized, simple but realistic tasks that people frequently perform online, covering 15 categories (e.g., purchases, appointments, job applications) and 144 real, production websites. They implement a lightweight interception layer that allows agents to interact with the live web but blocks the final submission request, enabling safe evaluation without real-world side effects. Tasks are structured to require capabilities such as interpreting user-provided documents, navigating multi-step workflows, handling heterogeneous UI designs, and performing extensive text entry in forms. They then evaluate seven frontier LLM-based agent systems—both proprietary and open-source—on the benchmark and measure task completion rates.

Result: On ClawBench, all evaluated models perform poorly relative to human expectations: both proprietary and open-source AI agents complete only a small subset of the 153 tasks. For instance, Claude Sonnet 4.6, one of the stronger systems tested, successfully completes only 33.3% of tasks. This indicates a substantial gap between current AI agent capabilities and the demands of real-world web task automation.

Conclusion: ClawBench reveals that current AI agents are far from being reliable, general-purpose assistants for everyday online activities. By operating on live websites and encompassing diverse, multi-step workflows, the benchmark exposes limitations that static, sandboxed evaluations miss. The authors argue that improving performance on ClawBench is a necessary step toward building agents that can safely and effectively automate routine aspects of users’ digital lives, and they position ClawBench as a more realistic testbed to drive and measure such progress.

Abstract: AI agents may be able to automate your inbox, but can they automate other routine aspects of your life? Everyday online tasks offer a realistic yet unsolved testbed for evaluating the next generation of AI agents. To this end, we introduce ClawBench, an evaluation framework of 153 simple tasks that people need to accomplish regularly in their lives and work, spanning 144 live platforms across 15 categories, from completing purchases and booking appointments to submitting job applications. These tasks require demanding capabilities beyond existing benchmarks, such as obtaining relevant information from user-provided documents, navigating multi-step workflows across diverse platforms, and write-heavy operations like filling in many detailed forms correctly. Unlike existing benchmarks that evaluate agents in offline sandboxes with static pages, ClawBench operates on production websites, preserving the full complexity, dynamic nature, and challenges of real-world web interaction. A lightweight interception layer captures and blocks only the final submission request, ensuring safe evaluation without real-world side effects. Our evaluations of 7 frontier models show that both proprietary and open-source models can complete only a small portion of these tasks. For example, Claude Sonnet 4.6 achieves only 33.3%. Progress on ClawBench brings us closer to AI agents that can function as reliable general-purpose assistants.

### [59] [Demystifying OPD: Length Inflation and Stabilization Strategies for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08527)
*Feng Luo,Yu-Neng Chuang,Guanchu Wang,Zicheng Xu,Xiaotian Han,Tianyi Zhang,Vladimir Braverman*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Identifies and fixes a key training instability in on-policy distillation for reasoning models, where rollouts suddenly become overly long and repetitive, causing truncation-dominated data and performance collapse. Proposes StableOPD, which constrains divergence from a reference and mixes rollouts to prevent this, yielding more stable training and better math reasoning performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: On-policy distillation is attractive because it trains student models on their own generated data while using stronger teacher supervision, aligning training and deployment distributions. However, the authors observe a critical and underexplored failure mode: during training, the student’s on-policy rollouts can suddenly become very long and repetitive, leading to many truncated trajectories that dominate the training set. This truncation collapse not only destabilizes optimization but also severely harms validation performance, especially in math reasoning tasks. There is a need to understand why this happens, how student-induced data collection interacts with the distillation objective to create a bias toward long, repetitive rollouts, and how to design a more stable on-policy distillation method that avoids this pathology and improves downstream reasoning quality.

Method: The paper first empirically characterizes a failure mode in standard on-policy distillation: rollouts experience abrupt length inflation, with repetitive content leading to saturation and widespread truncation, which in turn yields biased gradients and unstable training. They analyze this as a consequence of the feedback loop between the student’s own-induced data distribution and the distillation loss that implicitly rewards long, repetitive sequences. To solve this, they introduce StableOPD, a modified OPD framework that has two main components: (1) a reference-based divergence constraint, which regularizes the student’s policy to remain close to a reference model and counteracts drift toward excessively long, repetitive behaviors; and (2) rollout mixture distillation, which blends different types of rollouts (e.g., from the reference, teacher, and/or student) during training so that the data distribution is less dominated by pathological long trajectories. Together, these design choices are aimed at mitigating repetition-driven length inflation and stabilizing gradient signals.

Result: In experiments on multiple math reasoning datasets, the authors show that standard on-policy distillation suffers from truncation collapse, characterized by sudden spikes in rollout length, high repetition, and severe drops in validation performance. In contrast, StableOPD effectively prevents this collapse, leading to smoother and more stable training dynamics. Quantitatively, their stabilized framework improves performance by an average of 7.2% across the evaluated math reasoning benchmarks compared to baseline OPD setups.

Conclusion: The study concludes that a key instability in on-policy distillation arises from the interaction between student-generated data and a distillation objective that implicitly encourages long, repetitive rollouts, leading to truncation-dominated training and performance degradation. By incorporating a reference-based divergence constraint and rollout mixture distillation, StableOPD successfully mitigates this truncation collapse, yielding more stable optimization and substantial gains in math reasoning tasks. This suggests that carefully controlling the student’s distribution and diversifying training rollouts are crucial for robust, high-performance on-policy distillation.

Abstract: On-policy distillation (OPD) trains student models under their own induced distribution while leveraging supervision from stronger teachers. We identify a failure mode of OPD: as training progresses, on-policy rollouts can undergo abrupt length inflation, causing truncated trajectories to dominate the training data. This truncation collapse coincides with abrupt repetition saturation and induces biased gradient signals, leading to severe training instability and sharp degradation in validation performance. We attribute this problem to the interaction between student-induced data collection and the distillation objective, which implicitly favors long and repetitive rollouts. To address this issue, we propose StableOPD, a stabilized OPD framework that combines a reference-based divergence constraint with rollout mixture distillation. These together mitigate repetition-induced length inflation and further stabilize OPD training. Across multiple math reasoning datasets, our approach prevents truncation collapse, stabilizes training dynamics, and improves performance by 7.2% on average.

</details>

### [60] [An Analysis of Artificial Intelligence Adoption in NIH-Funded Research](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07424)
*Navapat Nananukul,Mayank Kejriwal*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper uses a human-in-the-loop large language model pipeline to classify and summarize nearly 60,000 NIH projects, quantifying how much of the portfolio involves AI, where it sits along the research-to-deployment pipeline, and how much addresses health disparities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: NIH and policymakers need a clear, quantitative view of where and how AI/ML is being used in funded biomedical research to guide funding strategy, institutional planning, and health equity policy. Traditional manual portfolio analysis cannot scale to tens of thousands of unstructured project descriptions, especially after the rapid expansion of AI and LLMs.

Method: The authors develop and apply a human-in-the-loop methodology using large language models to automatically classify and summarize NIH project abstracts at scale. LLMs perform semantic extraction and categorization of project descriptions (e.g., AI vs. non-AI, research stage from discovery to clinical deployment, focus on health disparities), with humans providing oversight, validation, and refinement. They apply this pipeline to 58,746 NIH-funded biomedical research projects from 2025.

Result: From the analyzed 2025 NIH portfolio, the authors find: (1) AI-related projects account for 15.9% of all projects and receive 13.4% more funding on average than non-AI projects, with a concentration in discovery, prediction, and data integration tasks across diseases; (2) 79% of AI projects are in research and development stages, while only 14.7% focus on clinical deployment or implementation, indicating a substantial gap between research and real-world use; (3) only 5.7% of AI projects focus on health disparities, revealing significant underinvestment in equity-focused AI research relative to NIH’s stated priorities.

Conclusion: LLM-based, human-in-the-loop portfolio analysis can effectively map AI adoption across a large biomedical funding agency. The current NIH AI portfolio is sizable and relatively well funded but heavily skewed toward early-stage research and insufficiently focused on health disparities. The authors argue that their framework can support evidence-based policy and funding interventions to better align AI investments with health equity and strategic priorities at NIH.

Abstract: Understanding the landscape of artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning (ML) adoption across the National Institutes of Health (NIH) portfolio is critical for research funding strategy, institutional planning, and health policy. The advent of large language models (LLMs) has fundamentally transformed research landscape analysis, enabling researchers to perform large-scale semantic extraction from thousands of unstructured research documents. In this paper, we illustrate a human-in-the-loop research methodology for LLMs to automatically classify and summarize research descriptions at scale. Using our methodology, we present a comprehensive analysis of 58,746 NIH-funded biomedical research projects from 2025. We show that: (1) AI constitutes 15.9% of the NIH portfolio with a 13.4% funding premium, concentrated in discovery, prediction, and data integration across disease domains; (2) a critical research-to-deployment gap exists, with 79% of AI projects remaining in research/development stages while only 14.7% engage in clinical deployment or implementation; and (3) health disparities research is severely underrepresented at just 5.7% of AI-funded work despite its importance to NIH's equity mission. These findings establish a framework for evidence-based policy interventions to align the NIH AI portfolio with health equity goals and strategic research priorities.

### [61] [Munkres' General Topology Autoformalized in Isabelle/HOL](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07455)
*Dustin Bryant,Jonathan Julián Huerta y Munive,Cezary Kaliszyk,Josef Urban*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: An experiment shows that large language models can efficiently assist in fully formalizing a whole standard topology textbook (Munkres, Chapters 2–8) in Isabelle/HOL, achieving 85k+ lines of code and 800+ proved results with modest human supervision.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Formalizing entire mainstream math textbooks in proof assistants is highly labor-intensive and slow using traditional workflows. The authors want to see whether modern LLMs can substantially accelerate this process and make large-scale, fully-verified formalizations of standard mathematical material feasible in practice.

Method: They run LLM-based coding agents (first ChatGPT 5.2, then Claude Opus 4.6) in an interactive loop with Isabelle/HOL, using a “sorry-first” workflow: first generate declarative proof skeletons with unfinished parts (sorry), then iteratively replace them with real proofs, leveraging Isabelle’s sledgehammer automation heavily. Over 24 active days, they guide the agents through all 39 sections of Munkres’ general topology, logging interactions and later analyzing both the resulting formalization and the human–LLM collaboration patterns. They also compare qualitatively against other autoformalization projects like Megalodon, HOL Light, and Naproche.

Result: The project produced over 85,000 lines of Isabelle/HOL code covering all 39 sections (Ch. 2–8) of Munkres’ Topology, with 806 results fully proved and no remaining sorry’s. Major theorems such as Tychonoff, Baire category, Nagata–Smirnov and Smirnov metrization, Stone–Čech compactification, Ascoli’s theorem, and a formalized space-filling curve are included. The authors report that the process took only 24 active days, with LLMs doing most of the proof authoring, and provide a detailed analysis of the corpus and interaction logs.

Conclusion: LLM-assisted formalization in Isabelle/HOL can handle a full, substantial mathematics textbook both quickly and cheaply when combined with Isabelle’s strengths (declarative proofs and sledgehammer). While human supervision and guidance remain important, the experiment suggests that large-scale, textbook-level autoformalization is already practically feasible and may significantly lower the barrier to creating extensive formal mathematical libraries.

Abstract: We describe an experiment in LLM-assisted autoformalization that produced over 85,000 lines of Isabelle/HOL code covering all 39 sections of Munkres' Topology (general topology, Chapters 2--8), from topological spaces through dimension theory. The LLM-based coding agents (initially ChatGPT 5.2 and then Claude Opus 4.6) used 24 active days for that. The formalization is complete: all 806 formal results are fully proved with zero sorry's. Proved results include the Tychonoff theorem, the Baire category theorem, the Nagata--Smirnov and Smirnov metrization theorems, the Stone--Čech compactification, Ascoli's theorem, the space-filling curve, and others. The methodology is based on a "sorry-first" declarative proof workflow combined with bulk use of sledgehammer - two of Isabelle major strengths. This leads to relatively fast autoformalization progress. We analyze the resulting formalization in detail, analyze the human--LLM interaction patterns from the session log, and briefly compare with related autoformalization efforts in Megalodon, HOL Light, and Naproche. The results indicate that LLM-assisted formalization of standard mathematical textbooks in Isabelle/HOL is quite feasible, cheap and fast, even if some human supervision is useful.

</details>


### [62] [M-ArtAgent: Evidence-Based Multimodal Agent for Implicit Art Influence Discovery](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07468)

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents M-ArtAgent, a multimodal agent that infers implicit artistic influence using verifiable evidence rather than just visual similarity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Implicit artistic influence is often undocumented, and existing methods mostly rely on embedding similarity or label-based graph completion, which confound visual resemblance with genuine historical influence and are prone to temporal inconsistencies and unverifiable claims. There is a need for a system that aligns with art-historical methodology, grounding influence detection in verifiable, temporally coherent evidence.

Method: The authors design M-ArtAgent, a multimodal Reasoning-and-Acting (ReAct)-style agent operating in four phases: Investigation, Corroboration, Falsification, and Verdict. It gathers and chains evidence from artworks and artist biographies while enforcing art-historical axioms (e.g., temporal order, documentation constraints). Two specialized operators are introduced: StyleComparator, which performs Wolfflin-style formal analysis of visual features, and ConceptRetriever, which grounds iconographic content using ICONCLASS. The agent uses a prompt-isolated critic for adversarial falsification of influence hypotheses, ensuring that intermediate reasoning steps are auditable and domain-constrained.

Result: On the WikiArt Influence Benchmark-100 (WIB-100), which includes 100 artists and 2,000 directed artist-pair influence hypotheses, M-ArtAgent achieves 83.7% F1 on the positive (influence) class, a Matthews correlation coefficient of 0.666, and ROC-AUC of 0.910. Additional leakage-control and robustness experiments show that performance gains remain even when explicit textual evidence of influence (e.g., phrases directly stating influence) is masked, indicating that the model is not simply exploiting obvious cues.

Conclusion: M-ArtAgent shows that implicit artistic influence can be assessed more reliably by combining multimodal perception with historically grounded adjudication, rather than relying on pattern matching or embedding similarity alone. Its evidence-based, falsification-oriented protocol and auditable operators provide a more principled framework for influence attribution in art history and potentially other cultural domains.

Abstract: Implicit artistic influence, although visually plausible, is often undocumented and thus poses a historically constrained attribution problem: resemblance is necessary but not sufficient evidence. Most prior systems reduce influence discovery to embedding similarity or label-driven graph completion, while recent multimodal large language models (LLMs) remain vulnerable to temporal inconsistency and unverified attributions. This paper introduces M-ArtAgent, an evidence-based multimodal agent that reframes implicit influence discovery as probabilistic adjudication. It follows a four-phase protocol consisting of Investigation, Corroboration, Falsification, and Verdict governed by a Reasoning and Acting (ReAct)-style controller that assembles verifiable evidence chains from images and biographies, enforces art-historical axioms, and subjects each hypothesis to adversarial falsification via a prompt-isolated critic. Two theory-grounded operators, StyleComparator for Wolfflin formal analysis and ConceptRetriever for ICONCLASS-based iconographic grounding, ensure that intermediate claims are formally auditable. On the balanced WikiArt Influence Benchmark-100 (WIB-100) of 100 artists and 2,000 directed pairs, M-ArtAgent achieves 83.7% positive-class F1, 0.666 Matthews correlation coefficient (MCC), and 0.910 area under the receiver operating characteristic curve (ROC-AUC), with leakage-control and robustness checks confirming that the gains persist when explicit influence phrases are masked. By coupling multimodal perception with domain-constrained falsification, M-ArtAgent demonstrates that implicit influence analysis benefits from historically grounded adjudication rather than pattern matching alone.

### [63] [ConsistRM: Improving Generative Reward Models via Consistency-Aware Self-Training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07484)
*Yu Liang,Liangxin Liu,Longzheng Wang,Yan Wang,Yueyang Zhang,Long Xia,Zhiyuan Sun,Daiting Shi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes ConsistRM, a self-training framework for generative reward models that eliminates human annotation and stabilizes training through consistency-aware rewards, achieving better performance and more consistent outputs than standard RFT.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Generative reward models, which generate textual feedback instead of scalar scores, can align LLMs with human preferences more flexibly. However, they depend heavily on expensive human-labeled data and existing self-training methods tend to be unstable and prone to reward hacking, limiting scalability and reliability. The authors aim to develop a scalable, annotation-free method that keeps GRM training stable and robust.

Method: The authors introduce ConsistRM, a self-training framework for GRMs that relies on two consistency-aware reward mechanisms instead of human labels. First, a Consistency-Aware Answer Reward generates pseudo-labels for candidate answers and enforces temporal consistency across training iterations, reducing optimization noise and instability. Second, a Consistency-Aware Critique Reward evaluates the semantic agreement among multiple critiques, assigning fine-grained, differentiated reward signals that reflect how consistently critiques support or oppose certain behaviors. Together, these rewards guide GRM training in a self-supervised, stability-focused manner, replacing standard scalar rewards used in vanilla RFT.

Result: On five benchmark datasets and across four different base language models, ConsistRM consistently outperforms vanilla Reinforcement Fine-Tuning (RFT) by an average margin of 1.5%. Empirical evaluations further show improved robustness: outputs from models trained with ConsistRM exhibit higher internal consistency and reduced sensitivity to the order of inputs (less position bias).

Conclusion: ConsistRM demonstrates that consistency-aware, self-training strategies can effectively replace human annotation in training generative reward models while improving stability and robustness. By leveraging temporal consistency for answer rewards and semantic consistency for critique rewards, the framework mitigates reward hacking and position bias, offering a scalable and more reliable approach for aligning LLMs with human preferences.

Abstract: Generative reward models (GRMs) have emerged as a promising approach for aligning Large Language Models (LLMs) with human preferences by offering greater representational capacity and flexibility than traditional scalar reward models. However, GRMs face two major challenges: reliance on costly human-annotated data restricts scalability, and self-training approaches often suffer from instability and vulnerability to reward hacking. To address these issues, we propose ConsistRM, a self-training framework that enables effective and stable GRM training without human annotations. ConsistRM incorporates the Consistency-Aware Answer Reward, which produces reliable pseudo-labels with temporal consistency, thereby providing more stable model optimization. Moreover, the Consistency-Aware Critique Reward is introduced to assess semantic consistency across multiple critiques and allocates fine-grained and differentiated rewards. Experiments on five benchmark datasets across four base models demonstrate that ConsistRM outperforms vanilla Reinforcement Fine-Tuning (RFT) by an average of 1.5%. Further analysis shows that ConsistRM enhances output consistency and mitigates position bias caused by input order, highlighting the effectiveness of consistency-aware rewards in improving GRMs.

### [64] [CLEAR: Context Augmentation from Contrastive Learning of Experience via Agentic Reflection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07487)
*Linbo Liu,Guande Wu,Han Ding,Yawei Wang,Qiang Zhou,Yuzhe Lu,Zhichao Xu,Huan Song,Panpan Xu,Lin Lee Cheong*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes CLEAR, a framework that trains a generative context augmentation model to create task-specific context for LLM agents, improving their performance over retrieval-based context methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agent context engineering relies heavily on retrieving and reusing past experience, which often does not directly fit new tasks and forces the execution agent to adapt the retrieved context, adding extra reasoning load and limiting performance. A more effective way is needed to provide task-specific, well-adapted context automatically.

Method: CLEAR introduces a two-stage framework. First, a reflection agent performs contrastive analysis over past execution trajectories to summarize useful, discriminative context for each observed task, forming supervised fine-tuning data. A context augmentation model (CAM) is then trained on these summaries via supervised fine-tuning. In the second stage, CAM is further optimized with reinforcement learning, where rewards come from running a task execution agent conditioned on CAM-generated context, encouraging CAM to generate context that leads to better task outcomes.

Result: On AppWorld, CLEAR increases task completion rate from 72.62% to 81.15% compared with a strong baseline agent. On a subset of WebShop, CLEAR improves the averaged reward from 0.68 to 0.74, consistently outperforming alternative context engineering baselines.

Conclusion: Generating task-specific context via CLEAR—using contrastive reflection and RL-optimized context augmentation—offers more suitable decision context for LLM agents than retrieval-based methods, significantly boosting performance on interactive benchmarks like AppWorld and WebShop.

Abstract: Large language model agents rely on effective model context to obtain task-relevant information for decision-making. Many existing context engineering approaches primarily rely on the context generated from the past experience and retrieval mechanisms that reuse these context. However, retrieved context from past tasks must be adapted by the execution agent to fit new situations, placing additional reasoning burden on the underlying LLM. To address this limitation, we propose a generative context augmentation framework using Contrastive Learning of Experience via Agentic Reflection (CLEAR). CLEAR first employs a reflection agent to perform contrastive analysis over past execution trajectories and summarize useful context for each observed task. These summaries are then used as supervised fine-tuning data to train a context augmentation model (CAM). Then we further optimize CAM using reinforcement learning, where the reward signal is obtained by running the task execution agent. By learning to generate task-specific knowledge rather than retrieve knowledge from the past, CAM produces context that is better tailored to the current task. We conduct comprehensive evaluations on the AppWorld and WebShop benchmarks. Experimental results show that CLEAR consistently outperforms strong baselines. It improves task completion rate from 72.62% to 81.15% on AppWorld test set and averaged reward from 0.68 to 0.74 on a subset of WebShop, compared with baseline agent. Our code is publicly available at https://github.com/awslabs/CLEAR.

*Kai Qin,Liangxin Liu,Yu Liang,Longzheng Wang,Yan Wang,Yueyang Zhang,Long Xia,Zhiyuan Sun,Houde Liu,Daiting Shi*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing reward models in RLHF, especially traditional scalar RMs, are limited in interpretability and generalization. Generative reward models are better but still largely focus only on outcome-level supervision (the final answer), ignoring the quality of the analytical process. This neglect of reasoning quality restricts how well they can model human preferences and leads to issues like positional bias and unstable evaluations. The paper aims to build a reward model that can explicitly consider and evaluate analytical reasoning to achieve better alignment and more robust preference judgments.

Method: The authors propose ReflectRM, a generative reward model that incorporates self-reflection. It is trained in a unified generative framework that jointly models two things: (1) response preference (which final answer is better) and (2) analysis preference (which reasoning/analysis is better). At inference time, ReflectRM generates and reflects on analyses for candidate responses, uses this self-reflection to identify the most reliable analysis, and then bases its final preference prediction on that analysis. This design explicitly ties evaluation of reasoning quality to preference decisions and is intended to reduce biases like over-reliance on position.

Result: On four benchmarks, ReflectRM consistently outperforms existing reward models, yielding an average accuracy improvement of +3.7 points on Qwen3-4B. It also significantly reduces positional bias, with a +10.2 point improvement compared to leading GRMs, demonstrating more stable and reliable evaluation behavior. Additional experiments show that learning both response preference and analysis preference together leads to mutual reinforcement and better overall performance.

Conclusion: Incorporating self-reflective analysis evaluation into generative reward models improves both accuracy and robustness of preference modeling in RLHF. ReflectRM, by jointly modeling response and analysis preference and using self-reflection at inference, achieves better performance and greatly mitigates positional bias compared with prior GRMs, suggesting that reasoning-aware RMs are a promising direction for advancing LLM alignment.

Abstract: Reward Models (RMs) are critical components in the Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF) pipeline, directly determining the alignment quality of Large Language Models (LLMs). Recently, Generative Reward Models (GRMs) have emerged as a superior paradigm, offering higher interpretability and stronger generalization than traditional scalar RMs. However, existing methods for GRMs focus primarily on outcome-level supervision, neglecting analytical process quality, which constrains their potential. To address this, we propose ReflectRM, a novel GRM that leverages self-reflection to assess analytical quality and enhance preference modeling. ReflectRM is trained under a unified generative framework for joint modeling of response preference and analysis preference. During inference, we use its self-reflection capability to identify the most reliable analysis, from which the final preference prediction is derived. Experiments across four benchmarks show that ReflectRM consistently improves performance, achieving an average accuracy gain of +3.7 on Qwen3-4B. Further experiments confirm that response preference and analysis preference are mutually reinforcing. Notably, ReflectRM substantially mitigates positional bias, yielding +10.2 improvement compared with leading GRMs and establishing itself as a more stable evaluator.

The Boltz-2 affinity predictions, calibrated on ChEMBL, achieve Spearman rank correlations of -0.53 to -0.64 with experimental data, and ROC AUC values between 0.88 and 0.93 for active vs. inactive discrimination, suggesting good ranking and classification performance for binding affinity in this setting.

Conclusion: Rhizome OS-1 shows that a semi-autonomous, multi-agent AI operating system, coupled with a graph-native generative model and physics-informed scoring, can produce structurally novel, target-focused chemical matter at scale while maintaining predictive fidelity for binding. This architecture supports rapid, adaptive inverse design workflows and points toward a new paradigm for early-stage small-molecule discovery, where AI agents coordinate end-to-end tasks from hypothesis generation to candidate selection, potentially reducing cycle times and expanding the searchable chemical space beyond what manual approaches can achieve.

Abstract: We introduce a semi-autonomous discovery system in which multi-modal AI agents function as a multi-disciplinary discovery team, acting as computational chemists, medicinal chemists, and patent agents, writing and executing analysis code, visually evaluating molecular candidates, assessing patentability, and adapting generation strategy from empirical screening feedback, while r1, a 246M-parameter Graph Neural Network (GNN) trained on 800M molecules, generates novel chemical matter directly on molecular graphs. Agents executed two campaigns in oncology (BCL6, EZH2), formulating medicinal chemistry hypotheses across three strategy tiers and generating libraries of 2,355-2,876 novel molecules per target. Across both targets, 91.9% of generated Murcko scaffolds are absent from ChEMBL for their respective targets, with Tanimoto distances of 0.56-0.69 to the nearest known active, confirming that the engine produces structurally distinct chemical matter rather than recapitulating known compounds. Binding affinity predictions using Boltz-2 were calibrated against ChEMBL experimental data, achieving Spearman correlations of -0.53 to -0.64 and ROC AUC values of 0.88 to 0.93. These results demonstrate that semi-autonomous agent systems, equipped with graph-native generative tools and physics-informed scoring, provide a foundation for a modern operating system for small molecule discovery. We show that Rhizome OS-1 enables a new paradigm for early-stage drug discovery by supporting scaled, rapid, and adaptive inverse design.

### [67] [Trust the AI, Doubt Yourself: The Effect of Urgency on Self-Confidence in Human-AI Interaction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07535)
*Baran Shajari,Xiaoran Liu,Kyanna Dagenais,Istvan David*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates how urgency in human-AI interactions affects user trust in AI versus users’ self-confidence and self-efficacy, finding that urgency does not change trust in AI but can undermine human confidence, especially without gradual onboarding.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While prior work has shown that interacting with AI tends to increase human trust in AI, less is known about how contextual factors like perceived urgency during AI prompts influence the human side of the relationship—particularly users’ confidence in their own abilities. Understanding this is important because over time reduced self-confidence could harm human performance and the sustainability of AI-assisted systems.

Method: The authors ran an experiment with 30 human participants who interacted with an AI system under conditions that varied in urgency and in how participants were introduced or ‘eased into’ the human-AI collaboration. They then measured trust in AI, self-confidence, and self-efficacy, and compared these measures across conditions.

Result: The study found that adding urgency to AI prompts did not significantly change the level of trust participants placed in the AI system. However, urgency and lack of preparation were associated with lower self-confidence and self-efficacy in participants. Participants who were gradually introduced to the human-AI setup reported higher confidence in their own work than those exposed abruptly to the AI interaction under urgent conditions.

Conclusion: Urgency in human-AI interactions may not harm users’ trust in AI but can erode their confidence in their own abilities, which can have long-term negative effects on decision quality and system sustainability. Systems should therefore be designed to ease users into AI-supported workflows and manage urgency carefully, and software engineers and decision-makers should account for these human factors when deploying AI tools.

Abstract: Studies show that interactions with an AI system fosters trust in human users towards AI. An often overlooked element of such interaction dynamics is the (sense of) urgency when the human user is prompted by an AI agent, e.g., for advice or guidance. In this paper, we show that although the presence of urgency in human-AI interactions does not affect the trust in AI, it may be detrimental to the human user's self-confidence and self-efficacy. In the long run, the loss of confidence may lead to performance loss, suboptimal decisions, human errors, and ultimately, unsustainable AI systems. Our evidence comes from an experiment with 30 human participants. Our results indicate that users may feel more confident in their work when they are eased into the human-AI setup rather than exposed to it without preparation. We elaborate on the implications of this finding for software engineers and decision-makers.

The demonstrated energy savings and maintained SLA adherence indicate that such frameworks can provide a viable foundation for broader, system-wide AI-based optimization in data centers.

Abstract: The growing scale and complexity of modern data centers present major challenges in balancing energy efficiency with outage risk. This paper introduces the Dual-Loop Control Framework (DLCF), a digital twin-based architecture designed to overcome these challenges. The framework comprises three core entities: the physical system, a digital twin, and a policy reservoir of diverse DRL agents. Theoretical analysis shows how DLCF can improve sample efficiency, generalization, safety, and optimality. Leveraging DLCF, we implemented the DCVerse platform and validated it through case studies on a real-world data center cooling system. The evaluation shows that our approach achieves up to 4.09% energy savings over conventional control strategies without violating SLA requirements. Additionally, the framework improves policy interpretability and supports more trustworthy DRL deployment. This work provides a foundation for reliable AI-based control in data centers and points toward future extensions for holistic, system-wide optimization.

### [70] [From Papers to Property Tables: A Priority-Based LLM Workflow for Materials Data Extraction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07584)
*Koushik Rameshbabu,Jing Luo,Ali Shargh,Khalid A. El-Awady,Jaafar A. El-Awady*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They build an automated, LLM-driven pipeline to extract, derive, and normalize detailed shot-level shock-physics data from full research articles, achieving high accuracy without task-specific fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scientific experimental data in materials science are buried in unstructured text, tables, and figures across many papers, and are reported inconsistently. Manual extraction is slow, labor-intensive, and error-prone, which limits the construction of large, high-quality databases needed for data-driven materials research. There is a need for scalable, accurate, and traceable methods to convert unstructured literature into structured datasets.

Method: They design a prompt-driven, hierarchical workflow around a large language model to reconstruct structured records for individual shock-physics experiments (shots). The pipeline targets 37 fields per shot and operates in three priority tiers: (T1) direct extraction from text and tables using LLM-based reading and parsing; (T2) physics-based derivation of missing fields using verified governing equations and relations, again assisted by the LLM; and (T3) digitization and interpretation of information from figures only when necessary. All extracted values are normalized into canonical units, labeled with their extraction priority for traceability, and subjected to physics-based consistency and plausibility checks. The system is implemented via an API to enable scalable, automated processing. They evaluate performance on 30 full-text papers covering alloy spall strength experiments, yielding 11,967 evaluated data points, and also perform cross-model comparisons between different LLMs and between API and interactive chat use.

Result: On the benchmark of 30 research articles and 11,967 data points, the workflow achieves high accuracy: 94.93% for directly extracted text/table fields (T1), 92.04% for physics-derived fields (T2), and 83.49% for figure-based fields (T3), leading to an overall weighted accuracy of 94.69%. Cross-model testing shows strong agreement across models for T1 and T2 fields, with more variability for T3 (figure-based) extraction. The API-based implementation maintains consistent extraction quality and, in some test cases, matches or surpasses the accuracy seen in chat-style interactions.

Conclusion: A hierarchical, LLM-driven extraction and derivation workflow can reliably convert complex, distributed information from full-text materials science articles into structured, analysis-ready, and traceable datasets, without requiring task-specific fine-tuning. The combination of priority tiers, physics-based derivations, unit normalization, and consistency checks yields high accuracy at scale. This approach is practical for building large, curated databases in shock physics and is likely extensible to other technical domains where experimental data are scattered across heterogeneous article content.

Abstract: Scientific data are widely dispersed across research articles and are often reported inconsistently across text, tables, and figures, making manual data extraction and aggregation slow and error-prone. We present a prompt-driven, hierarchical workflow that uses a large language model (LLM) to automatically extract and reconstruct structured, shot-level shock-physics experimental records by integrating information distributed across text, tables, figures, and physics-based derivations from full-text published research articles, using alloy spall strength as a representative case study. The pipeline targeted 37 experimentally relevant fields per shot and applied a three-level priority strategy: (T1) direct extraction from text/tables, (T2) physics-based derivation using verified governing relations, and (T3) digitization from figures when necessary. Extracted values were normalized to canonical units, tagged by priority for traceability, and validated with physics-based consistency and plausibility checks. Evaluated on a benchmark of 30 published research articles comprising 11,967 evaluated data points, the workflow achieved high overall accuracy, with priority-wise accuracies of 94.93% (T1), 92.04% (T2), and 83.49% (T3), and an overall weighted accuracy of 94.69%. Cross-model testing further indicated strong agreement for text/table and equation-derived fields, with lower agreement for figure-based extraction. Implementation through an API interface demonstrated the scalability of the approach, achieving consistent extraction performance and, in a subset of test cases, matching or exceeding chat-based accuracy. This workflow demonstrates a practical approach for converting unstructured technical literature into traceable, analysis-ready datasets without task-specific fine-tuning, enabling scalable database construction in materials science.

TL;DR: The paper studies how the lengths of prompts and solutions in math benchmarks affect large language model (LLM) performance, finding that longer prompts and solutions are associated with higher failure rates and greater empirical difficulty.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although math benchmarks are widely used to assess LLM reasoning, there is limited understanding of how basic structural properties of problems—specifically, how long the prompts and solutions are—shape model behavior and apparent difficulty. The authors aim to clarify whether these length features systematically impact evaluation outcomes and comparisons between models.

Method: The authors construct a new adversarial dataset of expert-authored mathematics problems and measure two structural variables: prompt length and solution length. They then analyze correlations between these length measures and model failure rates across multiple LLMs. Additionally, they conduct an exploratory cross-model disagreement analysis, applying a difficulty-adjusted normalized framework to see how length relates to the degree of separation or disagreement between models.

Result: Both prompt length and solution length show positive correlations with model failure: as either length increases, models are more likely to fail. In the cross-model disagreement analysis, after adjusting for difficulty and normalizing, both length variables still show weak negative associations with realized model separation, with prompt length having a slightly stronger effect.

Conclusion: Structural length variables—prompt and solution length—are systematically linked to empirical difficulty for LLMs on this math dataset. Longer prompts and solutions tend to increase failure and slightly reduce normalized cross-model separation, indicating that even simple structural features of benchmark items can meaningfully influence perceived model performance and difficulty.

Abstract: Mathematical benchmarks consisting of a range of mathematics problems are widely used to evaluate the reasoning abilities of large language models, yet little is known about how their structural properties influence model behaviour. In this work, we investigate two structural length variables, prompt length and solution length, and analyse how they relate to model performance on a newly constructed adversarial dataset of expert-authored mathematics problems. We find that both prompt and solution lengths correlate positively with increased model failure across models. We also include a secondary, exploratory analysis of cross-model disagreement. Under a difficulty-adjusted normalised analysis, both variables retain weak negative associations with realised model separation, slightly stronger for prompt length. Overall, our main robust finding is that structural length is linked to empirical difficulty in this dataset.
