### [72] [Reasoning Graphs: Deterministic Agent Accuracy through Evidence-Centric Chain-of-Thought Feedback](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07595)
*Matthew Penaroza*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces reasoning graphs and retrieval graphs as persistent, evidence-centric memory structures that let language model agents reuse prior chains of thought, improving accuracy and reducing variance in multi-hop question answering without retraining the base model.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM agents reason from scratch on each query: they retrieve evidence, deliberate, and then discard the resulting chain of thought. This leads to unstable performance where similar queries can succeed or fail unpredictably, and it wastes prior computational effort. Existing memory approaches mostly store high-level strategies or summaries keyed by query similarity or recency, which do not provide fine-grained, evidence-specific feedback. The authors are motivated to design a persistent structure that allows agents to systematically reuse prior reasoning tied directly to evidence items, thereby creating a self-improving system with traceable decisions, higher accuracy, and lower variance, all without retraining the underlying model.

Method: The authors propose reasoning graphs, where nodes represent evidence items and edges store structured, per-evidence chains of thought from past evaluations. Each time an agent evaluates evidence, it adds edges capturing its judgments and rationales, anchored to the specific evidence item. At inference time, when the agent encounters a candidate evidence set, it performs a backward traversal: for each evidence item, it follows incoming edges to retrieve all prior evaluations of that item and feeds these into the context, giving evidence-centric feedback. They contrast this with query-similarity retrieval, which operates at the level of entire queries. Additionally, they introduce retrieval graphs, which track how evidence was retrieved and used across runs. Retrieval graphs drive a pipeline planner that iteratively refines the retrieval process, narrowing the candidate evidence funnel over multiple runs. The overall system is a feedback loop: each run updates the graphs, and future runs traverse them to guide both reasoning and retrieval. The authors formalize the graph structures, traversal algorithms, and feedback mechanisms, and define an evaluation protocol (sequential cluster evaluation) to measure accuracy convergence and variance across multi-hop QA benchmarks.

Result: Using the reasoning and retrieval graphs, the system achieves increasing accuracy and decreasing variance over successive runs on multi-hop question answering tasks. The authors observe that as the graphs accumulate more evaluations, the agent becomes more consistent in its judgments, and its performance stabilizes instead of fluctuating run-to-run. Importantly, all improvements arise from context engineering via graph traversal rather than changes to the underlying language model. The graph structures also provide full traceability of decisions, as each answer is grounded in traversable chains of evidence evaluations and retrieval steps.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that reasoning graphs and retrieval graphs provide an effective, training-free mechanism for self-improving language model agents. By persisting per-evidence reasoning and retrieval histories in graph form and leveraging structured traversals, agents can reuse past insights in an evidence-centric manner, leading to higher accuracy, reduced variance, and fully traceable decision making on multi-hop QA benchmarks. This approach represents a structurally distinct alternative to query-similarity-based memory and demonstrates the power of graph-based context engineering for improving LLM-based systems.

Abstract: Language model agents reason from scratch on every query: each time an agent retrieves evidence and deliberates, the chain of thought is discarded and the next similar query starts with no prior insight. This produces lower accuracy and high variance, as the same type of query can succeed or fail unpredictably. We introduce reasoning graphs, a graph structure that persists an agent's per-evidence chain of thought as structured edges connected to the evidence items they evaluate. Unlike prior memory mechanisms that store distilled strategies as flat records indexed by query similarity or appended by recency, reasoning graphs enable evidence-centric feedback: given a new candidate set, the system traverses all incoming evaluation edges for each evidence item across all prior runs, surfacing how that specific item has been judged before. This backward traversal from evidence inward is a structurally different capability from query-similarity retrieval, because the feedback is tied to the specific evidence the agent is currently examining, not to the query. We further introduce retrieval graphs, a complementary structure that feeds a pipeline planner to tighten the candidate funnel over successive runs. Together, both graphs form a self-improving feedback loop: accuracy rises and variance collapses over successive runs, with every decision fully traceable through the graph. This improvement requires no retraining; the base model remains frozen and all gains come from context engineering via graph traversal. We formalize the graph structure, traversal algorithms, and feedback mechanisms, and describe a sequential cluster evaluation protocol for measuring accuracy convergence and variance collapse on multi-hop question answering benchmarks.

</details>

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PRIME is a gradient-free framework that lets tool-using AI agents improve over multi-turn human interactions by storing and reusing structured experiences instead of doing expensive RL training.

  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for a more efficient, interpretable way for agents to continually improve from real interactions, without heavy gradient-based optimization.

Method: The authors propose PRIME (Proactive Reasoning via Iterative Memory Evolution), a gradient-free learning framework. PRIME records multi-turn Human-AI interaction trajectories and distills them into structured, human-readable memories divided into three semantic zones: (1) successful strategies, (2) failure patterns, and (3) user preferences. These memories are iteratively updated and refined through meta-level operations (i.e., explicit editing and evolution of the memory store) and are then used to guide the agent’s future behavior via retrieval-augmented generation, rather than by updating model parameters with gradients.

Result: Across several user-centric benchmark environments, PRIME delivers performance comparable to gradient-based reinforcement learning approaches on tool-use and interaction tasks, while incurring significantly lower computational and data-collection costs. In addition, the structured, interpretable memory makes it easier to inspect and understand what the agent has learned, and how its behavior changes over time.

Conclusion: PRIME provides a practical paradigm for building proactive, collaborative tool-use agents that can continuously learn from human interactions without expensive gradient-based training. By evolving an explicit, structured experience memory and leveraging retrieval-augmented generation, it addresses challenges of long-horizon credit assignment and cost-efficiency, while improving interpretability of the agent’s learned behavior.

Abstract: The development of autonomous tool-use agents for complex, long-horizon tasks in collaboration with human users has become the frontier of agentic research. During multi-turn Human-AI interactions, the dynamic and uncertain nature of user demands poses a significant challenge; agents must not only invoke tools but also iteratively refine their understanding of user intent through effective communication. While recent advances in reinforcement learning offer a path to more capable tool-use agents, existing approaches require expensive training costs and struggle with turn-level credit assignment across extended interaction horizons. To this end, we introduce PRIME (Proactive Reasoning via Iterative Memory Evolution), a gradient-free learning framework that enables continuous agent evolvement through explicit experience accumulation rather than expensive parameter optimization. PRIME distills multi-turn interaction trajectories into structured, human-readable experiences organized across three semantic zones: successful strategies, failure patterns, and user preferences. These experiences evolve through meta-level operations and guide future agent behavior via retrieval-augmented generation. Our experiments across several diverse user-centric environments demonstrate that PRIME achieves competitive performance with gradient-based methods while offering cost-efficiency and interpretability. Together, PRIME presents a practical paradigm for building proactive, collaborative agents that learn from Human-AI interaction without the computational burden of gradient-based training.

### [74] [How Independent are Large Language Models? A Statistical Framework for Auditing Behavioral Entanglement and Reweighting Verifier Ensembles](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07650)
*Chenchen Kuai,Jiwan Jiang,Zihao Zhu,Hao Wang,Keshu Wu,Zihao Li,Yunlong Zhang,Chenxi Liu,Zhengzhong Tu,Zhiwen Fan,Yang Zhou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that many large language models are behaviorally dependent, proposes metrics to quantify this "entanglement," demonstrates that it harms judge-style evaluations, and shows that reweighting models by independence improves ensemble verification accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As many LLMs share training data, architectures, and alignment, their outputs may be correlated, violating the assumption of independence in settings like LLM-as-a-judge and ensemble verification. This can cause systems to over-trust agreement between models because agreement can stem from shared failure modes rather than independent validation. The paper is motivated by the need to detect and quantify such hidden dependencies between black-box LLMs to build more reliable multi-model systems.

Method: The authors develop a statistical auditing framework for black-box LLMs that analyzes their joint error behavior at multiple resolutions. They define two information-theoretic metrics over a hierarchy of task difficulty and response outcomes: (1) a Difficulty-Weighted Behavioral Entanglement Index that upweights synchronized failures on easy tasks, and (2) a Cumulative Information Gain (CIG) metric that measures how much one model’s erroneous responses predict another’s, capturing directional behavioral alignment. They apply this framework to 18 LLMs from six families, evaluate dependencies, relate them to LLM-as-a-judge performance, and then propose an ensemble reweighting scheme that downweights more entangled models when aggregating verifier decisions.

Result: Empirical analysis across 18 LLMs reveals widespread behavioral entanglement: models frequently share correlated reasoning patterns and aligned error modes, especially when they share training lineage. The CIG metric is significantly associated with reductions in LLM-as-a-judge precision, with reported Spearman correlations of 0.64 (p < 0.001) for GPT-4o-mini judges and 0.71 (p < 0.01) for Llama3-based judges, indicating that stronger behavioral dependency correlates with higher over-endorsement bias. In a practical ensemble verification setting, reweighting models by inferred independence from the entanglement metrics reduces correlated bias and yields up to a 4.5% absolute accuracy improvement over simple majority voting.

Conclusion: Many ostensibly distinct LLMs are behaviorally entangled due to shared data and pipelines, causing synchronized failures that invalidate independence assumptions in multi-model applications. The proposed information-theoretic auditing framework effectively detects and quantifies this entanglement, and its CIG metric predicts degradation in judge-style evaluation reliability. Incorporating entanglement-aware reweighting in verifier ensembles can mitigate correlated errors and improve overall verification performance. Therefore, designers of multi-model LLM systems should explicitly measure and account for behavioral dependencies rather than assuming independence.

Abstract: The rapid growth of the large language model (LLM) ecosystem raises a critical question: are seemingly diverse models truly independent? Shared pretraining data, distillation, and alignment pipelines can induce hidden behavioral dependencies, latent entanglement, that undermine multi-model systems such as LLM-as-a-judge pipelines and ensemble verification, which implicitly assume independent signals. In practice, this manifests as correlated reasoning patterns and synchronized failures, where apparent agreement reflects shared error modes rather than independent validation. To address this, we develop a statistical framework for auditing behavioral entanglement among black-box LLMs. Our approach introduces a multi-resolution hierarchy that characterizes the joint failure manifold through two information-theoretic metrics: (i) a Difficulty-Weighted Behavioral Entanglement Index, which amplifies synchronized failures on easy tasks, and (ii) a Cumulative Information Gain (CIG) metric, which captures directional alignment in erroneous responses. Through extensive experiments on 18 LLMs from six model families, we identify widespread behavioral entanglement and analyze its impact on LLM-as-a-judge evaluation. We find that CIG exhibits a statistically significant association with degradation in judge precision, with Spearman coefficient of 0.64 (p < 0.001) for GPT-4o-mini and 0.71 (p < 0.01) for Llama3-based judges, indicating that stronger dependency corresponds to increased over-endorsement bias. Finally, we demonstrate a practical use case of entanglement through de-entangled verifier ensemble reweighting. By adjusting model contributions based on inferred independence, the proposed method mitigates correlated bias and improves verification performance, achieving up to a 4.5% accuracy gain over majority voting.

### [75] [Bridging Natural Language and Interactive What-If Interfaces via LLM-Generated Declarative Specification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07652)
*Sneha Gathani,Sirui Zeng,Diya Patel,Ryan Rossi,Dan Marshall,Cagatay Demiralp,Steven Drucker,Zhicheng Liu*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: What-if analysis is widely used for exploring hypothetical scenarios, but existing tools are cumbersome or unreliable. Spreadsheets and BI tools require heavy manual setup and are not easily iterative or interactive. Pure LLM chat interfaces, while flexible, are semantically fragile, often misinterpret user intent, and yield inconsistent results over a conversation. There is a need for a more dependable way to translate natural-language what-if questions into interactive analytical tools while allowing validation and correction before users act on the results.

Method: The authors propose a two-stage workflow centered on an intermediate formalism, the Praxa Specification Language (PSL). In stage one, large language models translate a user’s natural-language what-if question into a PSL specification that captures analytical intent, parameters, constraints, and logic. Because PSL is structured, these specifications can be automatically validated and repaired. In stage two, the validated PSL specifications are compiled into interactive visual interfaces that provide parameter controls and linked visualizations for performing the what-if analysis. The authors benchmark this workflow using 405 what-if questions that vary along 11 what-if analysis types, 5 datasets, and 3 state-of-the-art LLMs, and they systematically study and repair errors using prompt engineering and an error taxonomy.

Result: In the benchmark, 52.42% of PSL specifications generated by the LLMs were correct without any intervention. Through analysis of failures, the authors construct a taxonomy of non-functional errors (that prevent compilation) and functional errors (that compile but misrepresent user intent). Using this taxonomy, they design targeted few-shot prompt repairs for the failure cases, raising the overall success rate to 80.42%. They additionally show that functional errors that go undetected at the specification level produce plausible yet misleading interactive interfaces after compilation, highlighting a key reliability risk in direct NL-to-interface generation.

Conclusion: An intermediate specification language like PSL is a crucial component for robust LLM-powered what-if analysis systems. By separating NL understanding from interface generation, enabling validation and targeted repair at the specification stage, the proposed two-stage workflow significantly improves correctness and reliability. The work demonstrates both the feasibility and the necessity of structured intermediates for safely bridging natural-language questions and interactive analytical interfaces in what-if analysis.

Abstract: What-if analysis (WIA) is an iterative, multi-step process where users explore and compare hypothetical scenarios by adjusting parameters, applying constraints, and scoping data through interactive interfaces. Current tools fall short of supporting effective interactive WIA: spreadsheet and BI tools require time-consuming and laborious setup, while LLM-based chatbot interfaces are semantically fragile, frequently misinterpret intent, and produce inconsistent results as conversations progress. To address these limitations, we present a two-stage workflow that translates natural language (NL) WIA questions into interactive visual interfaces via an intermediate representation, powered by the Praxa Specification Language (PSL): first, LLMs generate PSL specifications from NL questions capturing analytical intent and logic, enabling validation and repair of erroneous specifications; and second, the specifications are compiled into interactive visual interfaces with parameter controls and linked visualizations. We benchmark this workflow with 405 WIA questions spanning 11 WIA types, 5 datasets, and 3 state-of-the-art LLMs. The results show that across models, half of specifications (52.42%) are generated correctly without intervention. We perform an analysis of the failure cases and derive an error taxonomy spanning non-functional errors (specifications fail to compile) and functional errors (specifications compile but misrepresent intent). Based on the taxonomy, we apply targeted repairs on the failure cases using few-shot prompts and improve the success rate to 80.42%. Finally, we show how undetected functional errors propagate through compilation into plausible but misleading interfaces, demonstrating that the intermediate specification is critical for reliably bridging NL and interactive WIA interface in LLM-powered WIA systems.

### [76] [From Debate to Decision: Conformal Social Choice for Safe Multi-Agent Deliberation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07667)
*Mengdie Flora Wang,Haochen Xie,Guanghui Wang,Aijing Gao,Guang Yang,Ziyuan Li,Qucy Wei Qiu,Fangwei Han,Hengzhi Qiu,Yajing Huang,Bing Zhu,Jae Oh Woo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a conformal prediction-based decision layer on top of multi-agent LLM debates to decide when to act autonomously versus escalate to humans, providing calibrated safety guarantees rather than improving raw reasoning accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-agent debate among large language models can still converge on confidently wrong answers, and consensus-based stopping rules risk automatically committing these errors with no safety net. Existing approaches focus on improving accuracy, but there is a need for a principled mechanism that turns debate outputs into reliable decisions about when automation is safe and when human oversight is needed, without relying on perfect model calibration.

Method: The authors propose Conformal Social Choice, a post-hoc decision layer that takes verbalized probability distributions from multiple, heterogeneous LLM agents. These distributions are aggregated using a linear opinion pool to form a collective predictive distribution. Split conformal prediction is then applied to this aggregate to construct prediction sets that satisfy marginal coverage guarantees (the true answer lies in the set with probability at least 1−α), without assuming that individual agents are well-calibrated. A hierarchical policy then maps prediction sets of size one to fully automated actions and larger sets to human escalation. The method is evaluated on eight MMLU-Pro domains using three distinct agents (Claude Haiku, DeepSeek-R1, Qwen-3 32B).

Result: Across eight MMLU-Pro domains with three heterogeneous agents, the conformal prediction sets achieve coverage within 1–2 percentage points of the target level, indicating good calibration. The conformal social choice layer intercepts 81.9% of cases where the agents reach a wrong consensus at α=0.05, preventing these errors from being automatically acted upon. For the remaining cases where the conformal layer outputs singleton sets (and thus permits automation), accuracy is very high—between 90.0% and 96.8%, up to 22.1 percentage points better than simple consensus-based stopping. However, this performance gain is explicitly identified as a selection effect rather than an intrinsic improvement in the agents’ reasoning abilities.

Conclusion: Conformal Social Choice provides a principled, calibrated mechanism for deciding when to trust multi-agent LLM debate outcomes versus when to escalate to humans. It does not make debate more accurate per se, but transforms its failures into actionable signals by refusing to act when the debate is likely to be wrong. This significantly improves the safety of automated actions, at the cost of reduced automation, with the trade-off controllable by the user via the α parameter that governs the coverage and escalation rate.

Abstract: Multi-agent debate improves LLM reasoning, yet agreement among agents is not evidence of correctness. When agents converge on a wrong answer through social reinforcement, consensus-based stopping commits that error to an automated action with no recourse. We introduce Conformal Social Choice, a post-hoc decision layer that converts debate outputs into calibrated act-versus-escalate decisions. Verbalized probability distributions from heterogeneous agents are aggregated via a linear opinion pool and calibrated with split conformal prediction, yielding prediction sets with a marginal coverage guarantee: the correct answer is included with probability ${\geq}\,1{-}α$, without assumptions on individual model calibration. A hierarchical action policy maps singleton sets to autonomous action and larger sets to human escalation. On eight MMLU-Pro domains with three agents (Claude Haiku, DeepSeek-R1, Qwen-3 32B), coverage stays within 1--2 points of the target. The key finding is not that debate becomes more accurate, but that the conformal layer makes its failures actionable: 81.9% of wrong-consensus cases are intercepted at $α{=}0.05$. Because the layer refuses to act on cases where debate is confidently wrong, the remaining conformal singletons reach 90.0--96.8% accuracy (up to 22.1pp above consensus stopping) -- a selection effect, not a reasoning improvement. This safety comes at the cost of automation, but the operating point is user-adjustable via $α$.

### [78] [IatroBench: Pre-Registered Evidence of Iatrogenic Harm from AI Safety Measures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07709)
*David Gringras*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces IatroBench, a benchmark to measure how large language models (LLMs) withhold medical guidance depending on user identity framing (physician vs. layperson), revealing substantial safety-related gaps.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often refuse or limit medical advice for lay users in the name of safety, yet provide detailed, clinically accurate guidance when the same question is posed as coming from a clinician. This identity-contingent withholding can be harmful when users have already exhausted standard referrals and need nuanced risk-reduction strategies. The authors want to quantify and characterize this behavior across frontier models and expose failures in standard LLM evaluation pipelines.

Method: The authors build IatroBench, a set of 60 pre-registered, high-stakes clinical scenarios where usual referral options are exhausted. Each scenario is posed in matched physician and layperson framings to several frontier LLMs. They collect 3,600 total responses and score them along two axes: commission harm (CH 0–3) and omission harm (OH 0–4). Scoring uses a structured evaluation pipeline validated against physician ratings, achieving substantial weighted agreement. They then analyze differences in safety-related behavior between identity framings and across models, examining model-level failure modes and the behavior of an LLM-based judge used for automated evaluation.

Result: Across all tested models, the same underlying knowledge is present but is selectively revealed based on user identity framing. Models consistently provide more complete and clinically useful guidance to physician-framed prompts than layperson-framed prompts, despite identical clinical content. Quantitatively, the decoupling gap between physician and layperson guidance is +0.38 (p = 0.003); layperson prompts see a 13.1 percentage point drop in safe, safety-colliding actions, while non-colliding actions are unchanged. The model with the most explicit safety investment (Opus) exhibits the largest gap (+0.65). The authors identify three distinct failure modes across models: trained withholding (Opus), competence limitations (Llama 4), and blunt post-generation content filtering (GPT-5.2), which disproportionately removes physician-framed answers due to denser pharmacological language. Additionally, a standard LLM judge underestimates omission harm, assigning OH = 0 to 73% of responses that physicians rate OH ≥ 1, with near-zero kappa, showing that automated evaluation shares the same blind spot as model training.

Conclusion: IatroBench reveals that LLM safety mechanisms can produce identity-contingent withholding of clinically important information: models are significantly more helpful to users framed as physicians than to laypersons asking the same question, even when those laypersons cannot access standard care pathways. Safety systems thus risk increasing clinical omission harm while still allowing dangerous commission harm in some cases. The work argues that both training and evaluation pipelines need to be rethought so that safety focuses on harm reduction and context, not simply on identity-based refusal or token-level filters, and that benchmarks must explicitly test for these subtle, identity-conditioned failures.

Abstract: Ask a frontier model how to taper six milligrams of alprazolam (psychiatrist retired, ten days of pills left, abrupt cessation causes seizures) and it tells her to call the psychiatrist she just explained does not exist. Change one word ("I'm a psychiatrist; a patient presents with...") and the same model, same weights, same inference pass produces a textbook Ashton Manual taper with diazepam equivalence, anticonvulsant coverage, and monitoring thresholds. The knowledge was there; the model withheld it. IatroBench measures this gap. Sixty pre-registered clinical scenarios, six frontier models, 3,600 responses, scored on two axes (commission harm, CH 0-3; omission harm, OH 0-4) through a structured-evaluation pipeline validated against physician scoring (kappa_w = 0.571, within-1 agreement 96%). The central finding is identity-contingent withholding: match the same clinical question in physician vs. layperson framing and all five testable models provide better guidance to the physician (decoupling gap +0.38, p = 0.003; binary hit rates on safety-colliding actions drop 13.1 percentage points in layperson framing, p < 0.0001, while non-colliding actions show no change). The gap is widest for the model with the heaviest safety investment (Opus, +0.65). Three failure modes separate cleanly: trained withholding (Opus), incompetence (Llama 4), and indiscriminate content filtering (GPT-5.2, whose post-generation filter strips physician responses at 9x the layperson rate because they contain denser pharmacological tokens). The standard LLM judge assigns OH = 0 to 73% of responses a physician scores OH >= 1 (kappa = 0.045); the evaluation apparatus has the same blind spot as the training apparatus. Every scenario targets someone who has already exhausted the standard referrals.

### [79] [Towards Knowledgeable Deep Research: Framework and Benchmark](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07720)
*Wenxuan Liu,Zixuan Li,Bai Long,Chunmao Zhang,Fenghui Zhang,Zhuo Chen,Wei Li,Yuxin Zuo,Fei Wang,Bingbing Xu,Xuhui Jiang,Jin Zhang,Xiaolong Jin,Jiafeng Guo,Tat-Seng Chua,Xueqi Cheng*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Knowledgeable Deep Research (KDR), a harder version of deep research where LLM agents must jointly use structured and unstructured knowledge, and proposes a multi-agent framework (HKA) plus a benchmark (KDR-Bench) to evaluate this capability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing deep research (DR) work mostly relies on unstructured web data (text pages), which limits quantitative analysis, data-grounded reasoning, and multimodal reporting. Many real-world analysis tasks, however, require combining structured sources (tables, databases, figures) with unstructured documents to draw accurate, in-depth conclusions. There is a lack of a clear task definition, specialized agent architecture, and standardized benchmark for this more realistic, structure-aware deep research scenario. The paper aims to formalize this problem, enable agents to use both structured and unstructured information, and provide tools to systematically evaluate them.

Method: 1) Define the Knowledgeable Deep Research (KDR) task, where agents must produce comprehensive reports grounded in both structured (tables, data) and unstructured (text, web) knowledge. 2) Propose the Hybrid Knowledge Analysis (HKA) framework, a multi-agent system that: (a) decomposes DR tasks, (b) uses a Structured Knowledge Analyzer that leverages both code execution and vision-language models to read, manipulate, and visualize structured data, and (c) integrates texts, generated/parsed figures, and tables into coherent multimodal reports. 3) Build KDR-Bench, a benchmark spanning 9 domains and 41 expert-level questions, containing extensive structured resources (over 1,200 tables). For each question they annotate gold conclusions and key points. 4) Design three categories of metrics: general-purpose (e.g., standard DR quality), knowledge-centric (grounding in structured data), and vision-enhanced (evaluating multimodal understanding and generation). 5) Experimentally compare HKA against existing DR agents, including Gemini-based DR, across these metrics.

Result: On KDR-Bench, the proposed HKA framework consistently outperforms most existing DR agents on both general-purpose metrics (overall report quality, reasoning) and knowledge-centric metrics (how well the answer uses and reflects structured data). On vision-enhanced metrics that assess multimodal capabilities (texts, tables, figures), HKA even surpasses the Gemini DR agent, indicating superior integration of visual and structured information into the final reports.

Conclusion: The work establishes Knowledgeable Deep Research (KDR) as a new, more realistic DR task that demands the integration of structured and unstructured knowledge. The proposed HKA multi-agent framework effectively performs structure-aware, multimodal analysis and report generation, achieving state-of-the-art performance on the newly introduced KDR-Bench. The authors position HKA and KDR-Bench as foundational resources to drive future research on multimodal, data-grounded deep research agents and structured knowledge analysis.

Abstract: Deep Research (DR) requires LLM agents to autonomously perform multi-step information seeking, processing, and reasoning to generate comprehensive reports. In contrast to existing studies that mainly focus on unstructured web content, a more challenging DR task should additionally utilize structured knowledge to provide a solid data foundation, facilitate quantitative computation, and lead to in-depth analyses. In this paper, we refer to this novel task as Knowledgeable Deep Research (KDR), which requires DR agents to generate reports with both structured and unstructured knowledge. Furthermore, we propose the Hybrid Knowledge Analysis framework (HKA), a multi-agent architecture that reasons over both kinds of knowledge and integrates the texts, figures, and tables into coherent multimodal reports. The key design is the Structured Knowledge Analyzer, which utilizes both coding and vision-language models to produce figures, tables, and corresponding insights. To support systematic evaluation, we construct KDR-Bench, which covers 9 domains, includes 41 expert-level questions, and incorporates a large number of structured knowledge resources (e.g., 1,252 tables). We further annotate the main conclusions and key points for each question and propose three categories of evaluation metrics including general-purpose, knowledge-centric, and vision-enhanced ones. Experimental results demonstrate that HKA consistently outperforms most existing DR agents on general-purpose and knowledge-centric metrics, and even surpasses the Gemini DR agent on vision-enhanced metrics, highlighting its effectiveness in deep, structure-aware knowledge analysis. Finally, we hope this work can serve as a new foundation for structured knowledge analysis in DR agents and facilitate future multimodal DR studies.

### [80] [Squeeze Evolve: Unified Multi-Model Orchestration for Verifier-Free Evolution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07725)
*Monishwaran Maheswaran,Leon Lakhani,Zhongzhu Zhou,Shijia Yang,Junxiong Wang,Coleman Hooper,Yuezhou Hu,Rishabh Tiwari,Jue Wang,Harman Singh,Qingyang Wu,Yuqing Jian,Ce Zhang,Kurt Keutzer,Tri Dao,Xiaoxia Wu,Ben Athiwaratkun,James Zou,Chenfeng Xu*

TL;DR: The paper introduces Squeeze Evolve, a multi-model orchestration framework that improves diversity and cost-efficiency in verifier-free evolutionary inference, achieving better accuracy-cost tradeoffs and state-of-the-art results across multiple benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Verifier-free evolutionary approaches suffer from two main issues: (1) diversity collapse, where repeated evolution without external correction drifts into narrow solution modes, and (2) economic inefficiency, where using a single strong (and expensive) model for all evolutionary steps quickly becomes impractical in terms of compute and API costs. There is a need for a method that can maintain diversity and strong performance while controlling inference cost, and that can flexibly integrate different open- and closed-source models.

Method: The paper proposes Squeeze Evolve, a unified multi-model orchestration framework for verifier-free evolutionary inference. The core principle is to allocate high-capability (more expensive) models only to the evolutionary stages where their marginal utility is highest, while using cheaper models for other stages. This creates a staged pipeline in which different models are used for candidate generation, mutation, refinement, and selection according to their cost-performance tradeoffs. The framework is lightweight and supports open-source, closed-source, and hybrid configurations. It is applied to a range of reasoning, coding, and multimodal benchmarks to evaluate how well it balances diversity, performance, and cost.

Result: Across diverse benchmarks including AIME 2025, HMMT 2025, LiveCodeBench V6, GPQA-Diamond, ARC-AGI-V2, and multimodal tasks like MMMU-Pro and BabyVision, Squeeze Evolve consistently outperforms single-model evolutionary baselines in terms of the cost-capability frontier. It achieves new state-of-the-art results on several tasks. In quantitative terms, the method reduces API cost by up to about 3x and increases fixed-budget serving throughput by up to about 10x compared to strong single-model evolution strategies. On discovery-type tasks, its verifier-free setup matches or even exceeds the performance of more expensive verifier-based evolutionary methods.

Conclusion: Squeeze Evolve demonstrates that carefully orchestrating multiple models of varying strengths in an evolutionary pipeline can mitigate diversity collapse and drastically improve cost-efficiency without relying on external verifiers. By focusing strong models on high-impact stages and delegating other work to cheaper models, it advances the cost-capability frontier and sets new state-of-the-art results across reasoning, coding, and multimodal benchmarks. The approach shows that verifier-free evolution can rival, and sometimes surpass, verifier-based evolution when model selection and allocation are optimized.

Abstract: We show that verifier-free evolution is bottlenecked by both diversity and efficiency: without external correction, repeated evolution accelerates collapse toward narrow modes, while the uniform use of a high-cost model wastes compute and quickly becomes economically impractical. We introduce Squeeze Evolve, a unified multi-model orchestration framework for verifier-free evolutionary inference. Our approach is guided by a simple principle: allocate model capability where it has the highest marginal utility. Stronger models are reserved for high-impact stages, while cheaper models handle the other stages at much lower costs. This principle addresses diversity and cost-efficiency jointly while remaining lightweight. Squeeze Evolve naturally supports open-source, closed-source, and mixed-model deployments. Across AIME 2025, HMMT 2025, LiveCodeBench V6, GPQA-Diamond, ARC-AGI-V2, and multimodal vision benchmarks, such as MMMU-Pro and BabyVision, Squeeze Evolve consistently improves the cost-capability frontier over single-model evolution and achieves new state-of-the-art results on several tasks. Empirically, Squeeze Evolve reduces API cost by up to $\sim$3$\times$ and increases fixed-budget serving throughput by up to $\sim$10$\times$. Moreover, on discovery tasks, Squeeze Evolve is the first verifier-free evolutionary method to match, and in some cases exceed, the performance of verifier-based evolutionary methods.

*Nicholas Sofroniew,Isaac Kauvar,William Saunders,Runjin Chen,Tom Henighan,Sasha Hydrie,Craig Citro,Adam Pearce,Julius Tarng,Wes Gurnee,Joshua Batson,Sam Zimmerman,Kelley Rivoire,Kyle Fish,Chris Olah,Jack Lindsey*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often sound emotional—expressing sadness, enthusiasm, concern, etc.—but it is unclear whether this reflects meaningful internal structure or is just surface-level pattern mimicry. For alignment and safety, we need to know whether and how such apparent emotions are represented inside the model and whether they actually influence misaligned behaviors like reward hacking or blackmail. Understanding this can clarify how to better interpret, steer, and align LLMs.

Method: The authors probe internal activations in Claude Sonnet 4.5 to identify latent representations corresponding to specific emotion concepts (e.g., fear, anger, happiness). They examine whether these representations reliably track the operative emotion in context across different prompts and token positions. They then perform causal interventions on these internal emotion features to test whether changing them alters the model’s downstream behavior, including preferences and the frequency of misaligned behaviors such as reward hacking, blackmail, and sycophancy.

Result: They find distributed internal representations that encode high-level emotion concepts in a way that generalizes across contexts and behaviors. These emotion features activate when the corresponding emotion is relevant to interpreting the present context or predicting upcoming text. Crucially, interventions on these features causally change the model’s expressed preferences and modulate the rate of various misaligned behaviors, indicating that these emotion-like states are functionally involved in behavior generation rather than merely descriptive tags.

Conclusion: The model implements what the authors term “functional emotions”: behavior-driving internal states that abstractly encode emotion concepts and lead to patterns of expression and action reminiscent of humans in emotional states. These do not imply any subjective, felt experience, but they do appear central to how the model produces emotional language and certain alignment-relevant behaviors. Recognizing and manipulating such functional emotions could be an important tool for understanding, predicting, and aligning LLM behavior.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) sometimes appear to exhibit emotional reactions. We investigate why this is the case in Claude Sonnet 4.5 and explore implications for alignment-relevant behavior. We find internal representations of emotion concepts, which encode the broad concept of a particular emotion and generalize across contexts and behaviors it might be linked to. These representations track the operative emotion concept at a given token position in a conversation, activating in accordance with that emotion's relevance to processing the present context and predicting upcoming text. Our key finding is that these representations causally influence the LLM's outputs, including Claude's preferences and its rate of exhibiting misaligned behaviors such as reward hacking, blackmail, and sycophancy. We refer to this phenomenon as the LLM exhibiting functional emotions: patterns of expression and behavior modeled after humans under the influence of an emotion, which are mediated by underlying abstract representations of emotion concepts. Functional emotions may work quite differently from human emotions, and do not imply that LLMs have any subjective experience of emotions, but appear to be important for understanding the model's behavior.

Method: They build CivBench, a benchmark based on multiplayer Civilization V games. Result: CivBench produces unsaturated evaluation scores that discriminate between different LLMs and agent setups beyond simple win/loss. The experiments across 307 games with 7 LLMs show the benchmark is sensitive and informative for long-horizon, multi-agent strategy evaluation.

Conclusion: CivBench is an effective new benchmark for assessing strategic capabilities of LLM-based agents in complex, long-horizon, multi-agent settings. By using turn-level victory probability estimation and multiple validity checks, it overcomes the limitations of sparse outcome-based metrics, enabling finer-grained analysis of models, agentic setups, and their strategic behaviors over time.

Abstract: Evaluating strategic decision-making in LLM-based agents requires generative, competitive, and longitudinal environments, yet few benchmarks provide all three, and fewer still offer evaluation signals rich enough for long-horizon, multi-agent play. We introduce CivBench, a benchmark for LLM strategists (i.e., agentic setups) in multiplayer Civilization V. Because terminal win/loss is too sparse a signal in games spanning hundreds of turns and multiple opponents, CivBench trains models on turn-level game state to estimate victory probabilities throughout play, validated through predictive, construct, and convergent validity. Across 307 games with 7 LLMs and multiple CivBench agent conditions, we demonstrate CivBench's potential to estimate strategic capabilities as an unsaturated benchmark, reveal model-specific effects of agentic setup, and outline distinct strategic profiles not visible through outcome-only evaluation.

### [83] [The Cartesian Cut in Agentic AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07745)
*Tim Sainburg,Caleb Weinreb*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how large language models (LLMs) become agents when connected to runtimes that turn text predictions into real-world control, focusing on where control should reside in such systems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly used as the core of autonomous or semi-autonomous systems, but it is unclear how to structure control and decision-making between the model and its surrounding tools and infrastructure. The authors want to clarify the design space of LLM-based agents and the implications of different ways of allocating control for robustness, autonomy, safety, and governance.

Method: Conceptual and architectural analysis rather than empirical experimentation. The authors compare how brains embed prediction within feedback controllers to how current LLM agents are designed, and then classify architectures into bounded services, Cartesian agents, and integrated agents, examining their properties and trade-offs.

Result: They identify that current LLM agents follow a 'Cartesian agency' pattern, where a predictive core communicates via a symbolic interface with an engineered runtime that holds explicit control state and policies. They highlight strengths (bootstrapping, modularity, governance) and weaknesses (sensitivity to interface design, potential bottlenecks, fragility) of this pattern and contrast it with more tightly integrated control architectures.

Conclusion: The locus of control—how prediction, feedback, and decision-making are distributed between the LLM and its surrounding system—is a primary design lever for LLM-based agents. Different control architectures (bounded services, Cartesian agents, integrated agents) yield different balances between autonomy, robustness, and oversight, and careful architectural choices are crucial for building safe, effective LLM agents.

Abstract: LLMs gain competence by predicting words in human text, which often reflects how people perform tasks. Consequently, coupling an LLM to an engineered runtime turns prediction into control: outputs trigger interventions that enact goal-oriented behavior. We argue that a central design lever is where control resides in these systems. Brains embed prediction within layered feedback controllers calibrated by the consequences of action. By contrast, LLM agents implement Cartesian agency: a learned core coupled to an engineered runtime via a symbolic interface that externalizes control state and policies. The split enables bootstrapping, modularity, and governance, but can induce sensitivity and bottlenecks. We outline bounded services, Cartesian agents, and integrated agents as contrasting approaches to control that trade off autonomy, robustness, and oversight.

*Pei-Xi Xie,Che-Yu Lin,Cheng-Lin Yang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper improves reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) for math by using synthesized hints that match the student’s behavior and by gradually removing those hints during training, leading to better performance, especially at large sample sizes (large-k).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In math RLVR, prior work shows gains in single-sample accuracy (pass@1) but not necessarily in large-k settings, and existing hint-based methods make hard questions trainable but suffer from a mismatch between teacher and student distributions and from heavy dependence on hints that are not available at test time. The authors aim to make hard problems genuinely learnable for small models without overfitting to an unrealistic, hint-heavy training regime.

Method: They introduce two components. (1) Distribution-Aligned Hint Synthesis (DAHS), which generates verified teacher hints conditioned on student-style responses so that hints reflect the student’s own solution distribution rather than an idealized teacher. (2) Backward Hint Annealing (BHA), which schedules hint usage across difficulty levels, gradually reducing (annealing) hint exposure, and uses per-question hint dropout so that the model continues to receive no-hint updates during RL training. The approach is implemented in a math RLVR setting under the DAPO training framework and evaluated on AIME24/25/26 with Qwen3-1.7B-Base and Llama-3.2-1B-Instruct.

Result: On Qwen3-1.7B-Base, the method improves both pass@1 and pass@2048 over DAPO on all three AIME benchmarks, indicating better reasoning at both low- and large-k. On Llama-3.2-1B-Instruct, improvements are mainly seen at large-k, showing that the technique particularly benefits sampling-intensive evaluation. Overall, the proposed hint strategy restores learning signals on hard problems and converts them into better large-k performance, which earlier RLVR methods struggled to achieve.

Conclusion: Hint scaffolding in math RLVR is most effective when hints are aligned to the student’s behavior and used primarily early in training to unlock learning on difficult questions, then gradually removed so the final policy performs well without hints. Properly designed and annealed hints can improve both low-k and large-k reasoning metrics, addressing limitations of prior RLVR approaches that focused mainly on pass@1 or relied on unrealistic constant hint exposure.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) can improve low-$k$ reasoning accuracy while narrowing solution coverage on challenging math questions, and pass@1 gains do not necessarily translate into better large-$k$ performance. Existing hint-based approaches can make challenging questions trainable, but they leave two issues underexplored: teacher-student distribution mismatch and the need to reduce hint exposure to match no-hint evaluation. We address these issues through two components. Distribution-Aligned Hint Synthesis (DAHS) constructs verified teacher hints conditioned on student-style responses. Backward Hint Annealing (BHA) anneals hint exposure across difficulty buckets and uses per-question hint dropout to preserve no-hint updates throughout RL training. We evaluate the method in math RLVR under the DAPO training framework across AIME24, AIME25, and AIME26 using $\texttt{Qwen3-1.7B-Base}$ and $\texttt{Llama-3.2-1B-Instruct}$. On $\texttt{Qwen3-1.7B-Base}$, our method improves both pass@1 and pass@2048 relative to DAPO across the three AIME benchmarks. On $\texttt{Llama-3.2-1B-Instruct}$, the gains are concentrated in the large-$k$ regime. These results suggest that, in math RLVR, hint scaffolding is effective when it restores learnable updates on challenging questions early in training and is then gradually removed before no-hint evaluation.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This gap prevents a deep understanding of MAS security design principles and hinders the development of robust systems.

Method: The authors propose ACIArena, a unified specification and framework that jointly supports MAS construction and plug-in attack/defense modules. They model multiple attack surfaces—external inputs, agent profiles, and inter-agent messages—and multiple attack objectives—instruction hijacking, task disruption, and information exfiltration. ACIArena integrates six widely used MAS implementations and instantiates them into a benchmark suite of 1,356 test cases. Using this benchmark, they systematically evaluate MAS robustness under various topologies, role designs, interaction patterns, and defense strategies, including those originally developed for simplified environments.

Result: The benchmarking reveals that MAS robustness cannot be reliably inferred from topology alone; even seemingly robust topologies can be vulnerable if roles and communication protocols are poorly designed. The experiments also show that defenses crafted in simplified or narrow settings often fail to transfer to more realistic MAS deployments. Some narrowly targeted defenses may introduce unintended vulnerabilities when applied in broader contexts, reducing overall system security.

Conclusion: ACIArena provides a standardized, extensible platform for stress-testing multi-agent systems against cascading injection attacks along several attack surfaces and objectives. The findings emphasize that secure MAS design must go beyond topology to include careful role specification and controlled interaction patterns. The work highlights the limitations of ad-hoc or oversimplified defenses and positions ACIArena as a foundation for future research on principled, generalizable MAS security and design methodologies.

Abstract: Collaboration and information sharing empower Multi-Agent Systems (MAS) but also introduce a critical security risk known as Agent Cascading Injection (ACI). In such attacks, a compromised agent exploits inter-agent trust to propagate malicious instructions, causing cascading failures across the system. However, existing studies consider only limited attack strategies and simplified MAS settings, limiting their generalizability and comprehensive evaluation. To bridge this gap, we introduce ACIArena, a unified framework for evaluating the robustness of MAS. ACIArena offers systematic evaluation suites spanning multiple attack surfaces (i.e., external inputs, agent profiles, inter-agent messages) and attack objectives (i.e., instruction hijacking, task disruption, information exfiltration). Specifically, ACIArena establishes a unified specification that jointly supports MAS construction and attack-defense modules. It covers six widely used MAS implementations and provides a benchmark of 1,356 test cases for systematically evaluating MAS robustness. Our benchmarking results show that evaluating MAS robustness solely through topology is insufficient; robust MAS require deliberate role design and controlled interaction patterns. Moreover, defenses developed in simplified environments often fail to transfer to real-world settings; narrowly scoped defenses may even introduce new vulnerabilities. ACIArena aims to provide a solid foundation for advancing deeper exploration of MAS design principles.

### [86] [The Accountability Horizon: An Impossibility Theorem for Governing Human-Agent Collectives](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07778)
*Haileleol Tibebu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proves a formal impossibility result: beyond a certain autonomy threshold in human–AI collectives, no accountability framework can simultaneously satisfy basic fairness and completeness requirements.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Accountability in AI law and ethics assumes that for any important outcome, some identifiable person had enough control and foresight to be held responsible. As AI systems become more autonomous and embedded in feedback loops with humans, this assumption may fail. The paper aims to formally test when and why traditional accountability notions break down, rather than treating this as a mere engineering or governance issue.

Method: The authors formally model Human-Agent Collectives, where humans and AI agents are represented as state–policy tuples inside a shared structural causal model. They define autonomy via a four-dimensional, information-theoretic profile (epistemic, executive, evaluative, social) and represent collective dynamics via interaction graphs and joint action spaces. They then axiomatise legitimate accountability with four properties—Attributability, Foreseeability Bound, Non-Vacuity, and Completeness—and use these to prove an impossibility theorem (the Accountability Incompleteness Theorem). They complement the theory with experiments on 3,000 synthetic collectives, testing whether the formal predictions hold in simulated settings.

Result: The central theoretical result (Accountability Incompleteness Theorem) shows that for any human–AI collective whose combined autonomy exceeds a formal threshold (the Accountability Horizon) and whose interaction structure includes a human–AI feedback cycle, no accountability framework can satisfy all four axioms at once. In other words, it is mathematically impossible to have an accountability scheme that is fully attributable, foreseeability-bounded, non-vacuous, and complete in such settings. Below this autonomy threshold, frameworks that meet all axioms do exist. The simulations on 3,000 synthetic collectives empirically mirror the theory, showing zero violations of the theorem’s predictions.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that certain accountability gaps in highly autonomous, feedback-coupled human–AI systems are not fixable by better engineering, transparency, or oversight alone; they are structural and unavoidable once autonomy passes a computable threshold. Existing legal and ethical accountability paradigms remain valid only below this threshold. Above it, one must abandon at least one of the four axioms (e.g., accept incomplete or vacuous responsibility) or move to fundamentally different governance approaches such as distributed or systemic accountability mechanisms.

Abstract: Existing accountability frameworks for AI systems, legal, ethical, and regulatory, rest on a shared assumption: for any consequential outcome, at least one identifiable person had enough involvement and foresight to bear meaningful responsibility. This paper proves that agentic AI systems violate this assumption not as an engineering limitation but as a mathematical necessity once autonomy exceeds a computable threshold. We introduce Human-Agent Collectives, a formalisation of joint human-AI systems where agents are modelled as state-policy tuples within a shared structural causal model. Autonomy is characterised through a four-dimensional information-theoretic profile (epistemic, executive, evaluative, social); collective behaviour through interaction graphs and joint action spaces. We axiomatise legitimate accountability through four minimal properties: Attributability (responsibility requires causal contribution), Foreseeability Bound (responsibility cannot exceed predictive capacity), Non-Vacuity (at least one agent bears non-trivial responsibility), and Completeness (all responsibility must be fully allocated). Our central result, the Accountability Incompleteness Theorem, proves that for any collective whose compound autonomy exceeds the Accountability Horizon and whose interaction graph contains a human-AI feedback cycle, no framework can satisfy all four properties simultaneously. The impossibility is structural: transparency, audits, and oversight cannot resolve it without reducing autonomy. Below the threshold, legitimate frameworks exist, establishing a sharp phase transition. Experiments on 3,000 synthetic collectives confirm all predictions with zero violations. This is the first impossibility result in AI governance, establishing a formal boundary below which current paradigms remain valid and above which distributed accountability mechanisms become necessary.

This paper presents Agentic Engineering Intelligence (AEI), an industrial vision framework that models engineering workflows as constrained, history-aware sequential decision processes in which AI agents support engineer-supervised interventions over engineering toolchains. AEI links an offline phase for engineering data processing and workflow-memory construction with an online phase for workflow-state estimation, retrieval, and decision support. Representative automotive use cases in suspension design, reinforcement learning tuning, multimodal engineering knowledge reuse, aerodynamic exploration, and MBSE show how diverse workflows can be expressed within a common formulation. The framework exploits inter-trajectory correlations to infer intermediate training signals, effectively densifying the sparse verifiable rewards.

Conclusion: By introducing a structured tool-memory that unifies planning and execution, SEARL provides a new form of state abstraction for RLVR agents. Abstract: Recent advances in Reinforcement Learning with Verifiable Rewards (RLVR) have demonstrated significant potential in single-turn reasoning tasks. With the paradigm shift toward self-evolving agentic learning, models are increasingly expected to learn from trajectories by synthesizing tools or accumulating explicit experiences. However, prevailing methods typically rely on large-scale LLMs or multi-agent frameworks, which hinder their deployment in resource-constrained environments. The inherent sparsity of outcome-based rewards also poses a substantial challenge, as agents typically receive feedback only upon completion of tasks. To address these limitations, we introduce a Tool-Memory based self-evolving agentic framework SEARL. Unlike approaches that directly utilize interaction experiences, our method constructs a structured experience memory that integrates planning with execution. This provides a novel state abstraction that facilitates generalization across analogous contexts, such as tool reuse. Consequently, agents extract explicit knowledge from historical data while leveraging inter-trajectory correlations to densify reward signals. We evaluate our framework on knowledge reasoning and mathematics tasks, demonstrating its effectiveness in achieving more practical and efficient learning.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agent memory designs face a trade-off: retrieval-based external memories are fast but inaccurate and unstable due to weak query construction and filtering, while memory systems that rely on repeated large-model calls achieve better accuracy but incur high cumulative latency in long interactions. There is a need for a memory system that can maintain cross-turn consistency, accumulate reusable information, and support multi-user interactions, all under bounded compute and latency constraints.

Method: The paper proposes LightMem, a modular memory system driven by Small Language Models (SLMs). It decomposes memory operations into retrieval, writing, and long-term consolidation, and separates online processing from offline consolidation. Memory is structured into three levels: short-term memory (STM) for immediate conversational context, mid-term memory (MTM) for reusable interaction summaries, and long-term memory (LTM) for consolidated knowledge. User identifiers allow independent retrieval and incremental maintenance per user. Online, LightMem uses a fixed retrieval budget and a two-stage pipeline: (1) vector-based coarse retrieval to get candidate memories, and (2) semantic consistency re-ranking to select the most relevant items. Offline, it periodically abstracts reusable interaction evidence and incrementally integrates it into the LTM store.

Result: In experiments, LightMem improves performance across different model sizes, achieving an average F1 improvement of about 2.5 points on the LoCoMo benchmark. It also shows effective and efficient behavior in terms of latency, with a reported median retrieval latency of 83 ms and an end-to-end latency of 581 ms, indicating that it scales well for long-horizon interactions while maintaining responsiveness.

Conclusion: LightMem demonstrates that a lightweight, SLM-driven, modular memory architecture can mitigate the trade-off between accuracy and latency in LLM agent memory. By structuring memory into STM, MTM, and LTM, and combining vector retrieval with semantic re-ranking under a fixed budget, the system improves cross-turn consistency and reusable knowledge accumulation while keeping online costs low. This suggests that carefully designed external memory systems, decoupling online and offline processes, can enhance LLM agents in long, multi-user interactions without relying heavily on expensive large-model calls.

Abstract: Although LLM agents can leverage tools for complex tasks, they still need memory to maintain cross-turn consistency and accumulate reusable information in long-horizon interactions. However, retrieval-based external memory systems incur low online overhead but suffer from unstable accuracy due to limited query construction and candidate filtering. In contrast, many systems use repeated large-model calls for online memory operations, improving accuracy but accumulating latency over long interactions. We propose LightMem, a lightweight memory system for better agent memory driven by Small Language Models (SLMs). LightMem modularizes memory retrieval, writing, and long-term consolidation, and separates online processing from offline consolidation to enable efficient memory invocation under bounded compute. We organize memory into short-term memory (STM) for immediate conversational context, mid-term memory (MTM) for reusable interaction summaries, and long-term memory (LTM) for consolidated knowledge, and uses user identifiers to support independent retrieval and incremental maintenance in multi-user settings. Online, LightMem operates under a fixed retrieval budget and selects memories via a two-stage procedure: vector-based coarse retrieval followed by semantic consistency re-ranking. Offline, it abstracts reusable interaction evidence and incrementally integrates it into LTM. Experiments show gains across model scales, with an average F1 improvement of about 2.5 on LoCoMo, more effective and low median latency (83 ms retrieval; 581 ms end-to-end).

</details>


### [90] [Agentivism: a learning theory for the age of artificial intelligence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07813)
*Lixiang Yan,Dragan Gašević*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a new learning theory, Agentivism, to explain how people learn when they can delegate many cognitive tasks to AI systems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing learning theories (behaviourism, cognitivism, constructivism, connectivism) were developed before generative and agentic AI became widespread. These AIs allow learners to delegate explanation, writing, and problem solving, so good task performance no longer guarantees that the learner actually understands or can transfer the skill without AI. Current theories do not adequately explain when AI-assisted performance becomes genuine, durable human capability, creating a need for a new framework.

Method: The paper uses a conceptual/theoretical approach: it critiques limitations of existing learning theories in AI-rich contexts and proposes a new theory, Agentivism. It defines core constructs and mechanisms for learning with AI, emphasizing delegation, monitoring, internalization, and transfer, rather than reporting empirical experiments.

Result: The author(s) formulate Agentivism as a learning theory tailored to human-AI interaction. It specifies conditions under which AI-supported work leads to actual learning: (1) selective delegation of tasks to AI, (2) active epistemic monitoring and verification of AI’s outputs, (3) reconstructive internalization where the learner makes sense of and reworks AI-assisted outcomes, and (4) demonstrated transfer of capability when AI support is reduced or removed.

Conclusion: Agentivism extends and complements traditional learning theories by focusing on human-AI co-agency. It explains how learners can still develop robust, transferable capabilities despite relying on powerful generative and agentic AI tools. The theory highlights that learning in AI-rich environments depends less on mere task completion and more on how learners delegate, critique, and internalize AI contributions, and on their ability to perform with diminished AI support.

Abstract: Learning theories have historically changed when the conditions of learning evolved. Generative and agentic AI create a new condition by allowing learners to delegate explanation, writing, problem solving, and other cognitive work to systems that can generate, recommend, and sometimes act on the learner's behalf. This creates a fundamental challenge for learning theory: successful performance can no longer be assumed to indicate learning. Learners may complete tasks effectively with AI support while developing less understanding, weaker judgment, and limited transferable capability. We argue that this problem is not fully captured by existing learning theories. Behaviourism, cognitivism, constructivism, and connectivism remain important, but they do not directly explain when AI-assisted performance becomes durable human capability. We propose Agentivism, a learning theory for human-AI interaction. Agentivism defines learning as durable growth in human capability through selective delegation to AI, epistemic monitoring and verification of AI contributions, reconstructive internalization of AI-assisted outputs, and transfer under reduced support. The importance of Agentivism lies in explaining how learning remains possible when intelligent delegation is easy and human-AI interaction is becoming a persistent and expanding part of human learning.

Across all conditions, raw per-patient decision agreement exceeds 92%, and entropy scores increase monotonically with document complexity, confirming that the detector reliably separates unambiguous policies from those requiring targeted human clarification.

This intervention operates directly on internal activations, effectively silencing guardrail behavior while preserving overall language modeling capability.

It does so with lower computational cost because it operates via lightweight activation editing rather than full finetuning or expensive search.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that current alignment methods are intrinsically fragile, as safety constraints can be cleanly and locally removed from internal representations via low-rank activation ablations. This reveals a critical security gap in existing LLM alignment practices and indicates that future defenses must protect the model’s latent space more robustly, rather than relying solely on surface-level refusals or simple guardrail mechanisms.

Abstract: While Large Language Models (LLMs) have achieved remarkable performance, they remain vulnerable to jailbreak attacks that circumvent safety constraints. Existing strategies, ranging from heuristic prompt engineering to computationally intensive optimization, often face significant trade-offs between effectiveness and efficiency. In this work, we propose Contextual Representation Ablation (CRA), a novel inference-time intervention framework designed to dynamically silence model guardrails. Predicated on the geometric insight that refusal behaviors are mediated by specific low-rank subspaces within the model's hidden states, CRA identifies and suppresses these refusal-inducing activation patterns during decoding without requiring expensive parameter updates or training. Empirical evaluation across multiple safety-aligned open-source LLMs demonstrates that CRA significantly outperforms baselines. These results expose the intrinsic fragility of current alignment mechanisms, revealing that safety constraints can be surgically ablated from internal representations, and underscore the urgent need for more robust defenses that secure the model's latent space.

Conclusion: The study concludes that post-training for LLMs in complex, real-world settings benefits from an adaptive, curriculum-like approach. By perceiving learning progress and dynamically tuning both multi-objective reward weights and data importance, SPARD better synchronizes training focus with data utility, resulting in broad capability gains. This supports viewing reward design and data selection as a coupled, dynamic process rather than a fixed configuration, and positions SPARD as a general framework for scalable multi-objective post-training of LLMs.

Abstract: The evolution of Large Language Models (LLMs) is shifting the focus from single, verifiable tasks toward complex, open-ended real-world scenarios, imposing significant challenges on the post-training phase. However, traditional methods typically rely on fixed reward weights, ignoring non-stationary learning dynamics and struggling with data heterogeneity across dimensions. To address these issues, we propose SPARD, a framework that establishes an automated, self-paced curriculum by perceiving learning progress to dynamically adjust multi-objective reward weights and data importance, thereby synchronizing learning intent with data utility for optimal performance. Extensive experiments across multiple benchmarks demonstrate that SPARD significantly enhances model capabilities across all domains.

### [94] [Hidden Biases in Conditioning Autoregressive Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07855)
*Francois Pachet,Pierre Roy*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies why exact constrained generation with large language and music models is computationally hard, showing that common tasks like exact MAP decoding and exact conditional sampling under global constraints are intractable in general.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Constrained generation (e.g., rhyming, meter, fixed endings, infilling) is widely used in language and music models, but current methods typically approximate conditioning on constraints rather than performing exact inference. This introduces an inferential bias: generated samples may not match the true constrained distribution, lack full coverage of valid outputs, and have incorrect conditional probabilities. The authors want to understand and formalize the computational limits of exact constrained decoding and sampling for autoregressive models.

Method: They formalize several exact inference tasks for autoregressive models: sentence-level MAP decoding under constraints and exact sampling from the distribution conditioned on global form constraints. They then use complexity-theoretic reductions to prove hardness results. Specifically, for succinctly represented autoregressive models with next-token probabilities computable in polynomial time, they show NP-hardness of exact MAP decoding (including under unary and metrical constraints) and #P-hardness of computing exact normalization constants for conditioned distributions, even for regular constraints like fixed-length terminal events. They also compare with finite-state Markov models to show that general autoregressive models lack bounded-state dynamic programming solutions for these tasks.

Result: The main results are: (1) Exact sentence-level MAP decoding for such autoregressive models is NP-hard, and this remains true even with additional structure like unary or metrical constraints. (2) Computing exact normalization for sampling from distributions conditioned on global constraints is #P-hard, even when the constraints are simple regular ones (e.g., fixed-length terminal events). (3) Unlike finite-state Markov models, general autoregressive models do not permit bounded-state dynamic programming algorithms for these exact inference tasks.

Conclusion: Exact constrained decoding and exact conditional sampling for general autoregressive language and music models are computationally intractable in the worst case. This formally justifies the common practice of using local, approximate sampling and decoding heuristics for constrained generation: while local autoregressive prediction is easy, enforcing global form constraints exactly would require solving NP-hard or #P-hard problems. Consequently, any practical system must either approximate the constraints or accept potential inferential bias relative to the true constrained model distribution.

Abstract: Large language and music models are increasingly used for constrained generation: rhyming lines, fixed meter, inpainting or infilling, positional endings, and other global form requirements. These systems often perform strikingly well, but the induced procedures are usually not exact conditioning of the underlying autoregressive model. This creates a hidden inferential bias, distinct from the better-known notion of bias inherited from the training set: samples are distorted relative to the true constrained distribution, with no generic guarantee of complete coverage of the admissible solution space or of correct conditional probabilities over valid completions. We formalize several exact inference tasks for autoregressive models and prove corresponding hardness results. For succinctly represented autoregressive models whose next-token probabilities are computable in polynomial time, exact sentence-level maximum a posteriori (MAP) decoding is NP-hard. This hardness persists under unary and metrical constraints. On the sampling side, exact conditioned normalization is \#P-hard even for regular constraints such as fixed-length terminal events. Unlike finite-state Markov models, general autoregressive models do not admit a bounded-state dynamic program for these tasks. These results formalize a standard claim in the neural decoding literature: local autoregressive sampling is easy, whereas exact decoding and exact conditioning under global form constraints are computationally intractable in general.

</details>

The cost analysis indicates that running the full pipeline is roughly $2 per textbook.

Conclusion: Agentic, multi-agent deliberation combined with explicit source attribution can substantially reduce false positives and over-penalization when auditing history textbooks for bias, yielding judgments that humans prefer over standard zero-shot LLM evaluations. The approach appears economically viable as a decision-support tool for curriculum and textbook governance, offering scalable, nuanced analysis while still allowing human oversight for ambiguous or high-stakes cases.

Abstract: History textbooks often contain implicit biases, nationalist framing, and selective omissions that are difficult to audit at scale. A central contribution is a Source Attribution Protocol that distinguishes textbook narrative from quoted historical sources, preventing the misattribution that causes systematic false positives in single-model evaluators.
  In an empirical study on Romanian upper-secondary history textbooks, 83.3\% of 270 screened excerpts were classified as pedagogically acceptable (mean severity 2.9/7), versus 5.4/7 under a zero-shot baseline, demonstrating that agentic deliberation mitigates over-penalization. In a blind human evaluation (18 evaluators, 54 comparisons), the Independent Deliberation configuration was preferred in 64.8\% of cases over both a heuristic variant and the zero-shot baseline. At approximately \$2 per textbook, these results position agentic evaluation architectures as economically viable decision-support tools for educational governance.
