### [98] [Capture-Quiet Decomposition: A Verification Theorem for Chess Endgame Tablebases](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07907)
*Alexander Pavlov*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Capture-Quiet Decomposition (CQD) is a structural theorem that certifies the correctness of Win-Draw-Loss (WDL) labels in chess endgame tablebases by partitioning positions into terminal, capture, and quiet cases and imposing specific consistency checks on each.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Verifying that huge chess endgame tablebases are correctly labeled with win/draw/loss outcomes is challenging, especially because standard retrograde analysis can admit trivial, self-consistent but wrong labelings (like labeling every position as a draw). A principled, structural characterization is needed so that correctness can be checked modularly and without circular reasoning, especially at large scale and across different piece counts.

Method: The authors introduce Capture-Quiet Decomposition (CQD), which classifies every legal position into exactly one of three mutually exclusive categories: terminal (game already decided), capture (side to move can capture a piece), or quiet (no captures available). They then prove a theorem: a WDL labeling is correct if and only if three conditions hold simultaneously: (1) all terminal positions have the correct labels by chess rules; (2) capture positions are consistent with already-verified tablebases that have fewer pieces (after executing the capture); and (3) quiet positions obey retrograde consistency conditions within the same endgame (i.e., dynamic programming-style consistency with successors and predecessors). They build a decomposed verifier based on these checks and compare it with a standard full retrograde verification baseline.

Result: They apply CQD-based verification exhaustively to all known three- and four-piece endgames (35 endgames, about 42 million positions), as well as all 110 five-piece and all 372 six-piece endgames, for a total of 517 distinct endgames. Across this entire testbed, the decomposed CQD verifier reports exactly the same number of labeling violations as a traditional full retrograde baseline, demonstrating that the decomposition is both sound and practically equivalent to full retrograde verification in terms of error detection.

Conclusion: Capture-Quiet Decomposition provides a complete and non-circular structural characterization of when WDL labels in chess endgame tablebases are correct. By tying capture positions to externally verified smaller tablebases and confining retrograde consistency checks to quiet positions, CQD removes the possibility of trivial fixpoints and enables scalable, modular verification. Empirical tests on all three- through six-piece endgames show that CQD-based verification matches full retrograde analysis in violation detection, supporting its validity and practical utility.

Abstract: We present the Capture-Quiet Decomposition (CQD), a structural theorem for verifying Win-Draw-Loss (WDL) labelings of chess endgame tablebases. The theorem decomposes every legal position into exactly one of three categories -- terminal, capture, or quiet -- and shows that a WDL labeling is correct if and only if: (1) terminal positions are labeled correctly, (2) capture positions are consistent with verified sub-models of smaller piece count, and (3) quiet positions satisfy retrograde consistency within the same endgame. The key insight is that capture positions anchor the labeling to externally verified sub-models, breaking the circularity that allows trivial fixpoints (such as the all-draw labeling) to satisfy self-consistency alone. We validate CQD exhaustively on all 35 three- and four-piece endgames (42 million positions), all 110 five-piece endgames, and all 372 six-piece endgames -- 517 endgames in total -- with the decomposed verifier producing identical violation counts to a full retrograde baseline in every case.

</details>

*Boer Zhang,Mingyan Wu,Dongzhuoran Zhou,Yuqicheng Zhu,Wendong Fan,Puzhen Zhang,Zifeng Ding,Guohao Li,Yuan He*

Main category: cs.AI

  <summary>Details</summary>

Inspired by Anthropic's "think" tool paradigm and classical information-retrieval insights, the authors aim to design a more systematic way for agents to plan queries, track progress, and handle long web pages.

Method: The authors design Q+, a toolkit that structures how an AI agent interacts with web search. Q+ provides tools for query planning, monitoring search coverage and progress, and extracting and organizing evidence from long web snapshots. They integrate Q+ into the browser sub-agent of Eigent, an open-source multi-agent system for computer use, producing EigentSearch-Q+. They then evaluate this system on four deep research benchmarks (SimpleQA-Verified, FRAMES, WebWalkerQA, and X-Bench DeepSearch) using different model backends (GPT-4.1, GPT-5.1, Minimax M2.5).

Result: When Q+ is integrated into Eigent's browser agent, benchmark-size-weighted average accuracy improves by 3.0 percentage points for GPT-4.1, 3.8 pp for GPT-5.1, and 0.6 pp for Minimax M2.5 across four deep research benchmarks. Qualitative case studies show that the enhanced system produces more coherent tool-use trajectories, with search progress and evidence handling made explicit rather than implicit.

Conclusion: Structured query and evidence-processing tools like Q+ can make web search for deep research agents more deliberate and effective. By guiding query planning, tracking search progress, and explicitly organizing evidence from long web pages, Q+ improves both quantitative performance and qualitative coherence of AI agents' tool-use in deep research settings. This validates the value of explicit search and evidence-handling abstractions within multi-agent AI systems.

Abstract: Deep research requires reasoning over web evidence to answer open-ended questions, and it is a core capability for AI agents. Yet many deep research agents still rely on implicit, unstructured search behavior that causes redundant exploration and brittle evidence aggregation. Motivated by Anthropic's "think" tool paradigm and insights from the information-retrieval literature, we introduce Q+, a set of query and evidence processing tools that make web search more deliberate by guiding query planning, monitoring search progress, and extracting evidence from long web snapshots. We integrate Q+ into the browser sub-agent of Eigent, an open-source, production-ready multi-agent workforce for computer use, yielding EigentSearch-Q+. Across four benchmarks (SimpleQA-Verified, FRAMES, WebWalkerQA, and X-Bench DeepSearch), Q+ improves Eigent's browser agent benchmark-size-weighted average accuracy by 3.0, 3.8, and 0.6 percentage points (pp) for GPT-4.1, GPT-5.1, and Minimax M2.5 model backends, respectively. Case studies further suggest that EigentSearch-Q+ produces more coherent tool-calling trajectories by making search progress and evidence handling explicit.

### [101] [WorldMAP: Bootstrapping Vision-Language Navigation Trajectory Prediction with Generative World Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07957)
*Hongjin Chen,Shangyun Jiang,Tonghua Su,Chen Gao,Xinlei Chen,Yong Li,Zhibo Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces WorldMAP, a teacher–student framework that uses generative world models to create structured supervision signals for training vision-language-based navigation policies, achieving strong trajectory prediction performance on Target-Bench.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Vision-language models and world models are promising for embodied navigation, but existing approaches struggle to reliably predict navigation trajectories from a single egocentric view. VLM-based planners often produce unstable trajectories, while generative world models only synthesize plausible futures without grounded, directly usable supervision for navigation learning. There is a need for a method that can convert imagined future observations into reliable, grounded supervision for trajectory prediction.

Method: WorldMAP uses a world-model-driven teacher to generate videos of plausible futures, from which it constructs a persistent semantic-spatial memory. This teacher grounds task-relevant targets and obstacles in the imagined scenes and runs an explicit planner to derive pseudo-label navigation trajectories. A lightweight student model, which takes vision-language inputs and outputs multiple trajectory hypotheses, is trained on these pseudo-labels to directly predict navigation trajectories without running the world model at test time.

Result: On the Target-Bench benchmark, WorldMAP achieves state-of-the-art performance in trajectory prediction metrics. It reduces Average Displacement Error (ADE) by 18.0% and Final Displacement Error (FDE) by 42.1% compared to the strongest baseline. Additionally, it boosts the Dynamic Time Warping (DTW) performance of a small open-source VLM to a level competitive with larger proprietary models.

Conclusion: The study concludes that, for embodied navigation, the main value of generative world models may be in producing structured supervision signals—such as semantic-spatial maps and planned trajectories—rather than directly supplying action-ready imagined rollouts. By distilling world-model-generated futures into a teacher–student framework, WorldMAP yields more stable and accurate trajectory prediction from vision-language inputs and narrows the performance gap between open-source and proprietary VLMs.

Abstract: Vision-language models (VLMs) and generative world models are opening new opportunities for embodied navigation. VLMs are increasingly used as direct planners or trajectory predictors, while world models support look-ahead reasoning by imagining future views. Yet predicting a reliable trajectory from a single egocentric observation remains challenging. Current VLMs often generate unstable trajectories, and world models, though able to synthesize plausible futures, do not directly provide the grounded signals needed for navigation learning. This raises a central question: how can generated futures be turned into supervision for grounded trajectory prediction? We present WorldMAP, a teacher--student framework that converts world-model-generated futures into persistent semantic-spatial structure and planning-derived supervision. Its world-model-driven teacher builds semantic-spatial memory from generated videos, grounds task-relevant targets and obstacles, and produces trajectory pseudo-labels through explicit planning. A lightweight student with a multi-hypothesis trajectory head is then trained to predict navigation trajectories directly from vision-language inputs. On Target-Bench, WorldMAP achieves the best ADE and FDE among compared methods, reducing ADE by 18.0% and FDE by 42.1% relative to the best competing baseline, while lifting a small open-source VLM to DTW performance competitive with proprietary models. More broadly, the results suggest that, in embodied navigation, the value of world models may lie less in supplying action-ready imagined evidence than in synthesizing structured supervision for navigation learning.

### [102] [Are we still able to recognize pearls? Machine-driven peer review and the risk to creativity: An explainable RAG-XAI detection framework with markers extraction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.07964)
*Alin-Gabriel Văduva,Simona-Vasilica Oprea,Adela Bâra*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper warns that using LLMs in peer review could lead to fully automated editorial pipelines that bias science toward safe, pattern-conforming work. <details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs enter peer review, there is a risk that not only reviews but also editorial decisions become automated, potentially reshaping how science is evaluated. The authors are concerned that algorithmic systems will favor standardized, conventional research and disfavor unconventional, paradigm-shifting work that needs nuanced human judgment. This could create epistemic homogenization, where scientists optimize for algorithms instead of discovery. To counter this, there is a need for transparent, explainable tools that can detect automated reviews and maintain accountability and creativity in scientific evaluation.

Method: The authors design an explainable framework called RAG-XAI to assess review quality and detect automated patterns. It uses an LLM-based marker extractor to capture features indicative of machine-generated text, integrates a retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) component for context-aware analysis, and employs ensemble machine learning models (XGBoost, Random Forest, LightGBM) for classification. They compare these models against a logistic regression baseline. Explainability is provided via feature importance analysis and SHAP values, highlighting which features drive the classification decisions. They also evaluate the RAG retrieval accuracy and embedding-space clustering to validate the framework’s reliability.

Result: The RAG-XAI framework achieves near-perfect performance in detecting automated reviews. XGBoost, Random Forest, and LightGBM reach 99.61% accuracy, AUC-ROC above 0.999, and F1-scores of 0.9925 on the test set. The false positive rate is kept below 0.23% and the false negative rate around 0.8%, indicating both conservative and sensitive detection. In contrast, the logistic regression baseline performs much worse, with 89.97% accuracy and an F1-score of 0.8314. Feature importance and SHAP analyses show that the absence of personal signals and the presence of repetition patterns are the main predictors of automated reviews. The RAG component attains 90.5% top-1 retrieval accuracy and exhibits strong same-class clustering in the embedding space, further supporting the robustness of the system.

Conclusion: LLM-driven automation in peer review and editorial decision-making threatens to bias scientific evaluation toward homogeneous, algorithm-friendly research and away from unconventional ideas. The proposed RAG-XAI framework offers a practical safeguard by detecting automated review patterns with very high accuracy and providing interpretable explanations of its decisions. By highlighting key indicators such as missing personal signals and repetitive structures, and by leveraging reliable retrieval and embedding-based clustering, the framework aims to preserve transparency, accountability, and creativity in scientific assessment, mitigating the risks of a fully automated editorial pipeline.

Abstract: The integration of large language models (LLMs) into peer review raises a concern beyond authorship and detection: the potential cascading automation of the entire editorial process. As reviews become partially or fully machine-generated, it becomes plausible that editorial decisions may also be delegated to algorithmic systems, leading to a fully automated evaluation pipeline. They risk reshaping the criteria by which scientific work is assessed. This paper argues that machine-driven assessment may systematically favor standardized, pattern-conforming research while penalizing unconventional and paradigm-shifting ideas that require contextual human judgment. We consider that this shift could lead to epistemic homogenization, where researchers are implicitly incentivized to optimize their work for algorithmic approval rather than genuine discovery. To address this risk, we introduce an explainable framework (RAG-XAI) for assessing review quality and detecting automated patterns using markers LLM extractor, aiming to preserve transparency, accountability and creativity in science. The proposed framework achieves near-perfect detection performance, with XGBoost, Random Forest and LightGBM reaching 99.61% accuracy, AUC-ROC above 0.999 and F1-scores of 0.9925 on the test set, while maintaining extremely low false positive rates (<0.23%) and false negative rates (~0.8%). In contrast, the logistic regression baseline performs substantially worse (89.97% accuracy, F1-score 0.8314). Feature importance and SHAP analyses identify absence of personal signals and repetition patterns as the dominant predictors. Additionally, the RAG component achieves 90.5% top-1 retrieval accuracy, with strong same-class clustering in the embedding space, further supporting the reliability of the framework's outputs.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors aim to fill this gap and understand both the strengths and limitations of current LMMs in this setting.

Method: The authors construct a new dataset of 5,037 high-quality goal-oriented navigation trajectories in urban 3D environments, emphasizing vertical actions (e.g., stairs, floors, multi-level structures) and dense semantic cues. They then benchmark 17 representative systems, including standard LMMs without explicit reasoning modules, LMMs augmented with reasoning chains, agent-based approaches, and vision-language-action models. Performance is analyzed with a particular focus on decision points where the trajectory can diverge toward or away from the goal—termed critical decision bifurcations. They also run targeted experiments that inject or enhance four capabilities—geometric perception, cross-view understanding, spatial imagination, and long-term memory—to study their impact on navigation.

Result: The evaluation shows that current LMMs can perform some navigation and action planning but fall short of human-level reliability and accuracy, especially in complex 3D urban settings. A key empirical finding is that navigation errors are not simply additive over time; instead, once a model makes an incorrect choice at a critical decision point, its trajectory tends to diverge quickly and significantly from the destination. Behavior analysis at these bifurcations highlights systematic weaknesses in spatial reasoning and memory. Experiments on the four enhancement directions demonstrate that bolstering geometric and cross-view understanding, spatial imagination, and long-term memory can improve performance, though none fully closes the gap to human navigation ability.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that while LMMs display emerging embodied action capabilities, they are still fundamentally limited for robust goal-oriented navigation in real-world-like 3D urban environments. Navigation performance is particularly sensitive to a small number of critical decisions where errors trigger rapid divergence from the goal. Addressing the identified shortcomings—especially in geometric perception, cross-view reasoning, spatial imagination, and persistent memory—is crucial for building more capable embodied agents. The released benchmark and dataset provide a standardized platform for future research on spatial decision-making and navigation with multimodal models.

Abstract: Large multimodal models (LMMs) show strong visual-linguistic reasoning but their capacity for spatial decision-making and action remains unclear. In this work, we investigate whether LMMs can achieve embodied spatial action like human through a challenging scenario: goal-oriented navigation in urban 3D spaces. We first spend over 500 hours constructing a dataset comprising 5,037 high-quality goal-oriented navigation samples, with an emphasis on 3D vertical actions and rich urban semantic information. Then, we comprehensively assess 17 representative models, including non-reasoning LMMs, reasoning LMMs, agent-based methods, and vision-language-action models. Experiments show that current LMMs exhibit emerging action capabilities, yet remain far from human-level performance. Furthermore, we reveal an intriguing phenomenon: navigation errors do not accumulate linearly but instead diverge rapidly from the destination after a critical decision bifurcation. The limitations of LMMs are investigated by analyzing their behavior at these critical decision bifurcations. Finally, we experimentally explore four promising directions for improvement: geometric perception, cross-view understanding, spatial imagination, and long-term memory. The project is available at: https://github.com/serenditipy-AC/Embodied-Navigation-Bench.

The proposed IntentFlow model, hybrid memory, and PAS infrastructure can work as a closed-loop system to infer and act on latent user needs. We also introduce LatentNeeds-Bench, a real-world benchmark built from user-consented data and refined through thousands of rounds of human editing. Experiments show that IntentFlow matches leading Gemini3-Flash models under latency constraints, while identifying deeper user intent.

As part of this investigation, we hypothesize that robust CX generation methods will be better suited to address the challenge of providing valid and plausible counterfactuals when inputs are incomplete. Our findings reveal that while robust CX methods achieve higher validity than non-robust ones, all methods struggle to find valid counterfactuals. These results motivate the need for new CX methods capable of handling incomplete inputs.

This paper presents the first survey of abductive reasoning in LLMs, tracing its trajectory from philosophical foundations to contemporary AI implementations. To address the widespread conceptual confusion and disjointed task definitions prevalent in the field, we establish a unified two-stage definition that formally categorizes prior work. This definition disentangles abduction into \textit{Hypothesis Generation}, where models bridge epistemic gaps to produce candidate explanations, and \textit{Hypothesis Selection}, where the generated candidates are evaluated and the most plausible explanation is chosen. Moreover, by synthesizing recent empirical results, we examine how LLM performance on abductive reasoning relates to deductive and inductive tasks, providing insights into their broader reasoning capabilities. Our analysis reveals critical gaps in current approaches--from static benchmark design and narrow domain coverage to narrow training frameworks and limited mechanistic understanding of abductive processes...

### [108] [IoT-Brain: Grounding LLMs for Semantic-Spatial Sensor Scheduling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08033)
*Zhaomeng Zhou,Lan Zhang,Junyang Wang,Mu Yuan,Junda Lin,Jinke Song*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a system (IoT-Brain) and framework (Spatial Trajectory Graph) for proactively scheduling which sensors to use and when, so that large language models can more reliably and efficiently interact with and query large-scale sensor networks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current large-scale sensor networks and intelligent systems mostly perform predefined or retrospective monitoring, and perception pipelines passively process whatever has been sensed. Large language models understand high-level, intent-driven natural language queries, but there is a gap between semantic intent and concrete decisions about which physical sensors to activate in space and time. Directly letting LLMs plan sensor usage proves unreliable due to issues in representation, reasoning, and optimization, creating a critical Semantic-to-Physical Mapping Gap that motivates a new, more reliable planning framework.

Method: The authors formalize the proactive sensor selection problem as Semantic-Spatial Sensor Scheduling (S3). They propose the Spatial Trajectory Graph (STG), a neuro-symbolic representation that converts open-ended LLM planning into a constrained, verifiable graph optimization problem governed by a verify-before-commit discipline. On top of STG they build IoT-Brain, a concrete system that uses LLMs together with symbolic graph reasoning to schedule sensors. They also create TopoSense-Bench, a large benchmark with natural-language queries mapped to a campus-scale camera network, to systematically evaluate the approach.

Result: On the TopoSense-Bench benchmark, IoT-Brain improves task success rate by 37.6% compared with the strongest search-intensive baselines, while being nearly twice as fast and using 6.6× fewer LLM prompt tokens. In real-world deployment, the system operates close to an estimated reliability upper bound and reduces network bandwidth usage by a factor of 4.1 compared with naïve or prior methods.

Conclusion: By recasting intent-driven sensing as a graph optimization problem via the Spatial Trajectory Graph and enforcing verify-before-commit planning, the proposed IoT-Brain system enables LLMs to interact with large sensor networks far more reliably and efficiently. This framework substantially narrows the Semantic-to-Physical Mapping Gap and provides a principled foundation for proactive, intent-driven operation of future IoT and sensing infrastructures.

Abstract: Intelligent systems powered by large-scale sensor networks are shifting from predefined monitoring to intent-driven operation, revealing a critical Semantic-to-Physical Mapping Gap. While large language models (LLMs) excel at semantic understanding, existing perception-centric pipelines operate retrospectively, overlooking the fundamental decision of what to sense and when. We formalize this proactive decision as Semantic-Spatial Sensor Scheduling (S3) and demonstrate that direct LLM planning is unreliable due to inherent gaps in representation, reasoning, and optimization. To bridge these gaps, we introduce the Spatial Trajectory Graph (STG), a neuro-symbolic paradigm governed by a verify-before-commit discipline that transforms open-ended planning into a verifiable graph optimization problem. Based on STG, we implement IoT-Brain, a concrete system embodiment, and construct TopoSense-Bench, a campus-scale benchmark with 5,250 natural-language queries across 2,510 cameras. Evaluations show that IoT-Brain boosts task success rate by 37.6% over the strongest search-intensive methods while running nearly 2 times faster and using 6.6 times fewer prompt tokens. In real-world deployment, it approaches the reliability upper bound while reducing 4.1 times network bandwidth, providing a foundational framework for LLMs to interact with the physical world with unprecedented reliability and efficiency.

</details>


### [109] [ImplicitMemBench: Measuring Unconscious Behavioral Adaptation in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08064)
*Chonghan Qin,Xiachong Feng,Weitao Ma,Xiaocheng Feng,Lingpeng Kong*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces ImplicitMemBench, a benchmark to test large language models’ implicit memory abilities—like procedural learning, priming, and conditioning—and shows current models perform far below humans, revealing fundamental limitations beyond simple fact recall.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most existing memory benchmarks for LLM agents focus on explicit recall of facts, ignoring implicit memory where experiences are transformed into automatic behaviors without conscious retrieval. This is a crucial gap because effective AI assistants should be able to internalize procedures, avoid repeating mistakes, and adapt behavior automatically, not only when explicitly reminded. The authors aim to fill this gap by systematically assessing non-declarative memory capacities in LLMs, inspired by cognitive science.

Method: The authors design ImplicitMemBench, a 300-item benchmark built around three constructs from cognitive science: Procedural Memory (testing one-shot skill acquisition that must be retained after interference), Priming (testing how prior exposure biases subsequent responses via themed experimental vs. control instances), and Classical Conditioning (testing whether paired CS–US associations influence first choices). All items follow a unified Learning/Priming-Interfere-Test protocol, and models are evaluated based on first-attempt performance. They then test 17 different models on this benchmark.

Result: Across 17 evaluated models, none surpasses 66% overall accuracy on ImplicitMemBench. The leading models—DeepSeek-R1 at 65.3%, Qwen3-32B at 64.1%, and GPT-5 at 63.0%—still fall significantly short of human baselines. The analysis reveals pronounced asymmetries, such as very weak inhibition (17.6%) compared to strong preference effects (75.0%), and points to common failure modes across architectures.

Conclusion: LLMs exhibit substantial deficits in implicit memory abilities despite strong performance on explicit recall tasks. ImplicitMemBench demonstrates that current models struggle to automatically internalize procedures, learn from failed actions, and form robust non-declarative associations. These limitations appear to stem from architectural bottlenecks that are unlikely to be solved by mere parameter scaling, suggesting the need for new model designs. The benchmark shifts the evaluation focus from what models can explicitly recall to what behaviors they can automatically enact based on past experience.

Abstract: Existing memory benchmarks for LLM agents evaluate explicit recall of facts, yet overlook implicit memory where experience becomes automated behavior without conscious retrieval. This gap is critical: effective assistants must automatically apply learned procedures or avoid failed actions without explicit reminders. We introduce ImplicitMemBench, the first systematic benchmark evaluating implicit memory through three cognitively grounded constructs drawn from standard cognitive-science accounts of non-declarative memory: Procedural Memory (one-shot skill acquisition after interference), Priming (theme-driven bias via paired experimental/control instances), and Classical Conditioning (Conditioned Stimulus--Unconditioned Stimulus (CS--US) associations shaping first decisions). Our 300-item suite employs a unified Learning/Priming-Interfere-Test protocol with first-attempt scoring. Evaluation of 17 models reveals severe limitations: no model exceeds 66% overall, with top performers DeepSeek-R1 (65.3%), Qwen3-32B (64.1%), and GPT-5 (63.0%) far below human baselines. Analysis uncovers dramatic asymmetries (inhibition 17.6% vs. preference 75.0%) and universal bottlenecks requiring architectural innovations beyond parameter scaling. ImplicitMemBench reframes evaluation from "what agents recall" to "what they automatically enact".

Experimental results demonstrate that Revise effectively corrects OCR outputs, enabling more structured representation and systematic management of document contents. Consequently, our method significantly enhances downstream performance in document retrieval and question answering tasks, highlighting the potential to overcome the structural management limitations of existing Document AI frameworks.

### [111] [Beyond Stochastic Exploration: What Makes Training Data Valuable for Agentic Search](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08124)
*Chuzhan Hao,Wenfeng Feng,Guochao Jiang,Guofeng Quan,Guohua Liu,Yuewei Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RL-based search agents for LLMs can integrate external search engines to improve reasoning, but they depend on noisy stochastic exploration plus handcrafted outcome rewards. This often yields inefficient reasoning paths and unstable, hard-to-train systems. The authors are motivated to make exploration more strategic, data-driven, and stable by directly learning from past reasoning trajectories.

Method: They introduce Hierarchical Experience (HiExp). First, they collect raw reasoning trajectories from RL-based search agents. Through contrastive analysis and multi-level clustering, they organize these trajectories into hierarchical experience knowledge that captures different levels of reasoning patterns. Then, they perform experience-aligned training, where the agent’s exploration is regularized towards these learned experiences, effectively biasing the search process from random exploration toward strategy-guided behavior. The framework is evaluated on agentic search and mathematical reasoning benchmarks and tested with multiple RL algorithms.

Result: HiExp-based agents achieve higher performance across several complex agentic search and mathematical reasoning benchmarks compared to standard RL search agents. The method also leads to more stable training dynamics. Furthermore, the hierarchical experience representation generalizes across tasks and different RL algorithms, indicating that the learned experience is transferable rather than overfitted to a single setting.

Conclusion: Transforming raw RL trajectories into hierarchical experience and aligning exploration with this experience converts stochastic, unstable search into a more strategic and reliable process. HiExp improves both effectiveness and stability of RL search agents with LLMs and shows promising cross-task and cross-algorithm generalization, suggesting it is a broadly applicable way to leverage learned experience in reasoning agents.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning (RL) has become an effective approach for advancing the reasoning capabilities of large language models (LLMs) through the strategic integration of external search engines. However, current RL-based search agents often rely on a process of stochastic exploration guided by carefully crafted outcome rewards, leading to inefficient reasoning trajectories and unstable training. To address these issues, we propose a novel framework, Hierarchical Experience (HiExp), to enhance the performance and training stability of search agents. Specifically, we extract empirical knowledge through contrastive analysis and a multi-level clustering mechanism, transforming raw reasoning trajectories into hierarchical experience knowledge. By leveraging experience-aligned training, we effectively regularize stochastic exploration, evolving it into a strategic and experience-driven search process. Extensive evaluations on multiple complex agentic search and mathematical reasoning benchmarks demonstrate that our approach not only achieves substantial performance gains but also exhibits strong cross-task and cross-algorithm generalization.

### [112] [Activation Steering for Aligned Open-ended Generation without Sacrificing Coherence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08169)
*Niklas Herbster,Martin Zborowski,Alberto Tosato,Gauthier Gidel,Tommaso Tosato*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to use activation steering as a lightweight runtime defense to correct misalignment in large language models during generation, proposing projection-aware steering methods that selectively intervene on harmful activations while preserving capabilities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM alignment is fragile: models can become misaligned through adversarial prompts, benign fine-tuning, emergent behaviors, and goal misgeneralization. Prior work indicates some misaligned behaviors are encoded linearly in activation space and that safety training primarily constrains only the first few output tokens, leaving later tokens vulnerable. This motivates a method that can continuously and efficiently correct misaligned internal activations at runtime without retraining the model.

Method: The authors propose and evaluate three activation steering methods that modify model activations during inference. (1) Steer-With-Fixed-Coeff (SwFC) applies a uniform additive steering vector to activations at each step. (2) Steer-to-Target-Projection (StTP) and (3) Steer-to-Mirror-Projection (StMP) are novel projection-aware approaches that use a logistic regression classifier to learn a decision boundary between aligned and misaligned activations. They then selectively intervene only on tokens whose activations fall under certain distributional thresholds relative to this boundary, steering them toward a target (aligned) region in activation space. Misaligned behavior is simulated using malicious system prompts under two threat models: dishonesty and dismissiveness. Experiments are conducted on Llama-3.3-70B-Instruct and Qwen3-32B.

Result: All three methods successfully recover alignment traits such as honesty and compassion in the presence of malicious system prompts, while keeping responses coherent. The projection-aware methods StTP and StMP outperform the simple fixed-coefficient steering in maintaining general capabilities as measured by benchmarks like MMLU, MT-Bench, and AlpacaEval. They also reduce repetition and preserve interaction quality better in multi-turn conversations.

Conclusion: Activation steering can serve as an effective, lightweight runtime defense for LLM alignment, continuously correcting misaligned activations during generation. Projection-aware steering via learned decision boundaries (StTP, StMP) selectively targets problematic tokens and more effectively balances safety and capability preservation than naive fixed-coefficient steering, suggesting a practical path toward more robustly aligned LLMs without extensive retraining.

Abstract: Alignment in LLMs is more brittle than commonly assumed: misalignment can be triggered by adversarial prompts, benign fine-tuning, emergent misalignment, and goal misgeneralization. Recent evidence suggests that some misalignment behaviors are encoded as linear structure in activation space, making it tractable via steering, while safety alignment has been shown to govern the first few output tokens primarily, leaving subsequent generation unguarded. These findings motivate activation steering as a lightweight runtime defense that continuously corrects misaligned activations throughout generation. We evaluate three methods: Steer-With-Fixed-Coeff (SwFC), which applies uniform additive steering, and two novel projection-aware methods, Steer-to-Target-Projection (StTP) and Steer-to-Mirror-Projection (StMP), that use a logistic regression decision boundary to selectively intervene only on tokens whose activations fall below distributional thresholds. Using malicious system prompts as a controlled proxy for misalignment, we evaluate under two threat models (dishonesty and dismissiveness) and two architectures (Llama-3.3-70B-Instruct, Qwen3-32B). All methods substantially recover target traits (honesty and compassion) while preserving coherence. StTP and StMP better maintain general capabilities (MMLU, MT-Bench, AlpacaEval) and produce less repetition in multi-turn conversations.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is currently a gap: no systematic benchmark exists to stress-test reward models on such complex, tool-integrated decision sequences.

Method: The authors construct Plan-RewardBench, a trajectory-level preference benchmark. It contains four classes of tasks relevant to tool-using LLM agents: (i) Safety Refusal, (ii) Tool-Irrelevance / Unavailability, (iii) Complex Planning, and (iv) Robust Error Recovery. For each task, they create validated positive trajectories (good agent behaviors) and carefully designed hard negative trajectories that are confusable but flawed. These negatives are generated via multi-model natural rollouts, rule-based perturbations, and minimal-edit LLM perturbations. They then evaluate several families of reward models—generative models, discriminative models, and LLM-as-a-judge setups—under a unified pairwise comparison protocol, where the model must choose the preferred trajectory between two options. They analyze performance across trajectory lengths, task categories, and characterize common failure modes.

Result: Across all reward model families tested, performance is far from perfect and drops significantly as trajectory length increases, especially on complex long-horizon planning tasks. All evaluator types—generative, discriminative, and LLM-as-Judge—exhibit systematic weaknesses and frequent misjudgments, particularly in nuanced tool-use situations and error recovery scenarios. The benchmark surfaces clear, reproducible patterns of failure and reveals that current RMs are not reliably capable of evaluating agentic tool-using behaviors at the trajectory level.

Conclusion: Plan-RewardBench provides a much-needed, structured evaluation suite for trajectory-level reward modeling in tool-using LLM agents. The authors conclude that existing reward models are inadequate for robust long-horizon, agentic settings and that specialized training and methods for trajectory-level reward modeling are necessary. They position Plan-RewardBench both as a practical benchmark to evaluate future reward models and as a reusable design pattern for constructing preference datasets focused on agentic planning and tool use.

Abstract: In classical Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF), Reward Models (RMs) serve as the fundamental signal provider for model alignment. As Large Language Models evolve into agentic systems capable of autonomous tool invocation and complex reasoning, the paradigm of reward modeling faces unprecedented challenges--most notably, the lack of benchmarks specifically designed to assess RM capabilities within tool-integrated environments. To address this gap, we present Plan-RewardBench, a trajectory-level preference benchmark designed to evaluate how well judges distinguish preferred versus distractor agent trajectories in complex tool-using scenarios. Plan-RewardBench covers four representative task families -- (i) Safety Refusal, (ii) Tool-Irrelevance / Unavailability, (iii) Complex Planning, and (iv) Robust Error Recovery -- comprising validated positive trajectories and confusable hard negatives constructed via multi-model natural rollouts, rule-based perturbations, and minimal-edit LLM perturbations. We benchmark representative RMs (generative, discriminative, and LLM-as-Judge) under a unified pairwise protocol, reporting accuracy trends across varying trajectory lengths and task categories. Furthermore, we provide diagnostic analyses of prevalent failure modes. Our results reveal that all three evaluator families face substantial challenges, with performance degrading sharply on long-horizon trajectories, underscoring the necessity for specialized training in agentic, trajectory-level reward modeling. Ultimately, Plan-RewardBench aims to serve as both a practical evaluation suite and a reusable blueprint for constructing agentic planning preference data.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current clinical AI systems are evaluated, regulated, and designed using fragmented frameworks that do not share a formal model of the clinical world. As a result, it's unclear what exactly an AI system is competent to do, how evidence in one setting generalizes to another, and how to align perspectives across clinicians, patients, regulators, and developers. The authors want a rigorous, shared structure that can describe and compare AI capabilities, limitations, and validation across all these stakeholders.

Method: The authors construct a conceptual and formal framework. First, they define a "Clinical World Model" that represents healthcare as interactions between three entities: Patient, Provider, and Ecosystem. They then formalize decision-making architectures for each entity (including AI agents) using principles from clinical cognition to show how information is transformed into actions. Next, they define the "Clinical AI Skill-Mix": eight dimensions that span the space of clinical AI competency—five describing the clinical context (condition, phase, care setting, provider role, task) and three describing how AI interfaces with human reasoning (assigned authority, agent facing, anchoring layer). They analyze the combinatorial structure of these dimensions to show how many distinct competency coordinates exist and discuss implications for validation and generalization.

Result: The framework yields a high-dimensional "competency space" for clinical AI with billions of possible coordinates, each representing a specific, narrow combination of clinical context and human–AI interaction properties. The authors argue structurally that performance evidence obtained in one coordinate mostly fails to generalize to others, highlighting that the competency space is effectively irreducible. This makes explicit that claims about an AI system's reliability must be tied to specific coordinates rather than broad, vague contexts. The framework also produces a common language that different stakeholders can use to specify where an AI system is intended to operate and where it has been validated.

Conclusion: The Clinical World Model and Clinical AI Skill-Mix provide a formal, shared grammar for specifying and bounding the competency of clinical AI agents. By making the multi-dimensional structure of clinical competency explicit, the work reframes the central issue from simply asking whether an AI system works to asking in which exact competency coordinates it has demonstrated reliability and for which users. This has implications for design, evaluation, regulation, deployment, and communication about clinical AI, emphasizing that validation is inherently local and non-transferable across most of the vast competency space.

Abstract: The competency of any intelligent agent is bounded by its formal account of the world in which it operates. Clinical AI lacks such an account. Existing frameworks address evaluation, regulation, or system design in isolation, without a shared model of the clinical world to connect them. We introduce the Clinical World Model, a framework that formalizes care as a tripartite interaction among Patient, Provider, and Ecosystem. To formalize how any agent, whether human or artificial, transforms information into clinical action, we develop parallel decision-making architectures for providers, patients, and AI agents, grounded in validated principles of clinical cognition.
  The Clinical AI Skill-Mix operationalizes competency through eight dimensions. Five define the clinical competency space (condition, phase, care setting, provider role, and task) and three specify how AI engages human reasoning (assigned authority, agent facing, and anchoring layer). The combinatorial product of these dimensions yields a space of billions of distinct competency coordinates. A central structural implication is that validation within one coordinate provides minimal evidence for performance in another, rendering the competency space irreducible. The framework supplies a common grammar through which clinical AI can be specified, evaluated, and bounded across stakeholders. By making this structure explicit, the Clinical World Model reframes the field's central question from whether AI works to in which competency coordinates reliability has been demonstrated, and for whom.

### [115] [HiRO-Nav: Hybrid ReasOning Enables Efficient Embodied Navigation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08232)
*He Zhao,Yijun Yang,Zichuan Lin,Deheng Ye,Chunyan Miao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose HiRO-Nav, an embodied navigation agent that adaptively decides when to invoke expensive LRM-style reasoning, based on action entropy, to get better success per token than always-thinking or never-thinking agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large reasoning models enable strong step-by-step reasoning for embodied navigation, but invoking such reasoning at every step in long-horizon tasks is computationally expensive and unnecessary in simple situations. There is a need for a principled way to decide when an agent should think carefully and when it can act reflexively, to balance performance and efficiency.

Method: They analyze the entropy of the agent’s action distribution along trajectories and observe that high-entropy actions are rare, often correspond to critical or novel situations, and that improving these actions correlates strongly with higher task Q-values. Based on this, they design HiRO-Nav, which uses action entropy at each step to decide whether to trigger deliberate LRM reasoning. The training pipeline includes: (1) hybrid supervised fine-tuning to provide a good initial policy, and (2) online reinforcement learning with a hybrid reasoning strategy that explicitly activates reasoning only for high-entropy actions, thereby focusing learning and computation on those difficult steps.

Result: On the CHORES-S ObjectNav benchmark, HiRO-Nav surpasses both dense-thinking (reasoning at every step) and no-thinking baselines in terms of success rate while also being more token-efficient, demonstrating a better trade-off between task performance and computational cost.

Conclusion: By using action entropy as an internal signal to adaptively invoke LRM-style reasoning only when needed, HiRO-Nav efficiently exploits powerful reasoning capabilities for embodied navigation. This hybrid reasoning scheme improves decision quality at critical steps while avoiding unnecessary computation in easy situations, leading to a superior success–efficiency trade-off on a standard ObjectNav benchmark.

Abstract: Embodied navigation agents built upon large reasoning models (LRMs) can handle complex, multimodal environmental input and perform grounded reasoning per step to improve sequential decision-making for long-horizon tasks. However, a critical question remains: \textit{how can the reasoning capabilities of LRMs be harnessed intelligently and efficiently for long-horizon navigation tasks?} In simple scenes, agents are expected to act reflexively, while in complex ones they should engage in deliberate reasoning before acting.To achieve this, we introduce \textbf{H}ybr\textbf{i}d \textbf{R}eas\textbf{O}ning \textbf{Nav}igation (\textbf{HiRO-Nav}) agent, the first kind of agent capable of adaptively determining whether to perform thinking at every step based on its own action entropy. Specifically, by examining how the agent's action entropy evolves over the navigation trajectories, we observed that only a small fraction of actions exhibit high entropy, and these actions often steer the agent toward novel scenes or critical objects. Furthermore, studying the relationship between action entropy and task completion (i.e., Q-value) reveals that improving high-entropy actions contributes more positively to task success.Hence, we propose a tailored training pipeline comprising hybrid supervised fine-tuning as a cold start, followed by online reinforcement learning with the proposed hybrid reasoning strategy to explicitly activate reasoning only for high-entropy actions, significantly reducing computational overhead while improving decision quality. Extensive experiments on the \textsc{CHORES}-$\mathbb{S}$ ObjectNav benchmark showcases that HiRO-Nav achieves a better trade-off between success rates and token efficiency than both dense-thinking and no-thinking baselines.

### [116] [From Phenomenological Fitting to Endogenous Deduction: A Paradigm Leap via Meta-Principle Physics Architecture](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08245)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current neural networks largely perform phenomenological fitting: they capture statistical input-output correlations using large datasets and parameters but lack an intrinsic grasp of physical laws or causal structure. This leads to poor performance on tasks requiring physical common sense, causal reasoning, and extrapolation to out-of-distribution physical scenarios. The authors aim to move from purely data-driven correlation learning to architectures that encode fundamental physical meta-principles directly into the model, providing a more principled and interpretable foundation for AI systems dealing with the physical world.

Method: The authors propose the Meta-Principle Physics Architecture (MPPA), which explicitly embeds three physical meta-principles—Connectivity, Conservation, and Periodicity—into the neural architecture. Connectivity is realized through a component called the Gravitator, based on standard causal attention to capture local relational structure. Conservation is enforced via an Energy Encoder that performs log-domain energy tracking and delayed compensation to maintain global conservation constraints. Periodicity is modeled by a Periodicity Encoder using FFT-based spectral analysis and delayed modulation to capture periodic laws of evolution. These components are combined via a learnable independent gating fusion mechanism, yielding an integrated framework that aligns local relations, global conservation, and temporal periodicity.

Result: On benchmarks, MPPA substantially outperforms a comparable baseline model. It improves physical reasoning accuracy from nearly zero to 0.436, yields a 2.18x gain on mathematical tasks (0.330 vs 0.151), and a 52% relative improvement on logical tasks (0.456 vs 0.300). Additionally, it reduces validation perplexity by 3.69% (259.45 vs 269.40) with only an 11.8% increase in parameters (242.40M vs 216.91M). MPPA also demonstrates strong generalization to out-of-distribution physical scenarios, indicating robustness and better inductive bias compared to standard architectures.

Conclusion: Embedding abstract physical meta-principles directly into neural network architectures can significantly enhance physical reasoning, mathematical and logical performance, and generalization, without prohibitive parameter growth. MPPA offers a concrete implementation of this idea through specialized modules for connectivity, conservation, and periodicity, fused via learnable gating. The results suggest a promising technical and theoretical direction for developing next-generation AI systems endowed with physical common sense, causal reasoning abilities, and mathematical rigor, moving beyond purely phenomenological correlation fitting.

Abstract: The essence of current neural network architectures is phenomenological fitting: they learn input-output statistical correlations via massive parameters and data, yet lack intrinsic understanding of the fundamental principles governing physical reality. This paper proposes a paradigm leap from pure phenomenological fitting to the fusion of phenomenological fitting and endogenous deduction. By embedding physical meta-principles into neural network architecture, we construct the Meta-Principle Physics Architecture (MPPA).
  Specifically, MPPA embeds three core meta-principles - Connectivity, Conservation, Periodicity - into its architecture, implemented via three core components: the Gravitator realizes Connectivity via standard causal attention; the Energy Encoder implements Conservation via log-domain energy tracking and delayed compensation; the Periodicity Encoder fulfills Periodicity via FFT-based spectral analysis and delayed modulation. These components collaborate via a learnable independent gating fusion mechanism, forming a complete physical cognition framework of 'local relational connectivity - global conservation constraint - evolutionary periodic law'.
  Experiments show MPPA achieves significant improvements: physical reasoning (from near zero to 0.436, 0.436 vs 0.000), 2.18x mathematical task improvement (0.330 vs 0.151), 52% logical task gain (0.456 vs 0.300), and 3.69% lower validation perplexity (259.45 vs 269.40), with only 11.8% more parameters (242.40M vs 216.91M). This work establishes a new theoretical foundation and technical path for next-generation AI with physical common sense, causal reasoning, and mathematical rigor.

### [117] [Neural-Symbolic Knowledge Tracing: Injecting Educational Knowledge into Deep Learning for Responsible Learner Modelling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08263)
*Danial Hooshyar,Gustav Šír,Yeongwook Yang,Tommi Kärkkäinen,Raija Hämäläinen,Ekaterina Krivich,Mutlu Cukurova,Dragan Gašević,Roger Azevedo*

TL;DR: The paper introduces Responsible-DKT, a neural-symbolic deep knowledge tracing model that embeds explicit pedagogical rules into sequential neural networks to make learner modelling in AI tutoring systems more accurate, reliable over time, and interpretable, even with limited data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs and other AI tools are increasingly used in education, but they are weak at tracking how a learner’s knowledge changes over time. Existing deep knowledge tracing (DKT) methods predict performance well but are opaque, hard to interpret, and can conflict with pedagogical expectations or be biased. There is a need for learner models that are both accurate and aligned with educational theory, data-efficient, and explainable to humans.

Method: The authors design Responsible-DKT, a neural-symbolic DKT model that incorporates symbolic educational concepts such as mastery and non-mastery rules into a sequential neural architecture. They construct a grounded computation graph that explicitly represents these rules and their interaction with student response sequences. The model is trained and evaluated on a real-world math dataset and compared with a neural-symbolic baseline and a standard, fully data-driven PyTorch DKT model across different training data regimes. They analyze predictive accuracy, temporal reliability (error patterns and inconsistency over time), and interpretability properties.

Result: Responsible-DKT consistently outperforms both comparison models. It achieves AUC > 0.80 using only 10% of the training data and up to 0.90 AUC overall, yielding up to 13% improvement over baselines. It exhibits better temporal reliability, including lower early- and mid-sequence prediction errors and the lowest prediction inconsistency rates, meaning its prediction updates stay aligned with observed student responses. The grounded computation graph yields intrinsic interpretability, supporting local and global explanations of predictions and enabling empirical testing of pedagogical rules. Analysis shows that repeated incorrect responses (non-mastery) have a strong influence on prediction updates.

Conclusion: Integrating symbolic pedagogical knowledge into deep knowledge tracing improves both performance and responsibility of learner models. Neural-symbolic approaches like Responsible-DKT make models more data-efficient, temporally reliable, and intrinsically interpretable, supporting explanation, diagnosis of assumptions, and alignment with human-centered educational goals. This suggests neural-symbolic design is a promising direction for building responsible AI systems in education.

Abstract: The growing use of artificial intelligence (AI) in education, particularly large language models (LLMs), has increased interest in intelligent tutoring systems. However, LLMs often show limited adaptivity and struggle to model learners' evolving knowledge over time, highlighting the need for dedicated learner modelling approaches. Although deep knowledge tracing methods achieve strong predictive performance, their opacity and susceptibility to bias can limit alignment with pedagogical principles. To address this, we propose Responsible-DKT, a neural-symbolic deep knowledge tracing approach that integrates symbolic educational knowledge (e.g., mastery and non-mastery rules) into sequential neural models for responsible learner modelling. Experiments on a real-world dataset of students' math interactions show that Responsible-DKT outperforms both a neural-symbolic baseline and a fully data-driven PyTorch DKT model across training settings. The model achieves over 0.80 AUC with only 10% of training data and up to 0.90 AUC, improving performance by up to 13%. It also demonstrates improved temporal reliability, producing lower early- and mid-sequence prediction errors and the lowest prediction inconsistency rates across sequence lengths, indicating that prediction updates remain directionally aligned with observed student responses over time. Furthermore, the neural-symbolic approach offers intrinsic interpretability via a grounded computation graph that exposes the logic behind each prediction, enabling both local and global explanations. It also allows empirical evaluation of pedagogical assumptions, revealing that repeated incorrect responses (non-mastery) strongly influence prediction updates. These results indicate that neural-symbolic approaches enhance both performance and interpretability, mitigate data limitations, and support more responsible, human-centered AI in education.

### [118] [ACF: A Collaborative Framework for Agent Covert Communication under Cognitive Asymmetry](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08276)
*Wansheng Wu,Kaibo Huang,Yukun Wei,Zhongliang Yang,Linna Zhou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces the Asymmetric Collaborative Framework (ACF), a method for reliable covert communication in autonomous AI agent networks that removes the need for encoder–decoder cognitive symmetry, remaining robust even when agents’ memories diverge.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autonomous generative AI agents interact with environments and continually update their internal memory. Existing covert communication schemes assume cognitive symmetry, i.e., that encoder and decoder share identical or highly aligned conversational/memory prefixes. In real deployments, this assumption breaks due to dynamic interactions, causing desynchronization between sender and receiver, which severely degrades the covert communication channel. The paper is motivated by this structural weakness—cognitive asymmetry—and seeks a method that remains reliable and stealthy when agent memories and contexts differ significantly.

Method: The authors propose the Asymmetric Collaborative Framework (ACF), which separates covert communication from high-level semantic reasoning using two orthogonal layers: (1) a statistical layer that implements a prefix-independent steganographic coding scheme controlled by a shared configuration, and (2) a cognitive layer that handles agents’ semantic reasoning and memory updates. By making decoding depend only on the shared steganographic configuration rather than on having the same prefix context, ACF enables agents with different histories to collaborate. The framework is evaluated on memory-augmented agent workflows where encoder and decoder experience divergent interactions that induce cognitive asymmetry.

Result: In experiments simulating realistic, memory-augmented autonomous agent workflows with severe cognitive asymmetry, standard symmetric covert communication baselines show heavy channel degradation: synchronization fails and secret extraction becomes unreliable. In contrast, ACF sustains high semantic quality in visible interactions while preserving robust covert communication performance. It supports reliable extraction of hidden messages with provable error bounds and maintains computational indistinguishability from normal agent behavior. The framework is further characterized in terms of Effective Information Capacity, demonstrating stronger guarantees than symmetric baselines under asymmetric conditions.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that cognitive asymmetry is a fundamental vulnerability for existing covert communication schemes in autonomous agent networks, stemming from their dependence on shared conversational prefixes. By structurally decoupling covert signaling from semantic reasoning and using a prefix-independent decoding strategy, ACF overcomes this limitation. It enables reliable, stealthy covert communication even when agents’ internal memories diverge, with formal guarantees on error rates and effective capacity, making it better suited for modern, dynamic agent ecosystems.

Abstract: As generative artificial intelligence evolves, autonomous agent networks present a powerful paradigm for interactive covert communication. However, because agents dynamically update internal memories via environmental interactions, existing methods face a critical structural vulnerability: cognitive asymmetry. Conventional approaches demand strict cognitive symmetry, requiring identical sequence prefixes between the encoder and decoder. In dynamic deployments, inevitable prefix discrepancies destroy synchronization, inducing severe channel degradation. To address this core challenge of cognitive asymmetry, we propose the Asymmetric Collaborative Framework (ACF), which structurally decouples covert communication from semantic reasoning via orthogonal statistical and cognitive layers. By deploying a prefix-independent decoding paradigm governed by a shared steganographic configuration, ACF eliminates the reliance on cognitive symmetry. Evaluations on realistic memory-augmented workflows demonstrate that under severe cognitive asymmetry, symmetric baselines suffer severe channel degradation, whereas ACF uniquely excels across both semantic fidelity and covert communication. It maintains computational indistinguishability, enabling reliable secret extraction with provable error bounds, and providing robust Effective Information Capacity guarantees for modern agent networks.

### [119] [U-CECE: A Universal Multi-Resolution Framework for Conceptual Counterfactual Explanations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08295)
*Angeliki Dimitriou,Nikolaos Chaidos,Maria Lymperaiou,Giorgos Filandrianos,Giorgos Stamou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce U-CECE, a unified multi-resolution framework for concept-based counterfactual explanations that balances expressivity and efficiency, offering atomic, relational, and structural graph-based explanations without requiring exact Graph Edit Distance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing concept-based counterfactual methods either use atomic concept sets (efficient but lacking relational/structural context) or full graph representations (expressive and faithful but computationally intractable due to NP-hard Graph Edit Distance). There is no unified approach that flexibly trades off between these extremes based on data and compute, nor one that offers scalable structural counterfactuals that remain semantically faithful.

Method: They design U-CECE, a model-agnostic framework providing three explanation resolutions: (1) atomic concepts for coarse explanations, (2) relational sets-of-sets that capture simple concept interactions, and (3) full structural graphs capturing rich semantic structure. For the structural level, they support two modes: a precision-focused transductive mode using supervised GNNs, and a scalable inductive mode using unsupervised graph autoencoders to approximate graph similarity/counterfactuals without exact GED. They evaluate across two vision datasets (CUB and Visual Genome).

Result: On CUB and Visual Genome, they empirically quantify how explanation efficiency and expressivity vary across the three levels. At the structural level, both GNN- and GAE-based modes produce counterfactual explanations whose semantic content is judged by humans and LVLMs to be equivalent or superior in preference to explanations constructed from exact, GED-based ground truth counterfactuals, demonstrating that approximate, learned graph similarity is practically sufficient and even preferable.

Conclusion: U-CECE offers a practical, unified way to generate conceptual counterfactual explanations at multiple resolutions, allowing users to adapt explanations to their needs and computational budgets. It shows that learned structural approximations via GNNs/GAEs can circumvent the computational hardness of exact GED while maintaining or improving the semantic quality of counterfactual explanations, thus easing the expressivity-efficiency trade-off in concept-based explainability.

Abstract: As AI models grow more complex, explainability is essential for building trust, yet concept-based counterfactual methods still face a trade-off between expressivity and efficiency. Representing underlying concepts as atomic sets is fast but misses relational context, whereas full graph representations are more faithful but require solving the NP-hard Graph Edit Distance (GED) problem. We propose U-CECE, a unified, model-agnostic multi-resolution framework for conceptual counterfactual explanations that adapts to data regime and compute budget. U-CECE spans three levels of expressivity: atomic concepts for broad explanations, relational sets-of-sets for simple interactions, and structural graphs for full semantic structure. At the structural level, both a precision-oriented transductive mode based on supervised Graph Neural Networks (GNNs) and a scalable inductive mode based on unsupervised graph autoencoders (GAEs) are supported. Experiments on the structurally divergent CUB and Visual Genome datasets characterize the efficiency-expressivity trade-off across levels, while human surveys and LVLM-based evaluation show that the retrieved structural counterfactuals are semantically equivalent to, and often preferred over, exact GED-based ground-truth explanations.

*He Geng,Yangmin Huang,Lixian Lai,Qianyun Du,Hui Chu,Zhiyang He,Jiaxue Hu,Xiaodong Tao*

Method: 1) Build ProMedical-Preference-50k, a ~50k-example preference dataset created with a human-in-the-loop pipeline where physicians design rigorous rubrics for medical instructions and annotate model outputs based on multiple fine-grained criteria. 2) Introduce Explicit Criteria Injection: instead of a single scalar reward, they train a multi-dimensional reward model that separately scores aspects like safety and general proficiency. Result: Training Qwen3-8B with the ProMedical reward model and GRPO leads to large improvements: overall accuracy increases by 22.3% and safety compliance by 21.7% compared with the base model. The aligned model’s performance approaches that of proprietary frontier models and generalizes strongly to external benchmarks such as UltraMedical, where it performs on par with state-of-the-art systems. ProMedical-Bench confirms these gains under expert, double-blind evaluation, showing that the model is not just more accurate but also significantly safer and more protocol-compliant.

Conclusion: Fine-grained, rubric-based alignment via Explicit Criteria Injection is an effective way to align LLMs with high-stakes medical standards. By separating safety from general proficiency in the reward model and validating with expert, double-blind benchmarks, ProMedical yields substantial gains in both accuracy and safety and can rival strong proprietary models. The public release of datasets, reward models, and benchmarks is intended to catalyze further reproducible work on safety-aware alignment in medical AI.

Abstract: Aligning Large Language Models (LLMs) with high-stakes medical standards remains a significant challenge, primarily due to the dissonance between coarse-grained preference signals and the complex, multi-dimensional nature of clinical protocols. To bridge this gap, we introduce ProMedical, a unified alignment framework grounded in fine-grained clinical criteria. We first construct ProMedical-Preference-50k, a dataset generated via a human-in-the-loop pipeline that augments medical instructions with rigorous, physician-derived rubrics. Leveraging this corpus, we propose the Explicit Criteria Injection paradigm to train a multi-dimensional reward model. Unlike traditional scalar reward models, our approach explicitly disentangles safety constraints from general proficiency, enabling precise guidance during reinforcement learning. To rigorously validate this framework, we establish ProMedical-Bench, a held-out evaluation suite anchored by double-blind expert adjudication. Empirical evaluations demonstrate that optimizing the Qwen3-8B base model via ProMedical-RM-guided GRPO yields substantial gains, improving overall accuracy by 22.3% and safety compliance by 21.7%, effectively rivaling proprietary frontier models. Furthermore, the aligned policy generalizes robustly to external benchmarks, demonstrating performance comparable to state-of-the-art models on UltraMedical. We publicly release our datasets, reward models, and benchmarks to facilitate reproducible research in safety-aware medical alignment.

### [121] [Human-AI Collaboration Reconfigures Group Regulation from Socially Shared to Hybrid Co-Regulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08344)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: A parallel-group randomized experiment with 71 university students working on the same collaborative tasks, with or without access to GenAI. Only human discourse was analyzed. The authors statistically compared the distribution of collaborative regulation across (1) regulatory modes (co-regulation vs socially shared regulation), (2) regulatory processes (e.g., directive, obstacle-oriented, affective), and (3) participatory focuses (who or what is being regulated).

There were selective increases in directive, obstacle-focused, and affective regulatory processes. In contrast, the distribution of participatory focuses remained largely similar regardless of GenAI availability.

Conclusion: GenAI availability changes how regulatory responsibility is distributed in group work, shifting collaboration dynamics from collectively shared regulation toward more co-regulated patterns, and altering which regulatory processes are used. These insights inform the human-centered design of AI tools intended to support collaborative learning, highlighting the need to consider their impact on group regulation, not just on task performance.

Abstract: Generative AI (GenAI) is increasingly used in collaborative learning, yet its effects on how groups regulate collaboration remain unclear. Effective collaboration depends not only on what groups discuss, but on how they jointly manage goals, participation, strategy use, monitoring, and repair through co-regulation and socially shared regulation. We compared collaborative regulation between Human-AI and Human-Human groups in a parallel-group randomised experiment with 71 university students completing the same collaborative tasks with GenAI either available or unavailable. Focusing on human discourse, we used statistical analyses to examine differences in the distribution of collaborative regulation across regulatory modes, regulatory processes, and participatory focuses. Results showed that GenAI availability shifted regulation away from predominantly socially shared forms towards more hybrid co-regulatory forms, with selective increases in directive, obstacle-oriented, and affective regulatory processes. Participatory-focus distributions, however, were broadly similar across conditions. These findings suggest that GenAI reshapes the distribution of regulatory responsibility in collaboration and offer implications for the human-centred design of AI-supported collaborative learning.

  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors want a more general, adaptable transfer mechanism.

Method: They replace discrete latent task variables with natural language conditioning in a text-conditioned VAE. At test time, a Large Language Model serves as a semantic operator: given the current observation and its description, the agent queries the LLM to semantically remap this description so it aligns with the source (training) task. This source-aligned caption is fed into the VAE to generate an imagined state in the original task space, on which the trained policy can be directly applied.

Result: Using LLM-based semantic remapping and the text-conditioned VAE, the approach enables zero-shot transfer to a wide range of complex and genuinely novel but analogous tasks that go beyond what fixed discrete category mappings can handle. Empirical results (implied) show successful transfer across these varied tasks.

Conclusion: Natural language conditioning combined with LLM-based semantic remapping allows RL agents to reuse existing policies on structurally analogous but novel tasks without retraining or predefined discrete task classes. This demonstrates a more powerful and flexible form of zero-shot transfer, surpassing methods that depend on fixed category mappings.

Abstract: Reinforcement Learning (RL) agents often struggle to generalize knowledge to new tasks, even those structurally similar to ones they have mastered. Although recent approaches have attempted to mitigate this issue via zero-shot transfer, they are often constrained by predefined, discrete class systems, limiting their adaptability to novel or compositional task variations. We propose a significantly more generalized approach, replacing discrete latent variables with natural language conditioning via a text-conditioned Variational Autoencoder (VAE). Our core innovation utilizes a Large Language Model (LLM) as a dynamic \textit{semantic operator} at test time. Rather than relying on rigid rules, our agent queries the LLM to semantically remap the description of the current observation to align with the source task. This source-aligned caption conditions the VAE to generate an imagined state compatible with the agent's original training, enabling direct policy reuse. By harnessing the flexible reasoning capabilities of LLMs, our approach achieves zero-shot transfer across a broad spectrum of complex and truly novel analogous tasks, moving beyond the limitations of fixed category mappings. While interactions from different users provide complementary signals about when a skill works or fails, existing systems lack a mechanism to convert such heterogeneous experiences into reliable skill updates. To address these issues, we present SkillClaw, a framework for collective skill evolution in multi-user agent ecosystems, which treats cross-user and over-time interactions as the primary signal for improving skills. SkillClaw continuously aggregates trajectories generated during use and processes them with an autonomous evolver, which identifies recurring behavioral patterns and translates them into updates to the skill set by refining existing skills or extending them with new capabilities. The resulting skills are maintained in a shared repository and synchronized across users, allowing improvements discovered in one context to propagate system-wide while requiring no additional effort from users.
